OF ADAPTIVE VARIATION IN CULTURE

Marshall Di Sahlins

Submitted in partial fulfillment of the requirements
for the degree of Doctor of Philosophy, in the
Faculty of Political Science
Columbia University

SOCIAL STRATIFICATION IN POLYNESIA: A STUDY
0^ ADAPTIVE VARIATION IN CULTURE

by
Marshall D. Sahlins

ABSTRACT

This is a study of adaptive variation in culture.
An attempt is made to understand certain differences in the

stratification systems of abori inal Polynesian cultures

in lirht of the fundamental problem which all cultures face,
exploitin'- the environment in order to materially sustain

life.

Polynesian cultures, due to their historical unity,

may be considered as members of a sin le cultural species

adaptin'" to vary in-

habitats.

It is held that the techno-

lorical articulations of the cultures to their environments

are basic adaptations and that the systems of social organi­
zation and social stratification vary as the technoenvironmental conditions vary.

No teleology or theory of

unilateral necessary and sufficient causation is intended by
this methodology, only that the aspects of culture mentioned
are co-variables.

The dissertation is divided into two narts.

In Part

One, fourteen Polynesian cultures are rated according to the

amount of stratification they contain.

Amount of stratifi­

cation is understood as the degree to which there exist in­
herited status differences and the degree to which these

2

status differences confer differential roles and privileges
in economic, socio-political and ceremonial life.

It is

found that the amount of stratification increases in Poly­

nesia as the productivity of techno-environmental interaction

increases.

Productivity is taken to mean the ability of the

producers to produce a surplus of food beyond their immedi­

ate or annual consumption needs.

The relationship between

stratification and productivity is intelligible because of
the redistributive economic system wh‘ch prevails in Poly­

nesia.

In this system surplus production is periodically

accumulated by chiefs and in large part redistributed among

producers of the surplus, among craft specialists and among

laborers on communal or "public" enterprises.

As productiv­

ity increases, the differential between the chiefly regulators
of distribution and the non-chiefly producers increases—not
only in distribution, but in allied economic, social and

religious affairs.
In Part Two, the formal characteristics of social

organisation and stratification in seventeen Polynesian cul­

tures are examined.

Two types of organization may be dis­

cerned on the volcanic Islands : the ramage organization an in­

ternally stratified system in which status is assigned on the

basis of the position of families in large genealogical net­
works; and the descent line organization, wherein discrete

kin units hold status titles based on a traditional, mytholog­
ical order of precedence.

The two systems correspond to dif­

fering geographical distributions of highly productive resources:

3

the rampe organization is found on islands with scattered
resource zones, the descent line on islands with a concentra­

tion of resource zones.

On atolls, systems of social organi­

zation show a tendency toward the formation of complex cross­

cutting groups based on varying principles of descent or
association.

This tendency is related to scarcity conditions

on atolls which prohibit productive specialization yet which
encourage the formation of separate organizations for each
type of production.
The broad generalizations that emerge from the dis­

sertation include (other factors constant): that stratifica­
tion increases as productivity increases; that the form of

stratification in areas of high productivity is related to

the spatial distribution of resources; and that long occupa­
tion of a stable environment by a culture of low productivity

tends to produce a multiplicity of cross-cutting social group­
ings.

INTRODUCTION

This is a study of adaptive variation in culture.
We seek to understand differences in a particular aspect of

the aboriginal cultures of Polynesia, social stratification,

in light of particular cultural adaptations to the various

environmental conditions present in the islands.

As the

technological system is the means by which a culture articu­
lates with the natural environment, our problem becomes one
of relating social stratification differences to differences

in the techno-environmental situation.

If co-variable rela­

tionships of this nature can be discovered, a deep understand­
ing of cultural variation is obtained.

For in discovering

these relationships we relate cultural differences to the

basic problem which cultures face—coping with the natural

world, and deriving from it the energy necessary for continued
existence.

General Considerations
Relating differences in the sociological (or social

organizational) realm of culture to the techniques of ex­
ploiting the external world and to conditions of that exter­

nal world is not a new concept in anthropology.
back to the last century and beyond.

It dates

There have been two

major schools of thought, the ’technologists’ and the

• environmentalists. •

Among others, Lewis Henry Morgan,

E. B. Tylor, Karl Marx and Friedrich Engels in the last cen­

tury, and Hobhouse, Wheeler and Ginsberg, V. Gordon Childe

and Leslie A. White in this century have held that social
systems vary as technological systems vary.^

The most ex­

plicit statement of this hypothesis comes from White, who
writes :

The roles played by the several sub-systems in
the culture process as a whole are not equal by
any means. The primary role is played by the
technological system.... Social systems are in a
very real sense secondary and subsidiary to tech­
nological systems.... A social system is a func­
tion of a technological system.2
White makes an important addition to this statement, an addi­
tion which was neglected by most other technologists, namely,

L. H. Morgan, Ancient Society, (Kerr edition) nd
(First published, 1877); E. B. Tylor, Anthropology, 1898;
Karl Marx, A Contribution to the Critique of Political
Economy (Translated'from the second German edition by W. I.
Stone. Second edition published, 1897; first edition, 1859)
Cf. Preface and Introduction; F. Engels, The Origin of the
Family, Private Property and the State, 1942 (International
Publishers edition; first published, 1892); V. G. Childe,
What Happened in History, 1948a (Pelican edition; first pub­
lished, 1942); V. G. Childe, Man Makes Himself, 1948b
(Thinker^ Library edition; first published, 1937); Hobhouse,
Wheeler and Ginsberg, The Material Culture and Social Insti­
tutions of the Simpler Peoples, 1930.
As used in this paper, technological system is de­
fined, after White, as "composed of the material, mechanical,
physical, and chemical instruments, together with their use,
by means of which man, as an animal species, is articulated
with his natrual habitat." (1949, 364). By ’environment’
is meant such features and conditions of the world external
to man as temperature, rainfall, topography, natural flora
and fauna, mineral resources, water sources and the like.
2

White, op. dt., 365»

The functioning of any particular culture will
of course be conditioned by local environmental
conditions. But in a consideration of culture
as a whole, we may average all environments to­
gether to form a constant factor which may be ex­
cluded from our formula of cultural development.3
Thus social systems vary as technological systems vary—

environment held constant.
Parallel to the emphasis on technology as the key
to differences in social organization runs a hopythesis
which underscores the environmental ’determinants’ of cul­
tural differences in general, and social institutions in par­

ticular.

Outstanding among the environmentalists are

F. Ratzel and Ellsworth Huntington.^

The theory, briefly,

is that social organization and other aspects of cultural

systems vary with environmental conditions

Growing out of an environmental emphasis was the
culture area approach, developed largely by Clark Wissler

and A. L. Kroeber, which is in many respects an extension and

is not made explicit. Furthermore, Huntington chose to

racial temperament. We do not share Huntington’s theories
of racial temperament. There is widespread agreement today
that racial temperaments may be disregarded for the purpose
of understanding cultural variation.

refinement of environmentalism.

It was ‘impure’ environ­

mentalism, however; sometimes the emphasis shifted to tech­

nology, as when lissier derived the North American culture
areas from six "food" areas.
These two streams have met in the modern so-called

‘ecological’ concept, which, dealing with particular cul­
tures, has concentrated on variations in the technological

exploitations of specific environments as the clue to the
understanding of whole cultural, including sociological, dif­

ferences .

It should be noted that no essential contradic­

tion appears between the ’ecologistic’ concept and the others

mentioned.

White, for example, recognizes the importance

of environment when dealing with particular cultures (see

above)--but White is not dealing with particular cultures.
The cultural ecological method has been utilized in a number
of papers by Julian Steward and recently by Morton Fried.?

Karl Wittfogel also has used the ecological concept without

so designating it.

In actuality, Steward is often quite

vague as to the phenomena which are embraced by the term,

’ecology.’

Beside technology and environment, he often adds

social elements, purportedly the domain of culture which he

is explaining.

The clearest definition of ’ecology’ as used

in cultural studies comes from Fried:

"Ecology, which might

be called the action aspect of geography, combines indigenous
resources with a specific cultural inventory of exploitative
9
techniques..

It is this dual concept of the basic phenomena to

which social differences are relatable, woven into an evolu­
tionary approach, that is used here.

Polynesian cultures

can be considered as a single cultural species adapting it­

self to varying local habitats.

Social stratification is

taken to be intimately related to the particular adaptations
effected through the technological articulation with these

habitats.

As the environments or the technologies vary, the

social stratification will vary, not only in form but also in

amount or degree.

It is hoped that broader conclusions and

developmental generalizations may be derived from considera­

tion of such adaptive variations.
Mention should be made here of the type of determin­

ism that is involved in the concept of adaptive variation
and in the methodology of this work.

The basic aim of a study

g
K.

Wittfogel, Wirtschaft und Gesellschaft Chinas,

1931.
9

Fried, op. cit., 391.

b

of adaptive variation In culture is one of defining and

illustrating the functional relationships between the tech­
nological articulation of culture to environment and other

aspects of cultural systems.

In this the aim is one common

to science in all its manifestations, that of determining

variable relationships between different sets of phenomena.
The concept holds that the method and results of technologi­

cal exploitation of environment are basic cultural phenomena

upon which is reared a structure or pattern of social organi­
zation and ideology.

Thus with reference to the problem at

hand, it is postulated that given a certain constellation or
system of technology interacting with an environment, the

amount and form of social stratification is related to it

such that (other things constant), given a different system
of technological interaction, the amount and form of social

stratification would be different.

Here it should be specifically and emphatically re­
marked that no teleology and no postulate of unidirectional

necessary and sufficient causation is intended.

By this is

meant, first, no progressive change in the technological
aspect of culture, such as increasing productivity, is

assumed to be an inherent cultural phenomenon--ever changing
onward and upward, and carrying along the rest of culture
step by step.

As a corollary, it is not assumed that social

organization and stratification are everywhere determined by
technology in the sense that a change in social organization

must be preceded by a change in technology, or that technology

7

cannot be affected by changes in social organization and

ideology.

Nothing is necessarily implied in this work about

tne ’dynamics’ of the origin of a particular system of social

stratification or of technology.

The question of the origin

of variations in social stratification (or, for that matter,

of any culture trait or pattern) is a distinct problem from
that of the adaptive nature of social stratification—just

as in biology the study of genetic mutation is distinct from

the study of natural selection.

The essential methodological technique in the study
of cultural adaptation is that of correlation.

If concomi­

tant variations can be demonstrated between some phases of
technological articulation with environment and social strati­

fication, then the probability is high that the two are functjonally related.

However, the problem is not one of corre­

lation pure and simple.

If variations in social stratifica­

tion are to be considered as adaptive variations, some

structural relationship must be established, both deductively

and empirically, between social stratification and the cor­
related phases of technological exploitation of environment—
otherwise the correlation may be spurious.

The necessary

intervening steps between the technological base and the
stratification superstructure must be determined.

Such in­

tervening steps would take the form of cultural features and
mechanisms which need be present if a given correlation be­

tween stratification and technology is to obtain.

In this

work, certain features of the economic system, particularly

a

the method of distributing food and other goods, are found

to provide the link between technology and social stratifi­
cation.

It should be noted that the concept of adaptive
variation is not one that ignores historical processes of
diffusion, nor sides with 'internal development’ theories,
as opposed somehow to diffusionist theories.

This controversy

has raged in Polynesian ethnology as well as elsewhere.^0

Piddington has, in fact, outlined a number of factors, beside

diffusion, by which Polynesian chieftainship might be under­
stood: geographical or demographic, the influence of out­
standing personalities and institutional efflorescence.^
10

See Firth’s Introduction to R. Williamson, Religion
and Social Organization in Central Polynesia, 1937; and
Piddington in R. Williamson, Essays in"Polynesian EthnoIogy
(R. Piddington, ed.), 1939.
^Piddington, Essays in Polynesian Ethnology, 206­
256. As compared with the approach utilized here, the fol­
lowing comments on Piddington's factors which influence
change in stratification are relevant. The geographical or
demographic factor is, of course, the basic feature of adap­
tation. In studies of adaptive variation, the influence of
outstanding personalities can be considered a constant as it
can be assumed in any given Polynesian group the range of
personality capabilites is very similar if not identical;
the culture is the variable involved. Differences cannot be
attributed to constant factors. This is not to say that the
study of outstanding personalities is not relevant to a com­
plete understanding of the origin of variation. However, as
already indicated, the problem of the origin of variation in
culture is distinct from the problem of the action of cul­
tural selection or adaptation. As to 'institutional efflor­
escence, ' to use it as an explanatory concept is tautological
inasmuch as it is only a label for a unique constellation of
cultural traits in an area of cultural similarity. Pidding­
ton, himself, recognizes this when he writes that institu­
tional efflorescence is the resultant of the operation of
other variable factors.

9

From the point of view of cultural adaptation the
argument is spurious♦

Any given culture is a product of its

traditions, including diffusionary influences, and its inter­
relationship with its natural habitat.

One cannot have a

complete understanding of the differences between two cul­
tures without a knowledge of their previous forms — any more

than one can understand the differences between primates and
earthworms solely from the fact that primates took to the
trees whereas earthworms took to the earth.

As cultural

adaptation is the process of the interaction of cultural

forms with local habitats, the variations resulting in the
process of adaptation are dependent on the previous form and
outside influences upon it, as well as the actual adaptation.

With this in mind, the choice of Polynesian cul­

tures as the materials to be used in a study of cultural

adaptation is an advantageous one, for Polynesian cultures
as a whole have a great part of their history in common.
Nevertheless, at every turn, cognizance should be taken that

diffusion or some other historical process (perhaps non­
adap tive) may have been operating differentially on this or
that feature of social stratification in particular cultures.
For in that case variation might not be related to differen­
tial selection in present habitats, but due to the fact that

the forms being compared were different to begin with.

10

Hypotheses and Procedure
Social stratification may be divided into two aspects,
the amount of stratification and the form of the stratifica­

tion system.

'Amount of social stratification' refers to the
12
complexity of the status system,
i.e., the number of differ­

ent kinds of statuses, and the degree to which differential
status confers differential roles and privileges in economic,

socio-political and religious life.

'Form of stratification'

refers to the social organization of the stratification sys­

tem, that is, the sociological background in which statuses
operated.

The dissertation is divided into two parts, fol­

lowing this division of the subject matter.
In Part I, variations in the amount of stratifica­
tion in a number of islands will be considered.

The concept,

'amount of stratification,' when referring to groups organ­

ized on a kinship basis has an important implication for
broad developmental schemes of culture.

It is now common

knowledge that what are generally called 'the most primitive

societies' or societies on the 'lowest level’ of culture are

relatively unstratified.

Perhaps the most explicit exposi­

tion of this fact was made in a paper by Professor P. Kirchhoff,

unfortunately unpublished, called, "The Principles of Clan­
ship in Human Society."

Kirchhoff,writing in 1935, distin­

guished two types of organization which are usually designated
—

"

Hereinafter the words "status" and "rank" are used
interchangeably , as it has been found that making a distinc­
tion between them does not clarify any of our problems.

11

by the same word, "clan.”

The first type is the unilateral

exogamous clan, the outstanding characteristic of which is,

"every member of the clan is, as far as clan membership goes,

on an absolutely equal footing with the rest: the nearness

of relation to each other or to some ancestor being of no

consequence for a person's place in the clan."1^

From

this type of clan, called "equalltarian,” Kirchhoff dis­

tinguished the "conical clan," a unilateral, non-exogamous

grouping, which differed (aside from the marriage rules) in

that, "it is precisely the nearness of the relationship to
the common ancestor of the group which matters."^ As dis­

tinguished from the first type of clan: "The second principle
results in a group in which every single member, except
brothers or sisters, has a different standing: the concept

of the degree of relationship leads to different degrees of
membership in the clan.

Kirchhoff did not believe the two clans formed an
evolutionary sequence of stages, even though he correlated
the first type with a "lower" form of economy, and the second

with a "higher."

On the contrary, he believed they were both

divergent offshoots from some common ancestor.
need not concern us in its present form.

This problem

However, an even

broader distinction arises from Kirchhoff's dichotomy, which,

12

we submit, does have bearing on ’universal’ evolutionary
theory.

That is, kinship societies can be distinguished from

the point of view that in some there is little to no differ­
entiation of status, while in others there are marked status

distinctions between individuals and groups.

Kin societies

can be classified on this basis irrespective of the type of
clan. Landtman, for example, has treated innumerable socie­
ties in this way.16 Furthermore, we postulate that an evolu­

tionary sequence, at least a sequence of adaptive variations,

can be made which shows a gradient of more egalitarian to
more stratified, and which is correlated with proportional

changes in the techno-environmental sphere.
In Part One of the dissertation, a number of Poly­
nesian cultures are classified according to the amount of

stratification they show.

The cultures are then examined in

terms of the relationship between the amount of social strati­
fication and features of technological exploitation of environ­

ment, particularly productivity.

It will be shown that, other

factors being constant, the amount of stratification varies
as the productivity varies.

It is submitted that this concom­

itant variation is related to differentiation of statuses in
the process of the distribution of goods.

The greater the

productivity the greater would be the economic, social and
ceremonial distinction between distributors and producers.

G. Landtman, The Origin of the Inequality of the
Social Classes, 1938.

13

In Part Two specific social organizations from which
the forms of social stratification take their character are
treated from the viewpoint of adaptive variation.

Three

specific types of social stratification and social organiza—
tion may be distinguished in Polynesia : ramage, descent line

and atoll.

Each of these will be described and interpreted

in some detail in their particular manifestations in a number
of islands.

It is demonstrated that in the main each type is

adapted to a particular techno-environmental situation.

Ramage organization is found to be related to conditions of

widely spread zones of highly productive resources.

Descent

systems are found to be related to spatial concentration

of highly productive resource zones.

Atoll organizations are

found to be related to conditions of extremely low produe —
tivity and limited range of exploitative techniques.
It will be noticed that a number of aspects of social

stratification are not treated here.

In particular, the posi­

tion of women in the stratification system and the related
problem of the vasu relationships of western Polynesia are

omitted.

As in no island was there matriarchy in any form,

whether women ranked higher or lower than men is by and large
ignored.

Otherwise the basic problems would be obscured.

However, in some islands the descent system was flexible; re­
lationships could be traced through women instead of following

strict patrilineal descent.

As this is related to features

of social stratification we choose to consider basic, it is

considered here.

14

Sources

The dissertation is concerned with the Polynesian

cultures in the ethnological present, that is at the time
just prior to European influence.

Any trait described in

the ethnographic sources that can be attributed to European

Polynesia includes those islands

influence is excluded,

usually considered as having a Polynesian culture by anthro­
pologists .

By that is meant all the islands within the tri­

angle which may be formed between Hawaii, New Zealand and
Easter Island, excluding Fiji, but including those ’outliers’
lying outside the triangle, particularly Tikopla and Ontong

Java in the Solomon Islands,

Our major consideration in selecting cultures to be

compared was the availability of ethnographical studies done
by competent, trained observers.

Besides this, certain

islands for which there is no complete ethnographic source,
but which are traditionally considered important, Hawaii and

the Society Islands, were included.

A second consideration

was whether or not the materials included in the monographs
were adequate for our purposes.

Thus the islands of Niue and

Tubuai, on which there are ethnographic monographs, were ex­
cluded because of the paucity of information on economic and
social organization,

The reason for this is

Maori are also excluded from Part One.

not because of a paucity of information on the Maori, but
rather that the most comprehensive reports treat Maori culture

in generalized or 1 typical1 terms, often falling to make clear

the regional variation which existed.

Corresponding to local

conditions, Maori "tribes" varied in their technological sys­
tems from pure hunting and gathering to rather intensive horl-

culture.

In a study such as this (i.e., in Part One) where

the productivity differences connoted by these diversities of

techniques may be important, it would be necessary to have very
specific information on specific tribes.

The sources relied upon are, as indicated, primarily

ethnographical.

With the exception of the classic missionary

accounts, such as those of Gill, Ellis, Orsmund (Henry) and a

few other old accounts of the same category, the historical
sources were not heavily used.

tageous--in view of the time that would be expended and the

dubious nature of many of the reports.

More important is the

fact that the information from the historical sources is ex­

Finally, the monographs of the Bishop Museum on

the particu­

PART

ONE

THE AMOUNT OF STRATIFICATION

17

CHAPTER I
SOCIAL STRATIFICATION IN KINSHIP SOCIETIES

Kirchhoff’s paper, "The Principles of Clanship In
Human Society," makes a distinction of great heuristic value

to the study of primitive kinship society.

Kirchhoff pointed

out that in certain types of kinship societies there are

marked status distinctions, while in other types everyone is,
in theory at least, equal.

The distinction is all the more

illuminating, we submit, because it does not actually seem to
rest on the types of "clan" per se, as Kirchhoff conceived it,

but it goes beyond such formal categories as "clan."

Clans

do not exist everywhere, yet probably all kinship societies

can fit into some such distinction as "stratified" or "egali­
tarian" ("conical" and "equalitarian" in Kirchhoff’s termi­
nology).

Kirchhoff, as already pointed out, did not believe

that egalitarian clans gave rise to stratified clans, but

rather that the two represent divergent evolutionary courses,
perhaps from some common ancestor.

We have postulated, how­

ever, that a sequence or gradient of greater and greater

stratification can be made in kinship societies, and can be
seen to be related to certain measurable increases in tech­

nological-environmental conditions.

In that sense a sequence

of adaptive varieties can be developed and perhaps some evo­

lutionary conclusion be drawn.

In an attempt to demonstrate

18

that hypothesis we have chosen Polynesian societies as our
example.

To verify our hypothesis regarding the increase in

stratification is the problem in Part I of this work.

The

task that lies ahead is to outline, in sequence of increasing

amounts, the varieties of stratification in Polynesia, and to
relate these to the interaction of the respective technologies
with local environmental conditions.

In this chapter social

stratification in societies organized on a kinship basis will
be discussed with a view toward obtaining criteria by which
Polynesian societies can be ranked according to the amount of

stratification they show.

What is Social Stratification?
The first questions which confront us are, what is

egalitarianism and what is stratification?

Theoretically an

egalitarian society would be one in which every individual is

of absolutely equal status--no one outranks anyone.

That such

conditions might be observed in human societies is as impos­

sible as observing free falling bodies in vacua.

no such societies exist.

In actuality

There are differences in status

carrying differential privileges in every human organization.
But societies can be distinguished on the basis of the
socio-culturally defined qualifications for whatever status

differences may be present.

In certain societies, the Austra­

lian aboriginal communities would be examples, the basis of
qualification for status rests solely on principles which

every society uses to some extent, namely, age, sex and personal

19

attributes.

Aside from these qualifications, in the socie­

ties we speak of,1 there are none.

On two grounds a society

in which the only qualifications for status are age, sex and

personal attributes, can be called egalitarian.

First, be­

cause these qualifications are present in some degree in all
human societies, and can thus be considered a constant fea­

ture of human organization.

And secondly, because given these

qualifications for status, or holding them constant, every

individual has an equal chance to succeed to whatever statuses
may be open.

But a society unlike this, one that has sta­

tuses fixed by inheritance, or some mechanism or qualifica­

tion other than age, sex or personal attributes, can be called
stratified.

A further distinction might be made.

Societies can

be more or less stratified or more or less egalitarian.
eties can vary in amount of stratification.

Soci­

It is suggested

that one society may be considered more stratified than
another if it contain more statuses not open to anyone, which

statuses exercise greater differential privileges and roles in

10ther examples of this type would be pygmy groups
from the Philippines to the African Congo, the Sakai of
Malaya, the Great Basin Indians of North America, the Ona,
Yaghan and other South American •marginals,1 and the Bushman
of South Africa. With minor qualifications, slightly more
advanced groups, even lower horticulturalists and pastoral­
ists could also be included, as the southern California and
northeastern North American Indians, the Hottentots, many New
Guinea peoples, etc.
See also Landtman (1938, Chap. I) who discusses a
number of organizations.

20

economic activities, interpersonal affairs and religious

life.

On this basis we have drawn up a number of criteria
by which the amount of stratification in a kinship society

may be estimated.

These criteria may be divided into two

broad categories, structural and functional.

The structural

criterion of the amount of stratification is the degree to
which there exist differences in status or groups of statuses

which carry different privileges, but qualification for which
does not rest upon the universal qualifications of age, sex

and personal attributes—In short, the complexity of the
status system.

It is particularly with groups of statuses

that we will be concerned as these provide simple descriptive
generalizations.

classes.”

Groups of statuses might be called "social

However, in contrast to the social classes of in­

dustrial and other highly organized, state economies, the
groups of statuses in stratified kinship societies do not as

a rule imply great differences in accumulated wealth.

To

maintain a distinction therefore, in what are really two dif­
ferent types of phenomena, we shall hereafter refer to the

"social classes" in non-state societies and economies as,

"status levels."

Landtman’s definition of "classes” corres­

ponds closely to the concept of status levels:

By classes ... I understand more or less homo­
geneous divisions of the population, separated
from each other in respect to precedence and
privileges. As a rule these different groups re­
present different employments, whether it be that
special avocations are reserved for certain
classes, or that there is merely a general divi­
sion of work between them. To each class is

21

usually assigned a certain rank. When the dif­
ference of class is complete, this rank is here­
ditary, at least to a certain extent. Inequality
in standing and influence may exist without these
advantages being necessarily hereditary, but in
such cases the difference depends upon individual
conditions and does not create classes.2

By contrast, modern sociological definitions of class are
usually inapplicable to kinship societies, as such defini­

tions include such features as class antagonism, wealth accu­

mulation and occupational similarities—features not found

in primitive stratified societies.
The second or functional criterion of the amount of
stratification refers to the degree to which differences in
status confer differential privileges and roles in cultural

life.

The functional criteria can be divided into three

categories referring to different aspects of culture: economic,

socio-political or social regulatory and religious.

And these

categories may be further subdivided into a number of aspects.
Under the category of economic life, the degree to which non­

egalitarian status levels confer differential access to re­
sources, and differential roles and privileges in the distri­
bution, production and consumption of goods should be noted.

In the social regulatory sphere, different roles played by
the different status levels in the regulation of interpersonal
affairs such as expression of authority in the application of

sanction to wrongdoers ("justice"), in decisions as to peace
-

Landtman, op. cit., 36.
3Cf. Class, Status and Power, A Reader in Social
Stratification (R. Bendix and S.M. Lipset. eds.l. 1953.

and war, and the like must be considered.

Finally, in reli­

gious or socio-ceremonial activities differential status is
apt to be manifest in differential access to the superna­

tural, and in different ceremonial behaviors culturally ap­

propriate to given statuses.
These then are the criteria, structural and func­

tional, which we propose to use to estimate the amount of
social stratification in Polynesian societies.

It would be,

of course, preferable to have some mathematical index of
stratification based perhaps on behavioral measurements
But to apply such methods to written accounts of behavior

over which we have no control as to what is selected to be
observed is, of course, out of the question.

Our criteria of the amount of stratification are
interrelated and it is from this interrelationship that the
major hypothesis treated in Part I is constructed.

The dif­

ferential roles of different statuses in the distribution of
goods is perhaps the best point from which to begin to under­
stand the relationship of the various aspects of stratifica­

tion.

If there is a separation between statuses on the basis

of producers and regulators of the distribution of the goods
produced, certain consequences follow regarding the separa­
tions of such statuses in many other cultural activities.

As, for example, the number of certain types of
interactions, proposed by Chapple and Arensberg (E. Chapple
and C. Arensberg, "Measuring Human Relations," Genetic Psych
Monographs, 22, 3-1^7, 194-0).
—

23

In the first place there would be a tendency for the regu­

lator of distribution to exert some authority over produc­
tion itself--especially over productive activities which
necessitate an accompanying distribution, such as communal

labor or specialized labor.

Furthermore, control of distri­

bution and production implies some sort of control over, or

prior right of access to, productive resources and the tools
of production.

Again, control over the regulation of these

economic processes necessitates the exercise of authority in
interpersonal affairs, and differences in political or socialregulatory roles emerge.

Finally, all of these differential

activities would probably find validation in ideology and

ceremony.

Distinctions in belief as to the origin, sacredness

and like phenomena, regarding the different statuses, may
arise.

The relationship between the distributive role of
chiefs, or people of high status, and the exercise of author­

ity in other spheres has been pointed out many times in the

literature on Polynesia and elsewhere.

R. Thurnwald sees the

distributive function of the chief as the genesis of central­

ized political authority.^

Malinowski wrote:

I think that throughout the world we would
find that the relations between economics and
politics are of the same type. The chief, every­
where, acts as a tribal bai ker, collecting food,
storing it, and protecting it, and then using it
for the benefit of the whole community. His

^R. Thurnwald, Economics in Primitive Communities. 1932.
180-181.
---------------------------------------------------------- —'
* *

24

functions are the prototype of the public finance
system and the organizations of State treasuries
of to day. &

The co-variable aspect between the features of social
stratification outlined is clearly brought out in a number of
descriptions of Polynesian cultures.?

In all Polynesia econo­

mies different roles emerged in the system of distribution.

Surplus production, i.e., food and other material goods not

used by the producers, was constantly or periodically (de­
pending on the cycle of production) accumulated by certain

members of the community and then redistributed among the pro­
ducers.

A distinction is thus made between him who only pro­

duces, and the regulator of distribution on a large scale.
And this distinction, in Polynesia, is precisely the distinc­

tion between non-chief and chief, or between the group of non­
chiefs and the group of chiefs.

The chiefs acted as focal

points in a large system of collection and redistribution of

goods.

Professor Polanyi has labelled this type of distri­

bution, "the redistributive form of economic integration.*®
We propose to adopt that terminology, or for short, "the re­
distributive system."

A large proportion of distributions of

were ’redistributive’ in character.

From their redistribu­

tive activities, chiefs gained in prestige; others got the

necessities of life; all took a ’profit.’

And related to the

distributive role of the chiefs, as we shall see, were other
roles and privileges in other cultural activities, including

social and ceremonial life.
Given these co-variable aspects of stratification,

the hypothesis relating differences in the amount of strati­

fication with concomitant variations in technologicalenvironmental conditions becomes more meaningful.

As the

different features of stratification correspond to the dif­

ferentiation of roles in distribution, the amount of strati­
fication may be seen to be co-adaptive to the efficiency of

the technological exploitation of environment, or produc9
tivity.
Other factors being constant, the greater the in­

crease in productivity, the greater the scope and complexity
of the distribution (as, for example, to different kinds of

specialists); hence, the greater the separation between dis­
tributors and producers in many other aspects of culture.

It

should be noted that other factors are rarely constant in cul­
ture.

Although this may not affect the validity of the gen­

eral statement, it may affect its applicability in specific

given economically autonomous group. The term and concept
will be examined further in Chapter VI.

instances.

Comparisons within a group of historically re­

lated cultures, such as Polynesia, are therefore, apt to
be more significant (statistically) than cross-cultural

comparisons.
It may be remarked that the hypothesis presented

here is very closely related to one outlined by Melville
J. Herskovits.10 Herskovits points out that in societies
with little stratification there is little "surplus,” while

in more stratified societies there is a greater "surplus.”

For example :

"The absence of economic surplus is strikingly

correlated with an absence of economically privileged sta­

tus among the Australian tribes.Polynesian societies,

according to Herskovits, are generally more stratified and

have larger surpluses.

Herskovits is not always precise

about the relationship of these phenomena to the system of

distribution, but he points out that, "the problems of the
degree to which ... surpluses are found in various societies

and their manner of disposal among the members of given

groups come to the fore as among the most important elements—

and the most neglected--in the study of the economics of

Applying the Criteria of Stratification to
Polynesia Cultures
As the ethnographies of Polynesian cultures do not

treat social stratification according to the criteria out­
lined in this chapter, it is necessary to make a detailed ex­
planation of how the data are to be evaluated in the following
chapters in order to rank the cultures on a scale of amount

of stratification.
For each culture, the following aspects of social

stratification are considered: the number of status levels,

differential access rights of given statuses to strategic^
resources, differential roles in production distribution and
consumption, different roles in the regulation of interper­

sonal affairs including the application of sanctions to wrong­

doers, and differential ceremonial prerogatives.
In the cultures under consideration, two to three

groupings of social standing or status levels, based on in­
heritance, can be demarcated.

In delimiting status levels,

10

Ibid., 355-356.
Unfortunately, Herskovits’ defi­
nition of surplus is vague : "excess of goods over the minimum
demands of necessity." Sometimes it is the "necessity" of
the producer which is referred to, as in the above textual
quotation. This corresponds to the definition used here
(see Chap. VI). Other times it is the necessity of the soci­
ety as a whole (cf. op. clt.» 373)* The latter type of sur­
plus may be called "waste"
Waste rarely occurs in Polynesia;
there are no wholesale destructions of goods. * Surplus' is
not used here in the sense of ’waste.1
13
Hereinafter ’strategic’ is used with reference to
things useful in materially sustaining life.

reliance is not necessarily placed upon the ethnographer’s

distinctions of social grade (nor the natives’); rather, the
attempt is made to place all those on a comparable level who

exercise comparable privileges and play comparable roles in
social, economic and ceremonial life.

For example, while an

ethnographer might place the paramount chief in a class by

himself, it is unnecessary from our point of view, to struc­
turally segregate such a chief from others with reference to

whom he is merely primus inter pares.

In most of the soci­

eties considered, a scheme of social standing, based on order

of birth in a given line of descent, pervades the entire

group so that in actuality everyone in the society is of dif­
ferent rank from everyone else.

The gradation between one

status level and the next is so fine that it is difficult
(for both native and ethnographer) to ascertain where one type

of status leaves off and another begins (see Chapter VIII).
Consequently the concept of status level is most useful as a

descriptive generalization when referring to extremes or to
averages of social standing in Polynesia.

As to evaluation,

a society with a greater number of status levels than another
can be considered more stratified inasmuch as, by definition,
a more complex system of differential roles and privileges

must obtain.

Of course, where an egalitarian status group,

such as the elders of the community, is of greater impor­

tance than any hereditary status group, a strong element of
egalitarianism may be said to prevail in the society.

The rights of different statuses over the means of

29

production, especially as manifested in land and water

tenure, must be considered.

The common form of tenure, in

Polynesia may be termed ’managership,» or better, ’steward­
ship .»

In this pattern, every member of a group holding a

particular plot of land, or area of water, has a customary

right of access to the resource, but the head of the group
has the right to administer use of the resource for the bene­
fit of all the members »

In a few Polynesian societies each

small kin group (no larger than a few households) holds land
in this pattern, and the kindred head has the highest pre —

negatives in the entire society regarding the management of
the group’s plot.

In many Polynesian societies, however, the

right of the head of the kin group using a plot is subordi­
nated to overriding claims of managership by persons of chiefly

status.

Thus members of high status levels may be stewards

over a number of plots each having its own manager.

Such a

system may be designated ’overlapping stewardship,’ and due to

the differentiation by status involved, may be considered a

more stratified pattern than simple stewardship.

One of the

rights of stewardship is that of placing a tabu or prohibi­

tion on the use of a resource area or product of an area.
Such tabus are usually for the purpose of conserving the biota

of the area against times of famine or events requiring the
collection of large amounts of goods.

The rights of differ­

ent statuses in placing economic tabus and the manner of en­

forcing them should be noted*

A supernatural sanction usually

accompanies a tabu, but breach of the prohibition in some

3o

societies may bring secular punishment by members of chiefly
status.

Cognizance should also be taken of rights of dis­

possession, especially of dispossession of lower ranking

stewards by higher.

The existence of lands held in an undif­

ferentiated or communal manner with access free to all members

of the society may be considered an egalitarian trait.
Some differences in supervision of production are im­

plied by the stewardship pattern of tenure, especially by the
tabu-placing powers of stewards.

Supervision of production,

however, sometimes takes more pronounced and direct forms in

Polynesia.

The planting arrangements of individual households

may be supervised by members of high status levels; inspections

might be made and secular punishments imposed on those who

neglect their fields.

As opposed to supervision by household

heads, such arrangements may be considered more stratified
practices.

Furthermore, members of upper status levels may

also control communal and craft production, as only they
(through their roles in redistribution, see below) may be able

to supply the food necessary to support such enterprises.

The

extent to which higher status confers the right to regulate
communal and craft production is an indication of stratifica­
tion.

Another type of differential which arises with re­
spect to production is the segregation of chiefs from sub­
sistence production, and their dependence, instead, upon the
produce of other members of the society.

A society in which

the higher status members are divorced from production is taken

31

to be more stratified than one where such is not the case.
As already described, the Polynesian chiefs often
act as focal points of large-scale accumulations and redis­

tributions of goods.

All stewards, in fact, have some such

functions, the important difference being that usually all
surplus and other goods are at the chiefs’ call when a proper
occasion for collection arises, such as for storage against

famine, during and subsequent to communal labor, celebrating
life crisis rites in a chief’s family, for entertaining visi­

tors of rank and celebrating great religious feasts.

To what

extent such collection-redistribution roles are played by

chiefs, and whether or not forceful confiscations or secular
sanctions are visited on those who refuse to yield up goods,
are indices of stratification.

A general feature of consumption differentials in

Polynesia is the reservation of choice goods for persons of

rank.

Apart from this constant differential of strategic

consumption, there are wide variations in consumption of non-

strategic goods.

Some clothes, ornaments, mats and the like,

have elite circulations, and are consumed as insignia of rank.
The elaboration of insignia of rank is a measure of stratifi­
cation.

Passing now to social differentials, account should
be taken of general statements of observers as to the degree

of “arbitrariness," "despotism,” "equality,” "democracy,” etc.,

which they find in particular societies.

Due to the impres­

sionism and vagueness of the terminology involved, usually

32

no great weight can be placed on such evidence.

But some­

times valuable insights may be gained, especially if the im­

pressions of a number of different observers of the same
society show agreement.

Beside, the actual power structure

must be considered.

How and from whom do important social

decisions emanate?

A society in which the lowest status mem­

bers are consulted on important matters, or where such non­
stratified status groups as elders regulate socio-political

Q-ffs.irs, is less stratified than a society in which policies

are laid down by the highest in rank.
An important area in which social differentials may

manifest themselves is in the application of sanction to
wrong-doers, or "justice."

A significant question is the ex­

tent to which higher status allows the exercise of physical
force with impunity in defense of one’s rights.

Generally,

collective kinship retaliation in kind was the method of re­
venging injuries in Polynesia.

Within this pattern, chiefs

usually could muster the greatest body of supporters, and
hence inflict the greatest damage--as well as evade punish­

ment for injuries they inflicted on others.

But variations

in these abilities of upper status members occur.

The degree

to which chiefs rely on supernatural sanctions to enforce

orders, avenge injuries, etc., is an index of egalitarianism.
Important social differentials are revealed by the

tendency toward intermarriage within the highest status level.
In all Polynesian societies there is a preference, among

chiefs, for intra-status marriages--marriages which consolidate

3*

the economic, social and ceremonial prerogatives of high

rank.

In some societies this is rigidly enforced; close

relatives are picked as mates and marriage is never con­
tracted with those of low status.

In other societies, Intra-

chiefly marriage is merely a loose preference, often ignored.

The high position of certain members of Polynesian
societies is ceremonially celebrated by the famous mana-tabu

complex which surrounds them.

Chiefs are direct descendants

of divinity; consequently, they are imbued with certain

sacred power, mana.

In many Polynesian societies an elabor­

ate system of prohibitions (tabus) exists concerning touching

these sacred persons, touching their possessions, or other­
wise violating their sanctity.

Such systems of tabu are ex­

tensions of a general phenomenon of ceremonial prestige sur­

rounding the head of every family.

The elaborateness of the

tabu concerning members of high status levels is a measure of

the mana they possess, and hence may be used as a measure of

stratification.

Whether violations of these tabus are secu­

larly punished or merely supernaturally punished is also sig­

nificant stratification-wise.
Other ceremonial indications of stratification are

customs of paying respect to chiefs, such as bowing and other
forms of obeisance postures, and addressing or referring to
members of different status levels or their possessions by

unique sets of nouns and verbs.

Sometimes high status is sym­

bolized by exclusive participation in certain ceremonies such

as kava rites.

Finally, the degree to which the life crises

34

rites in high ranking families differ in kind and degree
from those generally found in the society is an index of
stratification.

*

a

*

In the following few chapters, fourteen Polynesian
cultures will be examined in light of these criteria of

stratification.

The data on each are not always complete,

but some information is usually to be found on every major

aspect of stratification.

Particular attention must be paid

to the character of the evidence regarding certain crucial
phenomena, such as the sanctions surrounding the personal and

economic tabus of chiefs, punishments of wrong-doers, the
ability of the chief to kill and banish others, and the like.

The data have been, in many cases, gathered from informants
long after the culture ceased to exist as an ongoing, autono­
mous entity.

There is always the possibility, especially

when dealing with sanctions, that the ’ideal’ culture, rather

than the actual workings of the society, has crept into

descriptive materials.
pretations.

Such could lead to serious misinter­

Consequently, whenever case materials are avail­

able on crucial points they are referred to in the discussion.
For convenience in handling the materials, the cul­

tures have been lumped into four major categories or group­

ings of stratification:

Group I, Group Ila, Group lib and

the materials in Chapters II-V.

35

Group III, from more to less stratified.

Finer discrimi­

nations are sometimes possible within a Group as to which
societies are most stratified, which least.

But due to the

rough and qualitative nature of the criteria used to esti­

mate the amount of stratification, more emphasis should be

placed on the major groupings than on any finer discrimina­
tions that may be made.

The two intermediate Groups, Ila

and lib, are placed under the same roman numeral, as the
amount of stratification is more nearly equivalent between
societies in Group Ila and societies in Group lib than be­

tween Group I and Ila, or III and IIb.

CHAPTER II
THE AMOUNT OF STRATIFICATION IN GROUP I SOCIETIES:
HAWAII, TONGA, SAMOA, TAHITI
In this chapter the data on the amount of stratifica­

tion in Hawaii, Tonga, Samoa and the Society Islands (primar­
ily Tahiti) are presented.

The outstanding characteristics

of stratification in these islands are summarized at the end
of the chapter.
Hawaii1

Status Levels
Three status levels may be delimited: the aril, chiefs

of islands and of large districts of an island, and their

"retinues" of close lineal relations ; konohiki, distant
relatives of a paramount chief; and the makaainana, the
"commoners," forming the major portion of the population.
The paramount chief of an island, usually the political unit,

was called Alii Nui.

His chief advisor was the kalaimoku.

The term kauwa, often used as a term of derogation,
is thought to refer to a fourth status level, an outcaste.

The status was inherited according to Malo.

2

E. Bryan

writes that the kauwa group may have been formed by those who
broke the ceremonial prohibitions (tabus), by conquered
peoples, or by remnants of an aboriginal stock.The eco­

nomic and social significance of such a group is not recorded.

There were no separate status levels of priests or
of craftsmen.

The status of a priest or a craftsman was essen­

tially determined by his inherited position in the society.

Economic Differentials
The Alii Nui managed, through the kalaimoku, the

use of the lands throughout an entire island; other alii
managed districts of the island, the konohiki managed
tracts of the districts; the makaainana held small subdivi­

sions of konohiki tracts.

Each manager or steward held sub­

ordinate rights, such as tabuuing the use of land, to the higher

2

Malo, op, cit. , 69•

^E. Bryan, op. cit., 65»

39

steward in whose domain the subordinate held lands and stew­

ardship prerogativesEach manager was considered the holder
of the "title” to the land he managed.

Prerogatives to the

use of the sea shore within one and one-half miles from shore

were held by the manager and the group bordering the sea.
The Alii Nui held the right to redistribute all lands
upon his accession.

In practice, severe tenure changes only

took place as a result of warfareThe paramount chief could
alienate the land of any manager and bestow it on someone else;
he did not appropriate it for himself (otherwise there would

have been vast differences in the land actually used by various
people, which there was not).

Anyone of higher status level

could dispossess a makaainana and replace him.

According to

Malo, secreting "wealth," and failure of the woman of the house-

hold to produce mats were sufficient reasons for a "commoner•s"
dispossession.

Dispossession might also result from failure

to contribute labor for irrigation works construction and from

failure to make the household plot productive.?

The frequency

of dispossessions is not recorded.

under the alii (Malo, op. cit., 56; Wise, op. clt., 88). A
few tracts were set aside for the alli's own support (Malo,

The system of access to water used in irrigation was
the same as access to land.

the allocation of water.

The alii and konohiki supervised

The allocation of water was propor­

tional to the amount of labor supplied by any land manager in

constructing the ditch, and with the acreage planted.

Thus

the amount of water controlled by status levels was propor­
tional to the amount of land controlled, and to the correspond­
ing number of subordinate relatives.

The konohiki or alii

could readjust agreed-upon allotments during dry seasons.

Any

makaainana who refused to contribute labor to the maintenance
of ditches and dams could be deprived of water rights or, as

indicated, of lands
The konohiki and alii initiated large-scale enter­
prises as a ditch construction, repair and cleaning, and also

engaged craftsmen.

Through their role in the redistributive

network they could subsidize such activities.

usually could not.

Makaainana

The konohiki, as supervisors of construc­

tion and maintenance of irrigation works, formed a "primitive

bureaucracy.r

They also directly supervised the production

of the households under them, making sure that the land was
cultivated.

As indicated, a makaainana could be dispossessed

if he failed to make a plot productive.
Anyone of higher status could call upon relatives of

lower status to contribute labor for communal enterprises.

Refusal by an alii to

Although evidence is not entirely consistent, it ap­
pears that the alii did not engage in subsistence production.^
The three status levels were the focal points of the

collection of larger and larger amounts of surplus food and

manufactured goods and of the redistribution of the same.

collection by an alii necessitated prior collection by konohlki

from makaalnana -- and down the same ladder for distribution.

Most of the goods so collected reached the producers even­

tually (especially those collected for feasts).
Goods passed up the social ladder at regular inter­

vals, especially in connection with the advent of ceremonies
requiring offerings, feasts concerning the upper status levels
(as life crisis rites in alii family), when specialists were
engaged by konohiki or alii, when war parties were organized

by the Alii Nui, to feed communal labor and particularly during
the annual religious cycle of the Makahiki when there was a
mass, ceremonial collection of goods involving an entire island,

it was usually for the purpose of amassing goods for redis­

tributions that those who managed lands placed tabus on the
use of the land or the appropriation of its resources.

Such

tabus were in the nature of conservation measures and occurred
generally in Polynesia.
Less regularized collections took the form of presents

or gifts made to alii and konohiki by those below them when­
ever some commodity was plentiful.

In no case was the amount

of the obligation fixed; it probably varied with the current

surplus.

The alii had large storehouses to hold accumulated

goods in preparation for redistributions.

According to Ellis,

an alii could seize foodstuffs from a makaalnana if the latter
did not contribute a sufficient amount.^

choice foods were reserved for the consumption of the chiefs.

The difference in strategic consumption, however, could not
have been marked — perhaps a difference between surfeit and

adequacy, as alii were fair and fat -- since Malo notes that
the ”commoners" were materially well off.15 Indeed, the obli­
gation of the alii to redistribute accumulated food insured an
adequate diet for all.

It was counseled to the paramount

chief:"When the people brought presents of food to the king

[Alii Nul].... It was a wise thing for the king to invite all
of the people to partake of the food, that they might not go

away fasting."

Malo writes that the storehouses controlled

by the Alii Nul were designed, "as a means of keeping the

people contented, so they would not desert the king."1?

This

slight difference in strategic consumption is fairly constant
throughout Polynesia along with the redistributive ethic, and
thus need not concern us in this comparative treatment.

Of greater comparative significance are differences in
the consumption of non-strategic or luxury goods.

These often

have an exclusive distribution among members of the upper
statuses and thus serve as insignia of rank.
was a multiplicity of insignia of rank.

Social Differentials

The native social system of the Hawaiian Islands has
gained notoriety for the marked social distinctions between

statuses and for the personal despotism and authority of chiefs
and "kings.”

An extreme, but not uncommon, view is expressed

by D. von den Steinen, "für Hawaii 1st charakteristlsch eine

ausgibildets Despotie des KBnigs...Der kSnig 1st der alleinlge
Herrscher und Machthaber.

Sein Wille 1st Gesetz."

1A

Malo

repeatedly writes of the despotism of chiefs and of the life
and death powers which the Alii Nui held over those of low
1 19
rank.

The administrative hierarchy in socio-political regu­
lation was the same as the hierarchy operative in economic
processes; consequently, the different status levels exerted
differing amounts of authority and power in the control of

interpersonal affairs.

The paramount chief, ostensibly in

consultation with other high chiefs — although Malo states

Case records are lacking.
Generally, collective action and responsibility on
the part of the parties involved was the rule for the applica­

tion of sanctions to actions as assault, murder, theft and so
forth.

As the higher the status, the larger the musterable

support, punishment varied according to the status of the par­

ties.

Within this framework a great deal of coercive force

could be applied by the chief in punishing those who infringed
his rights, especially if they were low in status.

People

were slain by alii, according to Ellis, Handy and Malo if they

violated an alii's economic or personal tabu, stole from a

he nor others list specific aboriginal cases.

To take another

example of the role played by status differentials, it is
stated that anyone who discovered a man tampering with a dam

could kill him on sight, but if the offender was of high rank,
the result of such a killing would be a considerable local
25
disturbance.

In Hawaii there were mechanisms for limiting the
tyrannical power of chiefs and konohikl.

Among them were

rebellion, assassination and moving out of the chief’s domain.

The presence of power inhibiting mechanisms of this sort is

a constant feature of Polynesian political life and hence will

be ignored in the comparative treatment of the islands.
It is notable in connection with status differentials

that the incest range condensed as one proceeds up the social
hierarchy.

Dispersal of economic, social and religious privi­

leges were thus prevented.

Care was taken to select the near­

est possible relative as a wife for a high chief — marriages

with makaalnana were scrupulously avoided and apparently hardly
ever occurred.

Among the highest chiefs, brother-sister, or

brother-half sister marriages were common.

Ceremonial Differentials

The Polynesian complex of restrictions (tabus) with
regard to approaching, touching or otherwise violating the
extreme sanctity (mana) of the high chiefs was elaborate and
rigidly enforced in Hawaii.

Malo describes a number of such

tabus, which commoners were slain for breaking, surrounding
the highest chiefs, and of which the following is only a par­

tial list: it was prohibited for a man’s shadow to fall on
the high chief’s house, back, robe or any possession; it was

prohibited to pass through his door, climb his stockade, to
put out in a racing canoe before him, to put on his robe or

his bark cloth; it was required that one kneel while he ate,

not appear in his presence with a wet bark cloth, or mud on
P

one’s head, etc."

In general, the makaalnana could not

touch anything used by the paramount chief.

Even the ground

the chief walked on became charged with mana and was avoided

by others.

In the presence of highest chiefs all had to pros­

trate themselves on the ground in a posture of extreme humil­
ity and obeisance.

An Alii Nul was preceded by heralds warn­

ing of his coming when he walked abroad so that the people
might prepare themselves.

addressing high chiefs.

Honorific terms were used in

this was a ’chief’s language’ or social dialect, as was found

elsewhere in Polynesia, particularly Samoa and Tonga.

A particularly spectacular

set of ceremonial phenomena which distinguished alii were

their life crisis rites.

There was a special ceremony to pro­

mote the conception of children among those of high rank, and

special ceremonies — including consecration of males on the
heiau — at birth.

Circumcision rites were elaborated in pro­

portion to rank, there being three forms of ceremony, for

high chiefs, lesser chiefs (konohiki ?) and others.

During an

alii’s sickness, an entire island might be practically immobi­
lized by tabus, no one being allowed to walk abroad, cook, or

the like.

Status Levels

Three status levels may be distinguished: the eike,

including the three paramount chiefs, viz., the Tui Tonga,
on
the Tui Haa Takalaua and the Tui Kanokupolu,
and the chiefs
and sub-chiefs of other large branch families (haa or "lin­

eages"); the metarule, attendants of the chiefs; and tua, the
populace.

The falefa or attendants of the TT were of the eike

level.

The falefa of the TK were also elke, although only

petty chiefs.

The highest chiefs had a "retinue" or "court"

composed of younger brothers and sisters and illegitimate

children of former high chiefs, as well as of falefa and mata-

pule.

Mariner mentioned the existence of a class of mua, in­

termediate between eike and matapule.

But Gifford found no

evidence of a functionally significant level of this type, al­

though offspring of eike-matapule unions were called ’mua.’^1
Craftsmen came from all statuses except eike, although certain

Economic Differentials

The system of access to land and sea resources was
similar in structure to that found in Hawaii, with the excep­
tion that, as irrigation was not practiced, chiefly control
over water rights was not present.

The TT held a position

and managership privileges comparable to that of the Alii Nul
in Hawaii.

The THT and TK were executives comparable to the

Hawaiian Kalaimoku.

Lesser eike and matapule exercised rights

equivalent to kohohlki.

Tua may be equated with mataainana.

The TT, however, could not dispossess other eike from the
tracts, tofia, which they hereditarily managed.A member of
a higher status level could remove a tua living under him, or
relocate a tua household on some other part of the tofia.
32

33

Mariner, op. clt., 11, 94.

Certain nets utilized for communal fishing expeditions were
controlled by chiefs from whom permission had to be obtained

to use them.
A large measure of control over various types of pro­

duction was exercised by members of the upper status level.

High level chiefs had petty chief emissaries who initiated
and directed many types of communal production, the labor for
which was supplied mainly by tua.

Some of these emissaries

were "national" supervisors, sent to various of the islands by
the TT; others were supervisors of the production of lineages
and were subordinates of the lineage chiefs.

Varieties of

communal fishing, the construction of chiefly tombs, "pigeon"

mounds and of fortresses were undertaken under the direction
of such supervisors.

Household production was also under the

supervision of these petty chiefs.

They inspected the fields

of each household to insure an adequate level of production.^

The inspectors also placed conservations! tabus.

They reported

on the status of production to the higher eike.

From these

reports, sanctions were placed on those who broke the tabus or

withheld surplus.

The punishments were physical, not super­

natural:
A commoner who broke the food tabus would be
thrashed or possibly killed. The infraction was
reported to Ata [the high chief of the lineage
under discussion], who determined the punishment
and ordered someone to administer it. There seems
to have been no fear of supernatural punishment.37

Gifford, op. clt., 68f, 98, 237; Williamson, op.

^Gifford, op. clt., 10^; Cf. 3^2.

The petty chief emissaries are the Tongan counterpart of the

"primitive bureaucracy” comprised of kohohikl in Hawaii.
There was a division of regulatory labor among them; some
supervised the placing of tabus on lands, others supervised

communal shell fish gathering, and so forth.
An alternative method by which communal labor was
directed was through decree in fono or council.

High chiefs

would summon lesser chiefs and inform them of the project to

be undertaken.

The latter would disperse and summon their fol

lowers in a fono or lesser order (following the land tenure

and status structure) until the source of most manpower, the
tua, were reached.

There were no discussions in fonos, only

decrees.

Via fonos and emissaries as well as through frequent
gifts (especially of first fruits) food and goods passed up

and down the social ladder in a redistributive system similar
to that of Hawaii.

Some of the food was used to support com­

munal production and specialized production.

As matapule and

eike were focal points of collection, they usually engaged
specialists.

However, commoners or tua might do so also if

they could amass the necessary food.

There is evidence that

eike siphoned off some of the accumulated food to feed their

own households, and thus eike may not have engaged in produc38
tion themselves.
In a fono described by Mariner, the chief
of the Vavau group urged all his chiefs to engage in agricul-

Social Differentials
At the present time the arbitrary powers of Tongan

eike have been superceded by British rule; hence, Gifford did

Redistribution was the hallmark of chieftainship.
This is graphically illustrated in Mariner’s account of the
reaction of a Tongan chief upon being instructed in the nature
of money:"’Certainly money is much handler, and more conveni­
ent, but then, as it will not spoil by being kept, people
will store it up, instead of sharing it out, as a chief ought
to do, and thus become selfish; whereas, if provisions were
the principal property of man, and it ought to be, as being
both the most useful and the most necessary, he could not
store it up, for it would spoil, and so he would be obliged
either to exchange it away for something else useful, or
share it out to his neighbors, and inferior chiefs and depen­
dents, for nothing’." (Mariner, pp. cit.. 1, 213-1^.)

Ceremonial Differentials
Ceremonially, the Tui Tonga was to Tonga as the Alii
Nui was to a Hawaiian island.

His sanctity was as great and

the tabu complex which surrounded him seems to have been about
6?
as elaborate as that in Hawaii.
Gifford repeatedly reports

that tabus laid by chiefs carried no supernatural sanctions,

the implication being that sanctions, if any, were secular.

He seems to be referring primarily to economic tabus, as he
cites examples of supernatural punishments for breach of the

personal tabu of eike.

However, after describing some personal

tabus pertaining to high chiefs he states: "Breaches are sub­
ject to punishment by thrashing, often by the chief himself.

60

„
Ibid., 184-185.
61Ibid., 112-113.

62See Gifford, op. cit., 71-75, 115f, 342-345;

b9

The conception of a supernatural punishment seems not to be
63
entertained.”
Aside from the usual restrictions regarding

the touching of the TT’s body and food, he was often carried
about on a litter, and was greeted with a special salutation

and institutionalized postures of humility and obeisance.

A

person wishing to see the TT first sat outside his house and
told a matapule attendant who ’announced’ the visit to the TT.
"Social dialects” were used for the TT and his high female rela­

tives; another was used for other eike, and another for the
people.

The tabus and ceremonial privileges of other high

chiefs were slightly less elaborate than those of the TT, al6k
though still far from simple. * Both personal tabu and the
ceremonial observation of such crisis rites as birth, marriage,
and death varied in elaborateness according to the status of
the person involved.

This correlates with the differing role

of tua, matapule and eike in the system of collection and dis­
tribution.

The higher the status, the more people ruled, the

greater the surplus collected, hence the greater the feast and

ceremony.

For example, the TT’s daughter had special puberty

rites; when a chief was ill, human sacrifices might be offered
for his recovery; long and elaborate ceremonies followed the

death of an eike during which the body might be embalmed, females

^Gifford, pp. cit., 345.
^Ibld., 123f.

60

strangled for burial with the body and a special class of
65
"undertakers" performed various rites *
Status was further symbolized by the order of prece­

dence in serving kava among men of high rank at ceremonial oc­

casions.

Seating positions at such affairs not only indicated

the fine gradations in rank, but the respective social position
of eike, matapule and tua.

Samoa
Status Levels

The stratification system in aboriginal Samoa can
be divided into two status levels, those with titles, matai,

and those without.

Within the titled level there were various

shades of rank and two types of titles, chief (alii) and
ing chief (tulafale).

talk­

These two titles were not ranked vis a

vis each other, but were found on all grades within the matai

This

In the vil­

lages, non-titled males, young and adult, formed a distinct
group, aumaga, led by the village chief’s heir, the manaia.
Wives of matais formed a group under the wife of the high

chief, and unmarried women formed a group also, the aualuma,

under the taupo, the ceremonial maid of the village, a member
of the village chief’s household.

Thus given the distinction

of titled and non-titled individuals, and the distinction be­
tween the sexes, a well defined stratification system of at

least two status levels is found in Samoa.

as ga’ia, who had functions in district and other large gov­

ernmental units. The lesser matai only functioned in village

Economic Differentials
Matais, both individually as heads of extended fam­

ily groups, but more significantly as a group in the village
fono, held the highest prerogatives with regard to access of

land and sea resources.

Fonos laid general prohibitions on

the use of land (tabus).

Control exercised by fonos over pro­

ductive activities was far-reaching.

According to Buelow, as cited by Williamson, the fono
of a place supervised the production of the individual house70
holds.
The council controlled both pig-breeding and land
cultivation.

Fono members, according to Buelow, made weekly

inspections.

If a family was found not to be producing

enough, it was ordered to do more planting. "The decisions
71
of the assembly were binding."
Failure to comply with the
demands of the fono brought a fine of pigs, fowls, tapa or

forced labor on "public works."

According to Kraemer, the

village fono issued directions to the households as to the

They saw to it that the requisite goods were collected.

although this equipment could be borrowed, a present was ex­

once punished by death.
The structure of the administrative hierarchy was,

as already indicated, one composed of a series of fonos of
matais.

The village fonos contributed representatives to

higher order fonos involving a number of villages or dis­

tricts, up to the fono which represented the autonomous poli­

tical unit.

In Manua, the higher order fonos were of virtu­

ally no importance, and the chief of the island group had, by
consequence, no great power.

In other areas as well, the

villages were more or less autonomous, and other fonos were

primarily concerned only with the nominations of the highest

chiefs, or, more importantly, with conducting warfare.
The village fono exercised authority in economic mat­
ters, as indicated above; It also ratified, even nominated,

candidates for matalship, created titles, deposed title hol­
ders, settled disputes and imposed punishments (see below),
regulated the conduct of the village in warfare and held cere­

monies to avert various types of catastrophes.

The method of

arriving at decisions was not by vote, but rather by debate

until unanimity was reached.

According to Schultz, actual de­

cisions at fonos had already been agreed upon through private

discussion and, "everyone knows beforehand more or less what

In all fonos the tulafale, representing

powerful tulafale alii who exerted considerable authority in

Ceremonial Differentials

The highest chiefs derived sanction for their posi­

tions from descent from the gods, and this in turn was re­

flected by the ceremonial tabus, mostly referring to contact
with their bodies or possessions, which surrounded them.
These prohibitions were as elaborate as those referring to

Status Level

Society Islands

108

Three non-egalitarian status levels can be distin­
guished: 1) the arii, or ruling group including their near
patrilineal relatives the iotoai; 2) raatira, sub-chiefs un­
der the arii, probably unrelated to them; and 3) manahune,
109
the great body of people.

of our information concerns Tahiti primarily.
