This scheme is in essential agreement with that of
Handy (1930,42). It differs from Orsmand’s (Henry, op,cit.,229)
in that here all aril are put into one, not two groups. It dif­
fers from that of Ellis (op, cit., 111,95) in that Ellis groups
manahune and raatira into one level and puts slaves and menials
in the third and lowest group. Our own, I think, will be found
to have the greatest functional significance.

74

Priestly status followed the secular status of the
priest.

Craftsmen also took the status assigned by birth.

The hiva, body of warriors or chief warrior, and

other functionaries forming the household (or "retinue") of
paramount chiefs were close relatives (iotoai) of the chief
they served.
The so-called 11 slaves" mentioned in the literature

were artisans whose family had customarily been craftsmen
for an arii and in that manner were attached to the aril’s

service, although not necessarily living with or near him.110
Economic Differentials

The arii, raatira and manahune fit into a system of
overlapping managership or stewardship of land and sea re­

sources as in Hawaii and Tonga.

Each steward had tabuing

powers over his section of land, those of the paramount chief

extended over all the land of the polity.111

The paramount

chief had the right to depose and banish a raatira, but even

so, the land and title controlled were kept in the raatira’s

111

had as much "individual rights" as anyone else. They, of
course, did not exercise control or managership over sections
as large as did raatira or aril.

family.

112

Some tools of production, as communal fishing

equipment, being made at the instigation of aril, were under
their control, and their permission had to be obtained in

order to use the equipment.
The aril controlled production, especially communal

and craft production.

Raatira were intermediaries in tasks

of organizing and supplying communal labor.

Raatira also sub­

sidized craft production, but the aril had specialists who
were more or less permanently attached to their households.

There is no information in the sources consulted as to whether
the raatira, or perhaps the iotoai, supervised and inspected

household production as was done in other islands in Group I.

In distribution, the three status levels played essen­

tially the same roles as did the Hawaiian and Tongan status
levels in redistributive activities.

As in these other islands,

as well as in Samoa, such activities were quite frequent and

were often grandiose in scale.

Social Differentials
General statements about the amount of authority
wielded by aril fall into two classes.

On the one hand, the

rule of the aril is spoken of as strong and arbitrary, while
on the other hand, the influence of raatira on the regulation
of Interpersonal affairs is emphasized.

Ellis compared the

"arbitrary monarchy" in the Society Islands to that of Hawaii,
although he found the different grades of society not so

"strongly defined" as in Hawaii.

He differentiated both

Hawaii and the Society Islands from the more egalitarian (our
terminology) societies of the Marquesas and New Zealand.
Absolute despotism of chiefs is indicated by early Spanish voy­
agers and by Moerenhout.^^ Bougainville thought that aril

had life and death powers over commoners.

125

Cook, Lesson, Moerenhout, J.H. Forster, Tati Salmon
126
and Ellis agree in stressing the powers of the raatira.

Within their own sub-provinces, as it were, the raatira en­
joyed a considerable autonomy and usually more so in this do­

main than the chief had over the entire polity.

This was a

great check on the despotism of the paramount chief, and neces­
sitated that he have raatira support at every turn.

For ex­

ample , if the chief decided on some action such as war, he

Ceremonial Differentials
The tabus protecting the persons, clothing, habita­

tions and other possessions of the high aril were fully as
elaborate as on other Group I islands.

Everything the arli

touched, including the ground he walked on, houses he entered,
etc., became charged with mana.

Consequently, the highest aril

did not enter other people's houses lest they have to vacate

and he was carried around on the backs of special bearers.
Honorific titles were applied to chiefs and to their equipment

on ceremonial occasions.

According to Handy, special forms of
llj.1
address were used for the three status levels.
The drink­

ing of kava was restricted to chiefs.

In the presence of high­

est chiefs, inferiors had to strip to the waist.

The sanc­

tions regarding the breaking of the aril’s tabu seem to have

been supernatural in large measure.

However, Ellis reports

that those who stood over paramount chiefs, or passed a hand

The ceremonial distinction between statuses does not

seem to have been a constant day-to-day affair.

Ellis notes

some striking contrasts; sometimes the paramount chief,

At any rate, Ellis also found

that: "The difference between [the aril] and the common people
was, in many respects, far greater than that which prevails

between the rulers and the ruled in most civilized countries.

Life crisis rites in aril families were different in
degree of elaboration and different in form from life crisis

rites among others.

Similarly, raatira life crises were more

ceremoniously celebrated than those of manahune.

sacrifices, an elaborate birth feast and seclusion of the

Among lesser chiefs, pigs

The tabu

The ceremonies occurring at the ini­

tiation, marriage and death, as well as at the installation

and during sickness of the highest chiefs were as spectacular
and distinct as the birth rites.

Summary
Group I societies, Hawaii, Tonga, Samoa and the

Society Islands have structurally complex systems of rank.
Hawaii, Tonga and Tahiti have three well defined status lev­

els, each having definite roles in economic and social life.
Some question arises as to whether three or two status levels

were operative in Samoa.

Although the problem is difficult

of resolution, there can be little doubt that the division of

Samoan matais into chiefs and talking chiefs introduces another
dimension of structural complexity in the ranking system of
—
Handy, op. cit., 24.
l^Arii Taimai E, op. cit.. passim; Ellis, op. cit.,
i, 349-3^0, 411» iii, 10?f, et passim; Ëenry, op. cit.,
175-178, 186f, 292f, et passim.

85

Samoa.

Preeminent rights of access to resources were held

by the upper status levels in each Group I society.

These

rights not only included prerogatives of managership over
land and sea as manifest in general tabuing powers, but also
those of dispossession of subordinates and of severe punish­

ment, without retaliation, against those who infringed chiefly
decrees regarding land use.
The higher status levels controlled communal and

craft production.

More significant for comparisons with

other Polynesian societies, in Hawaii, Tonga and Samoa, chiefs
exercised direct supervisory control over the production of
individual households.

Supervision of household production

included orders as to what was to be planted and the amount
thereof, and inspection by functionaries of the chiefs to see

that enough was planted.

Sanctions were again secular, and

applied with impunity by the chiefly level.

Whether such a

situation pertained in the Society Islands was not discovered.

A complex redistributive hierarchy with chiefs act­

ing as focal points of collection and redistribution, and the
middle status level acting as intermediaries, was character­

istic of Group I stratification.

The customary obligation of

rendering surplus to chiefs was enforced by forceful confis­

cations of goods, again without reprisals in most cases.

Some

material benefits were derived above and beyond the prestige
gained from redistribution.

Chiefs and their households were

in some measure fed from surplus goods collected from others,

8b

and the highest chiefs were divorced in large measure from
subsistence production.

Differential consumption may also be measured in the

large range of objects which served as insignia of chiefly
There were sumptuary clothing distinctions, differ­

status.

ences in house types, in ornamentation, etc., between various
ranks »
Arbitrary exercise oi physical force and control of

socio-regulatory processes by chiefs is evidenced in general
statements of observers, and in the differential applications

of sanctions to wrongdoers according to status.

Whereas

private, continuing retaliations in kind were the general rule
where slight difference in status obtained between the parties
concerned, offenses against chiefs were punished severely, and

with impunity.

Death, banishment and destruction of property

of others by chiefs were not infrequent punishments.

the social distinctions between the chiefs and others

were also manifest in extreme emphasis on intrafamilial mar­
riages in the upper status level.

Marriage of high chiefs

with members of lower status levels hardly seems to have oc­
curred at all in Group I societies; and when it did, as in

Tahiti, the difference in status could be ceremonially
equalized.

Ceremonial distinctions between ranks was well marked.
The tabu and mana pattern regarding the high chiefs was ex­
tremely complex.

Although the personal tabus regarding chiefs

almost by definition carry supernatural sanction, it was also

87

found that there was, in many cases, no hesitation in apply­
ing severe secular punishments against low ranking tabu

breakers♦
Chiefs' status was also ceremonially signaled by such
customs as elaborate postures of obeisance, the bearing of

the highest chiefs on litters or on the backs of others,
chiefs’ languages and exclusive participation in kava cere­

monies.
The amount of ceremony celebrating the life-cycle of

the individual varied according to rank.

Special forms of

life crisis rites, held on a spectacular scale, obtained in

chiefly families.
In view of the amount of stratification in Group I

societies, it is no accident that of all the Polynesian soci­
eties impacted by European influence, only in Hawaii, Tahiti
and Tonga was there the development of native secondary states

under the role of indigenous kings.

Only Samoa, of Group I

societies, failed to produce a native government, a fact that

may be partially attributed to the localization of authority
on a village level there.

-%
It is extremely difficult as well as hazardous, in
light of the lack of quantitative materials as well as the
nature of the evidence, to rank the particular Group I soci­

eties in a hierarchy of the amount of stratification.

The

great power wielded by Hawaiian konohiki and alii in the

88

supervision of water use, frequent and forceful sanctions
applied by alii upon those who infringed their tabus (both

personal and economic) and the rigid prohibition on inter­

status alliances in Hawaii, which crystallized in the famous

brother-sister marriagesseem to favor the Hawaiian
organization as most stratified.

On the other hand, there

are very good indications that comparable power was wielded
by Tongan eike against those who infringed their tabus, as
well as detailed evidence of rigid supervision of household
production in Tonga, and in Samoa as well.

On the basis of

the materials considered it seems safe to say only that Hawaii
was probably slightly more stratified than other Group I

societies.

1^-7Similar marriages were found among the high
chiefs or "kings" of highly stratified ancient Peru and
Egypt.

CHAPTER III
THE AMOUNT OF STRATIFICATION IN GROUP Ila SOCIETIES:
MANGAREVA, EASTER ISLAND, MANGAIA, UVEA

Materials on the amount of stratification in Mangareva,
Easter Island, Mangala and Uvea are presented in this chapter.
The characteristics of stratification in this group of islands
are summarized at the end of the chapter.

Mangareva

Status Levels
The status levels of Mangareva are very difficult to
analyze.

Both Buck and Laval agree that the two basic grades

^Principal Sources : P. Buck, Ethnology of Mangareva,
B,P,Bishop Mus. Bull», 157, 1938a; Honore Laval, L'Histoire

Jour, Poly. Soc., 27, 115-131, 1918; Williamson, 1924.
A great deal of ambiguity and lack of clarity is char­
acteristic of the literature relating to the stratification
system of Mangareva. This is true, unfortunately of early and
scientific observers alike. Terms such as "rent," "landlord,"
"tax," "tribute," "king," "estate," "contract,” etc., belie
the essential kinship organization. Meek has commented on the
lack of reliability of reports on land tenure wherein "English
terms such as 'rent' or 'lease' have been employed to denote
practices which bear only a superficial resemblance to those
denoted by these terms. The gifts given to chiefs as adminis­
trators have been assumed to be 'rent' and the chiefs to be
'landlords'." (C.K.Meek, Land, Law and Custom in the Colonies,
1946, 11-12.) The major ethnographic works on Mangareva are
replete with such obvious distortions, and the misconceptions
they occasion have been perpetuated in interpretive analyses.

After

terms used to refer to individuals, which include : togoiti,

akariki, mornomomo, pakaora, ragatira, tau, kio and kiore.

A

It may be indicated here that ’togoiti,’

’akariki1 and ’mornomomo’ refer to three grades of chiefs or

"nobles’Pakaora,’ ’ragatira’ and ’tau’ refer to inferior

Pakaora in par­

ticular denotes a person who has been given a large share of
conquered lands, thus becoming a steward of high order, although
still an urumanu.

warfare.

’Kio’ were members of groups defeated in

There were several courses open to kio after defeat.

They might take refuge with a powerful chief, perhaps a rela­

tive; they could occupy marginal, non-breadfruit land; or

they or their children might be adopted into another group.
When they attach themselves to another household they are

called "servants" or "menials" in the literature.

Kio usually

eventually either retrieved their lands through a reversal of
the fortunes of war, or were absorbed into other groups.

Kio

who remained fugitives after a war were termed kiore.

With regard to the number of status levels, the hier­
archy is best analyzed as composed of something more than two

levels, yet something less than three.

A tendency toward

three levels is manifest in two directions.

2

See pp. 298-300.

First the pakaora,

91

as managers of large plots are structurally comparable to the

intermediate levels in Group I societies.

However, pakaora had

little special functional significance in the stratification

system, they had

few roles or prerogatives, if any, beyond

those held by other urumanu.

Kio represent a tendency toward

the crystallization of a status level in another direction.

But again, the absorption of kio into other groups rapidly in­
tegrated them into the lowest grade of urumanu.
Neither craftsmen nor priests occupied or came from

any particular hierarchial status level.

The status of these

specialists was dependent on their inherited position in the
society.
Economic Differentials

The system of tenure of resources was identical in
principle to that which existed in Hawaii, Tonga and Tahiti.

The ten or twelve tribal chiefs were the supreme managers of

large sections of the islands.

Those of lesser status were

stewards of smaller sections.

Certain of the urumanu, prob­

ably kio, did not cultivate but concentrated on fishing.
kio were attached as menials to houses of "nobles."

Other

Buck

frequently makes the differential between "nobles" and "com­

moners" the possession of cultivable plots, the commoners hav3
ing none.
However, even people of very low status, kio, are
mentioned as cultivating land, albeit under someone else, and

3

Buck, op. cit., passim.

942

there is no doubt that "commoner” tau (often translated as
”farmers”) held customary usufruetory rights to land plots

Higher stewards could depose any of their underlings from stew­

ardship over a section of land for refusing to yield surplus

to chiefs, neglecting to produce crops, or an offense to the
higher stewards.

As was possibly true in all Polynesian dis­

possessions, the title would be bestowed on a near relative of
the former holder.*?

Tabuing powers went with land stewardship.

The punish­

ment for breach of a conservation tabu seems to have been ap­

plied by supernatural agents.

Buck reports that, "The insti­

tution of tapu applied sanctions generally in matters of a
religious nature in which punishment was usually relegated to
a supernatural agent."& It is not clear in this passage

whether it is economic tabus or personal tabus of chiefs about

which Buck was writing.

Referring specifically to tabus on

picking breadfruit Buck writes, on the same page: "Signs re­

presenting tapu impressed thieves, who feared supernatural

punishment if they infringed."
There are no direct statements that any differential

access to the tools of production prevailed.

On comparative

grounds it can be inferred, however, that equipment used in
communal strategic production was primarily under the chiefs'

^Ibid., 163, et passim; Laval, op. cit.. 223, 286f,

It might be mentioned here that irrigation agricul­

control.

ture was very small in scale, breadfruit rather than taro being
the major crop, and thus various rights regarding the use of

waters and ditches such as pertained in Hawaii were probably
non-existent.

The chiefs were the primary initiators of communal
and craft production, as was true in Group I societies.

House­

hold production, however, was supervised by household heads;

there is no evidence of a duly constituted body of chiefs or
inspectors who exercised direct control over the production

of individual households.

The collection-redistribution functions of the upper
status level in Group I societies were performed in Mangareva
by tribal chiefs; but there were fewer intermediaries, as
Hawaiian konohiki, between producer and distributor.

The oc­

casions of redistribution were of a similar nature as those

which involved redistribution in Group I: at life crisis rites

in chiefly families, during religious ceremonies, accompanying
communal labor, in compensating specialists and during famines.

Here, due to the nature of the major crop, breadfruit, large
collections were made seasonally -- at the time of harvest -­

and stored by the chiefs until used.

D’Urville, as cited by

Williamson, reported that any one refusing to yield his sur7
plus to the chiefs might be severely punished, even killed.

Social Differentials
General statements about the power structure of Man-

gareva reveal that the chiefs wielded a fair degree of author­

ity.

Almost all statements of this nature indicate that the

coercive power that the chief had was used temperately for
fear of revolt.

As has been already pointed out, every

Polynesian group had mechanisms, as revolt, for dealing with
chiefly abuse of power.

It may be significant, however, that

observers invariably have felt called upon to specifically

note the threat of using such mechanisms against the chiefs
whenever the power of the chiefs of Mangareva is discussed.

For example, Caillot (whom Williamson does not consider a
source too trustworthy) wrote that the paramount chief made
war and peace, commanded canoes and armies, governed the

nation, decreed "laws," rendered justice, instituted feasts
and ordered deaths for human sacrifice, but was arbitrary only

to a point, due to the threat of deposition,

Metraux, com­

menting on Laval's manuscript found that certain of the
"seigneurs" were tyrannical, but most "'aimaient à conserver
leur fermiers',"

Most interesting, from a comparative point

of view is Metraux' statement that, "Les Mangaréviens n'ont

pas poussé la devotion du pouvoir royal au meme degré que leur
freres de Hawaii ou de Tonga, mais leurs rois étaient entourés

"^Metraux, op, cit», 6^1*

96

d’un ’respect mystérieux.**

As indicated, political powers were concentrated in
togoiti hands.

Aside from powers arising out of their posi­

tions in the productive and distributive aspects of the
economy, they also exerted authority in matters pertaining to

offense and defense and the regulation of interpersonal af­

fairs.

The chiefs played initiatory roles in the decisions

regarding mobilization for war.

For such important decisions,

informal discus si. ons were held among them.
The chiefs had some jurisdictional powers.

To settle

disputes over land boundaries they sent emissaries (vavao), or
appeared in person.

When the chief appeared himself, he at­

tempted to fix the boundary by decree.

The decrees were not

binding: "Il était cependant des individus entetés qui ne
cédaient pas a la voix de leur chef ou ne le faisaient qu’a

contrecouer.

Aussi cieux-la couraient le risque d’avoir à

déménager, a moins que leur maitre n'eut pour eux de 1’attache-

ment.”

The predominant form of sanction for punishing wrong­

doers was collective retaliation in kind.

Revenge for the

murder of an ordinary party by an ordinary party called for
sorcery or a counter-killing.

But where large differences in

status were involved, the party of rank could often punish such
offenses severely and without retaliation.

Ceremonial Differentials

There were many tabus surrounding the paramount
chiefs.

The high chief’s person (especially head), house,

food, utensils and the like were sacred.

As indicated, the

violation of these tabus might be punished by spearing.

Togoiti were direct descendants of divinity; their heirs

were born in sacred places, and their abortions were dei­
fied.

We have found no evidence that chiefs were carried

on litters, that there was any developed ’chiefs’ language,’
or obeisance postures indicating extreme humility.

The life crisis rites in chiefly families were

much more elaborate than those among non-chiefs.

For ex­

ample , in chiefly families the pregnant mother was secluded

and guarded by priestesses; her body was specially painted;

she had a hair cutting ceremony in the eighth month which in­
cluded a whole series of feasts and rituals; the child was

born on the marae, etc.

The children of the paramount chief

were secluded in a special mountain nursery until adolescence,
being cared for by special attendants.

Similarly, marriage

and death rites among tagoiti took unique forms such as the
human sacrifices at a chief’s death.

The basic status levels were, as in Mangareva, the

chiefs and the non-chiefs.

Chiefs were heads of the ten large

branch families, or "tribes."

The secular leadership over

leader of the militarily dominant tribe.

The system of access to land and sea resources was

the pattern of overlapping stewardship already seen elsewhere.

The tribal chiefs were the paramount managers of tribal lands
and seas.

The ariki mau had supreme tabuing powers.

Mangala^®
Status Levels

Again two basic levels may be demarcated, an upper
and lower.

The upper, chiefly, status level includes the

two high priest-chiefs and the chiefs and sub-chiefs (pava
and kairanga respectively) of the large kin units or tribes.

Especially significant is the leader of the militarily domi­

nant tribe, the secular leader or chief of all Mangala,
NTemporal Lord of Mangala."

His position was temporary;

it depended on the continuation of the military dominance

of his tribe.

level.

The mass of people formed the lower status

Groups of people who were defeated in war were

either pushed out of their lands, eventually perishing;

took inferior lands and retained the possibility through war

or beneficence of the conquerors of regaining their origi­
nal lands; or were absorbed by adoption in a wife’s or
mother’s tribe.
Other priests beside the two priest-chiefs took

their status according to their secular status.

Craftsman­

ship was not particularly a status occupation.

Economic Differentials

The tribal chiefs were the supreme stewards over land
and sea in tribal districts in a pattern of access of re­
sources similar to the management system already frequently

encountered.

Defeated peoples, as indicated, were either

ceded land by the conquering tribe, or by relatives, or were
pushed into marginal lands.

Those defeated in war could be

ejected from lands which they held under conquerors or in other
60
tribes.
No evidence was found that non-chiefs not defeated

in war could be dispossessed by their chiefs.

In aboriginal

times there was a chiefly official, "The Ruler of Food,” who

in peace times could initiate closed seasons on certain water

and land resources ; since the nineteenth century this has been
61
carried out on a district-wide scale only.
Punishments for
breaking these economic tabus are discussed below.
Buck notes, in a general discussion of Cook Island

tenure, that with regard to irrigation waters, the chief who
ruled over a land area controlled the irrigation channel and
the distribution of water.

He could cut off water from anyone

who had not met the customary obligations of rendering his sur62
plus production to the chief.
Whether this refers primarily
to Rarotonga, where irrigation was more widespread than in

Mangaia, is not known.

As in the other islands discussed, communal produc­
tion and much craft production was initiated by the chiefs as

a corollary of their roles in the redistribution system.

With

regard to the actual participation of the upper status level
in subsistence production it may be noted that menial labor in
powerful families was done often by ’commoner’ relatives of

that family, who were usually members of defeated groups.
The priest-chiefs and their families were fed by daily con­
tributions of food.^ There was no bureaucratic control of

the production of individual households; rather, the family
head supervised familial production.
The upper status level members directed the accumula­
tion and redistributions of large masses of foods and other

Social Differentials

The administrative hierarchy corresponded to the sys­
tem of land managership and the roles of the respective sta­
tuses in distribution.

The tribal sub-chiefs formed an in­

formal council which aided the chief of the tribe in directing

most affairs, as preparations for war.

The TLM evidently ex­

erted the major share of his authority, in aboriginal days,

only over people whom he had conquered.

If there was trouble

in other districts the TLM merely visited and exhorted the
local chiefs to preserve the peace.

High priests, through

oracular proceedings, were able to exercise authority in some

instances.

Two cases are referred to by Buck wherein priests,

acting as mediums, saved sections of other tribes by decree­
ing banishment rather than death.in general, priests

"were wise enough to follow the feelings and desires of their
group when making known the wishes of the gods."
The underlying principle regulating the application

of sanction to wrongdoers was lex talionis, but with the

usual differences in support, reprisals varied with the status

of the parties concerned.

Most thefts were made by members

of defeated tribes, consequently, the holder of the plot dealt

harshly with thieves, spearing them if he could.

Gill records

a number of such cases.Buck interestingly points out that :

With regard to the punishment for murders, the same

situation pertained as with theft and adultery:

"If the family

of the murderer was weak and had no status, it was useless for
them to withstand a powerful family seeking vengeance. ...

If ... the positions were reversed it was equally useless for
a weak family to seek redress. ... If both families were power­
ful, the only recourse was war."^

Although the chiefs were able to punish severely and

without fear of retaliation in some instances, it is inter­
esting that: "Punishment for infringement of tapus was sup­
posed to be brought about automatically by the gods.

The gods

sometimes acted slowly. ... Sometimes the gods, if left to

themselves, acted not at all."

Buck does not differentiate

between personal and economic tabus here, but in either case,
infringement would be an offense against a member of the

chiefly level.

Ceremonial Differentials

Although the data on differing socio-ceremonial
activities and privileges are meagre, they indicate fairly

well marked distinctions in this aspect of stratification.
The concentration of social-ceremonial prerogatives, privi­

leges and obligations seems to have been heaviest with
respect to the two high priests.

As long as they did not

meddle in secular affairs, and especially when performing

sacred functions on their marae (temples), their persons were
sacred and inviolable.

They not only officiated at the in­

stallation of the TLM and the Ruler of Food, but, because of
their sacredness were not tatooed, could not dance and were

given marked obeisance, even by the TLM, when officiating on
their marae.

The sacredness of the TLM was not as great as

that of an officiating priest-chiefe

77Ibld., 90.

However, his installation

114

was an island-wide affair attended with much ceremony and

human sacrifices,

The life crisis rites in chiefly families were more
elaborate than those in non-chiefly families.

A number of

examples might be given: the birth of children of rank was
"a social occasion"

7A

and was celebrated by special cere­

monial forms; children of chiefs were secluded in youth to

develop fair, fat bodies; among the masses, living together
constituted marriage, but there were marriage ceremonies

among the ranking families; for some months after the death
of a person of chiefly blood, elaborate death recitals were
held, etc.

Status Levels

Two status levels may be distinguished:

1) the

titled members of the island community including the para­

mount chief, his six or seven counselors (or "ministers" as

they are termed in the literature) and the village chiefs;
2) the remainder of the population.

that the village chiefs were only on the border of true chief­
tainship as they were called ’village elders,’ matua fenua,

On

It may be remarked here that a seeming egalitarian character­

istic of the stratification system, the participation of all
adult males in village councils, is somewhat obviated by the
fact that in these councils there was no vote and the presid­

ing chief did most of the talking; the others concurred.
Economic Differentials
With regard to access to strategic resources, the

greatest difference between status levels occurred with
respect to village taro lands, which although they accrued

to villages as a whole, were allotted to households by the
village council, which, as indicated, was dominated by the
village chief.

Islets of the reef are said to belong to the

villages opposite them on the main island, but little is re­
corded of their use.

Aside from the village lands and cer­

tain lands not claimed by anyone in particular, other land
(the greatest share) was held by kinship groups or lineages.
However, the lineage head who managed the land did not neces­

sarily hold a title.

General statements about the power of chiefs in

119

Councils, under their presiding chiefs, formed the

administrative structure of the society.

Disregarding the

district council, which is presently found but is almost cer.
96
tainly a result of acculturation,
there were two types or

councils:

1) the council of the paramount chief and his min­

isters, all of whom were ranked vis a vis each other and all
of whom were members of the upper status level; 2) the village
council composed of the village chief and all adult males.

It is through these bodies that questions relating to public

works, festivals and even disputes were decided.

When a course

of action was arrived at by the council of the paramount chief,

the edict was communicated (by members of the paramount chief's

families acting as executives) to the lower order councils,
where it was effected.

Beside participating in the paramount

chief's council the so-called 'ministers' had certain functions.

For example, one of these councillors acted as a director of
offensive and defensive activities with one of the village

chiefs serving as the actual battle leader.
It is reported that councils had jurisdictional author­

ity.

They heard and adjudicated complaints, imposing fines

of food, bark cloth, mats and labor, and shaving a man's head
for adultery.

Burrows writes that chiefs adjudicated dis­

putes over land "on the basis of the chief's knowledge of tra-

as the only case discovered of reporting offences to council

and fining concerned the district council (a post-contact in­

also after European influence had begun."

The method of en­

forcing decisions is not described.

According to Burrows, "An old Uvean custom, now aban­

whole villages for misdeeds of one inhabitant."98

Another method of punishment, probably pre—European,
was through collective retaliation.

Here status was a factor.

122

was also symbolized by participation of chiefs in ceremonial

kava servings.
Birth celebrations of the first-born in chiefly fam­

ilies were more elaborate than those in other families and
the death rites of the three highest chiefs included special

Summary
Group Ila societies, Hangareva, Easter Island,

Mangala and Uvea, had stratification systems structurally
divisible into two status levels.

In Mangareva, Easter Is­

land and Mangala there was a tendency to form a third level
composed of peoples defeated in war.

In Hangareva, the ten­

dency toward a third level was also manifest in the distinc­
tion of pakaora, high order land managers, from other urumanu
(lower status level) holding smaller plots.

With less com­

plex status systems than Group I, economic and social proces­
ses under the chiefs’ directions can be expected to have been

less intricate in Group Ila.

The chain of command was shorter.

As in Group I, preeminent rights of access to re­
sources were held by the upper status levels.

These rights

included prerogatives of dispossessing lesser order stewards

in Hangareva and Easter Island, and of dispossessing defeated
peoples from assigned lands in Mangala.

The right of dis-

123

possession appears not to have prevailed in Uvea.

Some evi­

dence suggests that Mangaian chiefs could deprive underlings

of access to irrigation waters.

General tabuing powers were

an aspect of chiefly control over land and sea.

Sanctions

for transgressing economic tabus were supernatural in nature,
with the exception of Easter Island where secular punishments
obtained.

The chiefs controlled communal production, and usually
craft production as well.

In contrast to Group I societies,

there was no direct chiefly control over the production of in­

dividual households, in the sense of directing planting and
inspecting to insure adequate cultivation.

A redistributive hierarchy of two levels with chiefs
acting as focal points of collection and redistribution was

characteristic of Group Ila societies.

With the possible ex­

ception of Uvea, where one author reports that chiefs could
take goods, and Institutionalized plundering by the Easter

Island birdman, the customary obligation to give surplus to

chiefs was not enforced by forceful confiscations.

Highest

chiefs were supported by contributions of food by others, and

usually did not engage in subsistence production.
Insignia of rank were marked but evidently not as
numerous as in Group I societies.

Control of socio-régulatory processes was exercised

by chiefs.

General statements by observers indicate that a

fair degree of arbitrary authority was wielded by chiefs.
Differences in status resulted in differential applications of

124

sanctions to wrongdoers.

Within this pattern the chief could

punish severely in certain cases.

But more stringent limita­

tions on the chiefs’ ability to inflict secular punishments on
offenders appear to have been present in Group Zia as opposed
This can be seen in the frequent suicides

to Group I societies.

among chiefs in Mangareva resulting from insults to them, in the
lack of the Mangaian chief’s ability to control raiding of his
taro plot as the expression of his people’s feelings of injus­

tice at the hands of one of the chief’s near kin, by retaliation
of the "weak" against the "strong" in Easter Island in certain

cases and by the general predominance of supernatural sanctions
in punishing breaches of the chief’s personal tabu.
A preference for intra-status marriage existed among
the chiefly levels.

In no case was this preference as rigidly

enforced as in Group I.
Ceremonial distinctions between statuses were fairly
well marked.

The personal tabu system surrounding the high

chiefs, contained less different types of prohibitions than
comparable systems in Group I societies.

It can be inferred

that the mana of chiefs was less than in Group I.

With the

exception of Mangareva, supernatural punishments followed tabu

breaches of this kind, as already mentioned.

Obeisance pos­

tures in the presence of chiefs were not marked except in Uvea
where one sat down in the high chief's presence, and in Man­

gala where high priest-chiefs were treated with reverence when

officiating at their temples.

practiced.

The carrying of chiefs was not

A courtesy language was present in Uvea, but it was

125

less elaborate than the Samoan or the Tongan.
Special life crises rites often obtained in chiefly

families.

The rites were not as elaborate nor as different in

form from commoners' rites as those of chiefs' in Group I

societies.

Mangareva appears to have been most stratified of

Group Zia societies.

There was a greater tendency toward the

development of a third status level in Mangareva; secular,
severe punishments were applied to those who refused to render
up surplus production to chiefs, and to those who broke the per
sonal tabu of the high chief.

Chiefly life crisis rites were

most elaborate in Mangareva of the Group Ila societies.

Some

aspects of the Uvean stratification system indicate that a
lower rating may be assigned to it than to Mangareva, Mangala

and Master Island, viz., the lack of any tendency toward a
third status level, a greater amount of prestige given to el­

ders as may be seen in the fact that insignia of chiefly rank

were also worn by elders, and small amount of control over
familial lands exerted by chiefs.

However respect behavior to­

ward chiefs was more marked in Uvea than in other Group Ila
Islands, and it may be that the indications of lower stratifica­

tion are a function of poor data.

Without more rigorous cri­

teria of the amount of stratification, no distinction can be
made here between Mangala and Easter Island as to which was more

stratified.

CHAPTER IV

THE AMOUNT OF STRATIFICATION IN GROUP lib SOCIETIES:
MARQUESAS, TIKOPIA, FUTUNA
In this chapter materials on the amount of stratifi­
cation in the Marquesas, Tikopia and Futuna are presented.

The outstanding characteristics of stratification in these
societies are summarized at the end of the chapter.

Marquesas^

Status Levels
The ranking system was relatively undifferentiated.
Each tribe was a socio-political unit.

The tribal high

chief, hakaiki, the tribal priest, the heads of a few high

ranking families and the households of these men formed the
chiefly level.

The remainder of the population comprised the

lower status level, mata-ei-nana.

Priests and craftsmen took

generally described here (Handy, op. clt., 30-31). It is note­

widespread on Ua Pou (Linton, 192$, 101).

the status assigned by birth.

The tribal priest was a close

relative, usually a younger brother, of the tribal chief.

Economic Differentials
Access to land and sea resources was of the over­
lapping stewardship type.

The chief was the supreme stew­

ard, and every household head was also a steward of his

lands.

It is doubtful if there was any intermediate level

of stewardship.

The chief had general tabuing powers.

lations of tabus on resources generally brought supernatural

However, this did not confer material advan­

the people in feasts sponsored by the chiefs.
Chiefs held the highest prerogatives regarding the
use of large canoes and deep sea fishing nets.

By this is

meant that their permission was obtained before using the

equipment, and a gift of a share of the produce was made to
the chief if used by someone else.
Large-scale productive activities, including the har­

vesting of the first breadfruit crop, were initiated by the
chief.

Craft production was largely stimulated by chiefs,

but anyone who could raise the necessary goods might engage

craftsmen.

Chiefs, themselves, were freed, at least to some

extent, from subsistence production, being supported by goods
reserved from tribal collections and by fields cultivated by

members of their households.

There was no direct supervision

of household production by the chief; each household head de­

termined the planting arrangements of the family.

As elsewhere, the paramount chief received surplus
food and other goods and employed them in general distribu­

tions and in subsidizing craft specialist production.

Tribal

storage pits of breadfruit were under the chief’s control.
The first breadfruit crop was siphoned off and stored in these
pits.

The occasions for redistribution of these and other

goods were similar in nature to those on other islands, including

Evidence from other

Evidence from the early accounts of Stewart, LisIansky,
Krusenstern, Mathias and others, indicates that chiefs had no

jurisdictional powers, and that the injured party took matters

in its own hands in the settlement of disputes and retaliation
21
of murder, theft and other wrongs.
Linton, however, states
that the chief interfered in disputes by indicating which side
he favored, at which the other usually withdrew.

But accord­

ing to Linton, this was all that the chief could do; he had no
power to compel the disputants to take his suggestion.
Sorcery, ridicule and retaliation in kind (as resteal­

ing) were methods by which injured parties wreaked vengeance

on offenders.

But status differences might be felt in differ­

ential application of sanctions.

For example, Mathias wrote

that an injured party could retrieve a stolen article and take
even more, but a chief might kill the thief.Handy reports

was usually punished supernaturally, sometimes physically.*2^
Chiefs tended to marry women of high status, and on
some northern islands, chiefs never married women of low rank.

Ceremonial Differentials

The person, and especially the head of the chief were
tabu, and on Ua Pou and Nuku Hiva, his house and personal
possessions were also inviolable.

But the mana-tabu complex

was not elaborate with respect to the chief, according to
Handy, especially in the Southern Marquesas,
The sanctity of male children in general ... was
merely exaggerated in the case of male chiefs.
The head being the most tapu of all parts of the
body, for chiefs in particular but likewise for
every first-born, many precautions were taken to
prevent anything profane from passing over it or
touching it. The persons of both chiefs and
chiefesses were regarded as tapu in Nuku Hiva and
Ua Pou, but, apparently, in the southern section
of the group chiefs and people concerned them­
selves little about it,27

The structure of the ranking system was simple in
Tikopla.

guished:

1) the four chiefs, arIki, and their immediate fam­

ilies, palto arIki; 2) the "commoners" or non-chiefs, the re­
mainder of the populace.

Economic Differentials
The significance of the chief in economic processes

has been well summarized by Firth:
The chief has been shown to be the most important
single human factor in the economic life of the
Tikopia. Not only does he play a part as a pro­
ducer within his immediate household, but by initi­
ative and example he gives direction to the produc­
tive work of the community; he is titular owner of
the most valuable property of the members of his
clan; he imposes far-reaching restrictions on produc­
tion and consumption and in many important activi­

in theory, all canoes, as well as nets, houses and

This is an exten­

As Firth describes the

in it [a good] will give way to his [the chief’s]."^2

An im­

portant distinction should be made between this ideological ex­
tension of stewardship in other things, which confers no actual

managerial power for the chief, and the stewardship over land.

It is possible that the "overall stewardship" phenomenon

occurred generally in Polynesia, but due to the fact that
sophisticated field workers appeared on the scene long after

cultural decay set in (which is not true with reference to

Tikopia), the custom was not fully reported.
The chiefs initiated communal productive activities

and subsidized much of the craft specialization.

A chief

could not force a non-chief to engage in communal labor.

"Even in the case of an enterprise sponsored by a chief, there
are sometimes too few workers."^ The chief’s people "are not

constrained to assist him in any absolute sense.A number

of examples may be found in the literature: once when the
people were late at the rethatching of his canoe house, the
chief merely complained;

On another occasion at the ritual of yam planting
at Kafika the Ariki Kafika arrived at the cultiva­
tion at the agreed time before sunrise, but nearly
all his party were so late that most of the plant­
ing was over before they came. He was very angry
and whooped loudly twice to show his disapproval.
They were ashamed, though they later joked with
him about it.45
There was no duly constituted agency to regulate pro­

duction in non-chiefly households.

The method by which a chief

instigated production in other households was to work furi­

ously on his own cultivation.

This would activate others to

43
^Firth, 1950, 142.

139

work on theirs, as the implication was that some ceremony in­
volving their contributions would soon be forthcoming.^8

As

implied, chiefs were not exempt from subsistence production.^
Chiefs usually did not carry things and were screened from the

heaviest manual labor in group activities.

As mentioned above, the chiefs were the focii of accu­

mulations and redistributions of goods.

The collections and

distributions were occasioned by events similar in nature to
those occurring elsewhere in Polynesia.

Goods were often pre­

sented to the ariki in the form of presents.

There was no secu­

lar sanction backing up the customary obligation to give goods
to the ariki, and no forceful confiscations by ariki:

Failure to assist the chief in production or to
give him customary tribute incurs his displeasure
and grumbling, but involves no direct material
sanctions against the defaulter, and is not backed
up by any concensus of popular approval.4°

With regard to consumption, the differences between
chief and non-chief were often of quantity rather than quality.

Prestige goods were held by most families and hence were not,
properly speaking, insignia of rank.

To some extent chiefs and their immediate rela­
tives require and get choicer food, build some­
what larger houses and have finer mats and orna­
ments. But in most of their ordinary meals they
eat exactly the same provisions as other people,
they wear barkcloth of the same quality and their
house furniture is of the same simplicity. The*

Social Differentials
Firth makes several general statements to the effect

that chiefs exerted considerable authority in the regulation

of interpersonal affairs.

He writes that chiefs held life and

death powers over their people ("in the last resort"), and
that their orders were implicitly obeyed.^0 He also reports
that authority was absolute only in theory,^1 and that "the

forms of executive authority are vaguely defined and come into
operation only to cope with major breaches of the law, such as
go
incest or direct insult to the chief.It should be noted
that in any important decision, the chief consulted with other

men in his "clan," especially with the "ritual elders."
The chief’s power to adjudicate disputes was limited.
Firth refers to the chief’s Intervention into disputes as "the

crystallization of public opinion."^

In quarrels within

families and most quarrels over land, his opinion was not a

the dispute threatened to become serious, the chief would send

the land.

enough to induce the rival claimants to compose their dif58

Ceremonial Differentials

Chiefs were representatives of the gods.

They had

power (mana) over the fertility of the land, played a central

role in kava rites and a number of religious ceremonies.

Un­

like other men the chiefs did not cook nor usually carry

burdens.

However: "The system of personal tapu is not highly

developed in Tikopia - there is nothing approaching the com­
plexity of Maori regulations, for example - and in its field

of operation it appears to be largely a function of the kin68
ship situation."
The personal tabu of the chiefs did not
differ in kind from the tabu surrounding the head men of other
households.

For example, the tabu on touching the chief’s

head applied primarily with reference to his sons, not to the
body of the population.

The difference between chiefs and

commoners in this respect was the strictness with which the
69
tabu was obeyed.
In ordinary affairs there were no elaborate forms

of respect paid to chiefs.

Ariki and commoners, "mingle

freely, exchanges take place between them on a basis of general

Futuna

Status Levels

The population can be divided into two status levels,
those with titles and those without.

In the upper, titled

level can be placed the district chiefs and their attendants,

the district distributor of food and the village chiefs.

The

rest of the population falls in the untitled level.
As councils, rather than individuals as such, were the
major forces in economic as well as social life, it may be

wise here to outline the composition of the types of councils

so that the economic and social roles played by the different

status levels may be clearly seen in the ensuing discussion.
The highest political bodies in Futuna were the two "district"

councils, each presided over by the paramount district chief,

and composed of some district functionaries and the village
Another council on the village level was presided

chiefs.

over by the village chief; it was theoretically open to all

male members of the village, though in practice, only elders
attended.

The village council was a discussion group; in some

district councils, only the presiding chief’s opinion was

heard.

The district council was thus composed of members of

the upper status level, while the village council was a repre­
sentative body containing both upper and lower status members

(Second Ed.); Williamson, 1924*

(with the upper status level member, the village chief, not
exerting exclusive authority).
Economic Differentials
Access to land resources was mainly vested in kinship

groups whether or not they were headed by chiefs.

Heads of

kinship units were the stewards of the kinship land, but they
had no power of alienation or allotment without consent of
the group.

Small amounts of cultivated land in the mountains

and large fish nets were held and used communally by villages.

The district chief, evidently after consulting with titled

members of the district, could place general tabus on food
73
stuffs.
According to Smith, "no one dared violate [the
tabu] on pain of the anger of the gods."?^
Each household head supervised production in his

household.

Village communal enterprises were usually insti­

gated by the village council.

Sometimes villages did tasks

assigned to them by the district council of chiefs.

Special­

ists were often employed in connection with village tasks and

thus were engaged by the council.

Canoe-builders were sup­

ported by the man who wanted the canoe built, not necessarily

Not even the paramount district chiefs were exempt

Village and district councils often played the roles

of collecting and redistributing surplus foods and other
goods for ceremonies, feeding workers engaged in communal

labor or labor for the community, and so forth.

Sometimes

the household heads, meeting in the village council, decided
the contributions they were to make; sometimes the village

chief ordered the allotments.

"Householders will complain if

the levy seems too large."

Food stuffs and manufactured

goods collected as fines (see below) were also used for such

distributions.

For many district-wide feasts, one village was

designated to provide most of the food.

The chiefs, acting

as individuals, exercised some claim on the resources of

their villages for use in feasts on certain life crises rites

involving the upper status, and for entertaining visitors,
,et£.

Chiefs probably could not force contributions for such

occasions.
As usual there was little differential consumption
of strategic resources.

Burrows found few insignia of rank,

but earlier sources mention crowns of white feathers, a coco­
nut leaf necklace, a white tapa bracelet and staffs as dis­

tinctive accoutrements of various types of chiefs.??
Social Differentials

General statements by early observers indicate that
chiefs had relatively limited power.

The power structure (as outlined above) is an indi­

The elders in the village council —— which assigned

In one

chief and a fellow villager for getting into a quarrel.

Whether such an event could occur under aboriginal circum­

We have found no direct information as to whether or

Ceremonial Differentials
Information on the personal tabu complex surrounding
the high chiefs is meagre.

The degree to which the prohibi­

tions were elaborated is not known.

In Burrows’ time, failure

to observe respect behaviors was punished supernaturally.
Schouten and Lemaire describe rather marked forms of paying
respect to superiors.

However, it was to the visiting Euro­

peans that most of the homage was shown; how much a part of
Futuna life these respect and obeisance behaviors were is not

known.

Removing one’s turban when passing a high chief is

practiced today.

Chiefs were not carried.

Ibid., 98.
88

89
90

S. Smith, op. cit., 38.
Burrows, op. cit., 91.

Cited by Burrows, ibid., 90-91.

Polite forms of

15%

speech, consisting of several repetitions of everything

said, were customary when addressing superiors.

(Such forms

were used by an individual whenever respect was being shown,
not exclusively for respect to chiefs.)

Status was clearly

demarcated in the ceremonial kava circles, in which only chiefs
participated, along with appointed ceremonial attendants, mua.

Some of life crisis rites in chiefly families were
more ceremoniously celebrated than in other families.

Although

we could find no evidence of distinctive chiefly ceremonies

at birth or puberty, there was a special death ceremony and
special mourning rituals for the paramount chief.

As already

implied, marriage also may have been more formally celebrated

among chiefs than among others.

Summary

Group lib societies, Marquesas, Tikopia and Futuna,

had stratification systems containing two structural levels.
There was no tendency to form a third level, and the upper
level had few members compared to Group I and Ila societies.
The highest rights of access to resources were held

by the chief in the Marquesas and Tikopia.

In Futuna the pre­

rogatives to manage land were divided between the village chief
and the elders of the community.

Prerogatives did not usually

include the right to dispossess underlings.

High chiefs gen­

erally had the power to place widespread tabus.

The sanctions

for transgressing economic tabus were usually supernatural.

153

Some secular punishments obtained in the Marquesas,

The chiefs controlled communal production in the Mar­
quesas and Tikopia.

In Futuna, some communal production was

controlled by chiefs, some by the village council of a chief

and elders.

Craft production might be supported by anyone,

although it is likely that chiefs most often engaged special­

ists.

There was no direct chiefly control over production of

individual households.
The chiefs were focli of large-scale collections and

redistributions of goods.

The chiefs did not forcefully con­

fiscate the goods of others.

Chiefs were not freed from sub­

sistence activities, with the exception of Marquesan chiefs.

Insignia of status were few.

In Tikopia, quantity rather than

quality of valued possessions distinguished chiefs.

General statements by both early and late observers
indicate that the chiefs1 powers in interpersonal affairs

were limited.

The chiefs usually held informal consultations

with elders of the community before important decisions were

reached.
in

In Futuna, these consultations were institutionalized

the form of a council, and the village elders exerted a

good deal of authority.

Differences in status sometimes con­

ferred the right to punish offenders severely.

But violent

secular punishments inflicted by chiefs appear to have been
very rare, perhaps most frequent in the Marquesas.

Super­

natural sanctions applied to breaches of the chief’s personal

tabu.

Indeed, supernatural sanctions were the predominate

sanctions throughout the Group lib societies.

Violence in any

154

quarter of the society was infrequent.
A slight preference for chiefly marriages existed,
but standing was not lowered by marrying a non-chief.

In

Tikopia, at least, marriages of chiefs with non-chiefs were
the rule.
The personal tabu system of the high chiefs was usu­

ally unelaborate.

The prohibitions regarding chiefs were

similar to those which surrounded the head of any household

in Tikopia and the Marquesas.

In Tikopia, in fact, the tabu

of the chief was primarily respected in his own household —

whereas his son could not touch the ariki’s head, others could.

With the possible exception of Futuna, obeisance posturing and
elaborate forms of paying respect were nil.
Life crisis rites in chiefly families were often more

elaborate than in non-chiefly families.

Elaborate rights,

however, were not restricted to chiefs ; others could indulge
by amassing the goods requisite for the ceremonies.

The fact

that other families might be able to accumulate goods for cere­

monies comparable to the chiefly rites, and, as well, for sub­
sidizing craftsmen, indicates that the chief’s redistributive

functions were not much greater than those of some non-chiefs.

Differences by status in the life crisis rites were more in
the nature of differences in degree rather than form.
*

»

*

It is difficult to rate Group Ila societies vis a vis
each other on a gradient of stratification.

In one criterion

of stratification one society appears most stratified, in

155

another criterion, a different group is more stratified.

However, the important role played by the elders in Futuna
in the regulation of economic and social affairs indicates
that Futuna was the least stratified in Group Ila.

Some

aspects of the Marquesan stratification system appear to place

it on a higher level than Tlkopia: the occasional use of secu­

lar punishments for violations of the chief's personal and
economic tabus and the freeing of Marquesan chiefs from at

least some subsistence production.

In the northern Marquesas

one also finds a more rigid custom of intra-chiefly marriage,

and more elaborate personal tabus concerning chiefs.

CHAPTER V
THE AMOUNT OF STRATIFICATION IN GROUP III SOCIETIES:
PUKAPUKA, ONTONG JAVA, TOKELAU

Materials on the stratification systems of Pukapuka,
Ontong Java and Tokelau, are presented in this chapter.

A

summary of the outstanding characteristics of stratification
in this group of islands appears at the end of the

chapter.

Pukapuka1
Status Levels

The structure of the stratification system may be
divided into two levels:

1) the chiefs, aliki and sub­

chiefs, langatila, who were heads and sub-heads of kinship
groups and villages ; and 2) the remainder of the population.

The Beagleholes note, and the evidence bears them out, that

this status system was not, in the main, functionally re­
lated to the direction and control of the economic and regu­

latory life.

Rather the concentration of power rested with

the tupele, the old grey haired members of the Island society.

Entrance into the tupele was open to all male elders in

Pukapuka.

This group was especially important in the regu­

lation of island affairs, while at the village level, almost
all adults participated in regulatory functions.

In both

cases, the result is a society with an egalitarian emphasis.

The relative importance of age distinctions and ordinary kin­
ship groups, as opposed to the status levels, in community

life will be seen in the ensuing discussion.

Economic Differentials
There was no island-wide stewardship over land

exercised by the paramount chief.

Certain types of kinship

groups controlled certain types of land, with head of the

group being the trustee of his family's land.^

often belonged to the lower status level.

These heads

Each village had

prior rights to the reefs and fishing grounds off its shore.
The village chief, apparently, was not conceived of as having

any supreme control over fishing resources.

Each village also

controlled communal taro beds and reserve lands.

The communal

taro beds were divided into three sections : that of the men,
the women and the children. As a result, each household held
an amount proportionate to its size.^ Communal taro beds and

reserve lands of each village were watched over by guards who
prevented trespass, placed tabus on the land, reported thefts,

and the like.

The guards were either composed of all adult

3Ibld., 43.
^Ibld., 41.

158

males of the village, twenty-five serving at a time, or in

one village, by elders.

Decisions regarding the use of the

communal lands were made at village meetings in which the

elders and guards did the discussing.

Such objects as canoes

were controlled by kinship groups with the eldest male as
nominal owner; while certain fish wlers were controlled by
the village as a whole.

Most production was carried on and regulated within
households.

Certain activities were carried out on a com­

munal, village or island-wide scale or by age groups in the

community.'"

Deep-sea fishing in fleets was done by an age

group of young adult males under the direction of the tupele.
The village guards also fished in a group at the end of their

term, organized the care of village taro beds, and the prepar­
ation of inter-village contests in which they allotted tasks.
When the reserve lands accumulated an abundant supply of food,
the tabus on them were lifted and a communal gathering of
supplies was held under the direction of the guards.

The

decision for such and other actions involving village produc­
tion rested with the village council of chiefs and adult males.

Specialists were engaged and supported by both chiefs and

non-chiefs.

Chiefs were not supported by any kinship group

other than their own; that is, they did not receive "tribute"

from other households.

6

Whether or not they worked on sub­

sistence themselves is not known.
A relatively high frequency of distributive activi­

ties were reciprocal exchanges between kinship groups and thus
7
did not involve an intermediate regulator. Some redistribu­
tive activities also took place.

On the village level these

were arranged by the councils of the chiefs and adult males,

each household contributing according to its ability.

For

island-wide collections and redistributions, the chiefs and
tupele handled the arrangements, deciding on village contri­

redistribution were not, in the main, members of the upper
status level.

This can be further documented by consideration

of the manner in which councils operated (see below).

The old men (tupele) were supported by gifts of taro
from their own lineages and deep-sea fish which the young men
contributed.

On the whole, there is little evidence of dif­

ferential consumption of strategic resources between the dif­

ferent non-egalitarian status levels.

Chiefs might be given

certain portions of deep-sea fish as emblematic of their
status, but it is only the group of old men, the tupele, who

would primarily have deep-sea fish while other members of the

population had none (this may be found also in egalitarian
societies as the Australian aborigines).

The chiefs had bath­

ing and resting places reserved for their use, but differential

consumption of non-strategic goods serving as insignia of

The size of the chiefs’

Valued luxury

Social Differentials

General statements by the Beagleholes indicate a
limited development of power differentials by (inherited)
9
status in Pukapuka.
They found Pukapuka to have been more
egalitarian than certain (mainly Samoa and Tonga) western

Polynesian islands.^
The adult males, the chiefs, the sub-chiefs and the

elders were organized into a number of councils which regu­
lated interpersonal affairs.

There were village meetings at­

tended by all adult males including chiefs and sub-chiefs,
meetings of the tupele and meetings of the chiefs.

The vil­

lage councils discussed village affairs, settled intra-village
disputes (see below) and, as we have seen, matters concerning
the reserves.

The tupele discussed island affairs, while

elders of each village (along with the guards) discussed the
matters concerning the village taro beds.

The chiefs dis­

cussed island, and inter-kin group ("lineage”) matters.

The interworkings of these councils, as far as was

advisory and executive officers, but the real bal­
ance of power, in theory at any rate, seems to
have been with the old men. ... When the high chief
or his executive ... was an outstanding personality,
he might assume for himself many of the powers that
ordinarily lay with the old men; these powers would
revert to the old men on the chief’s death.13

Physical punishment was infrequently used as sanction

in Pukapukan justice."

"Sanctions invoked to deal with most

antisocial conduct were not secular, but depended on punish­
ment, usually sickness, sent to the wrongdoer by the gods."1^

Thieving from the reserves was usually so punished, as was
homicide.

During periods of severe food shortage, however, the
guards were empowered to kill those caught thieving from the
reserves; and if the gods delayed too long in punishing will­
ful homicide, group action might ensue and the murderer be
killed.1^

There was also a system of fining thieves caught in
the reserves by the guards.

This may have an aboriginal basis,

but certainly the coercive authority of the guards has been
increased through French influence.^8 Theft from an individual

might be settled by counter-theft, atonement by relatives of
the thief, or by a wrestling match. Insult by slander could
17
also be settled through wrestling.
Insult to a chief was

punished by sickness.

than another party do not appear in the evidence.

The chief entered his house through a special door.

boyhood.

He ate with his family, but his old clothes and mats were

his body.
Although there was no developed chief’s language,
there were certain forms of respect paid to chiefs.

A

special salutation was used in greeting a chief or his family;

P "I

when the chief finished bathing he was carried out of the

water; he was given the right of way on paths.

The chief had

certain priestly functions, he was ceremonially installed; he

had a ’sacred maid’ who remained a virgin all her life, and was
symbolic of the chief’s and his lineage’s status.

He also had

an attendant, toa, who was his executor and speaker.

Crisis rites were sometimes more elaborate, and more
rarely, different in form in chiefly as opposed to non-chiefly

families.

Ontong Java^

Status Levels

The system of statuses was simple.

Each of the two

tribe g existing on the atoll had a number of leaders, maakua,
who were the oldest male members of particular kinship groups

(of a patrilocally extended family nature) in which the title
passed.

The maakua were ranked among themselves ; three of the

eight in the Luangiua tribe were major maakua and appointed

Economic Differentials

Access to most of the land and sea resources was
controlled by kinship groups.

Managership over lands of this

category did not go beyond the kin group; the maakua were not

supreme stewards of land.

Hogbin differentiates "poor families"

from "wealthy families" in that poor families only held land
on which the village of the tribe was located, while wealthy

families had outlying islets as well.

How this land differen­

tial arose is difficult to envision from Hogbin’s materials,

but it seems possible that it is related to the de-population
which had occurred by Hogbin’s time, and the reduction and re­

grouping of joint families and villages which may also have
27
occurred.
The village in which the tribe centered communally
held two or three nearby islets as reserve lands.

occurring mainly in connection with the annual tribal reli­

At these ceremonies, the maakua

At times the term

’redistribution 1 can hardly be applied here, for although coco­

picked, after the maakua and guards took a share of each pile. 32
From distributions of this kind, and from offerings
to the tribal gods made at the sanga, the maakua derived some
of their subsistence.

However, the maakua probably did not

receive sufficient food during the sanga to last all year, and

hence it is likely that maakua engaged in productive activities
themselves.

There was very little differential consumption by the

different statuses.

Ordinarily maakua were not distinguished

by dress or other insignia of rank from others.

During tribal

ceremonies, however, the maakua wore regalia consisting of a
pandanus leaf necklace, a fan, a mat and a staff.

Punishment for theft of reserve foods, in which the

35
organized sanction in Ontong Java, according to Hogbin.

Otherwise punishments were brought about supernaturally, or
were inflicted through private retaliation by kin groups.

Murder within the patrilineal kin groups (usually resulting
from adultery), as well as incest and other "sins," were
supernaturally punished.^

In the course of private retalia­

tions , sometimes status difference was

manifest.

a maakua -- later the maakua made a compensation of goods ;
fights occurred between families of equal strength over adul­

Ceremonial Differentials
There was some development of social-ceremonial dif­

ferences between maakua and the remainder of the populacee

The maakua were in fact priests, and during the tribal cere­
monies they were more or less sacred and inviolable.

The

fertility of the land was vested in them; when a maakua became
ill, he was expected to commit suicide in order not to endanger

this fertility.

When a maakua died, the entire tribe gath­

ered for the death rites.

A certain social ceremonial pres­

tige, however, was held by the heads of all joint families

as representatives of the family founder.

The supreme sacred­

ness of the maakua accrued to them only when wearing their

regalia, i.e., during tribal ceremonies, at other times they
were no more sacred than any other headman.

Tokelau^"
Status Levels
There were two status levels.

The high chief and the

high priest (usually the latter’s sister’s son) on each atoll
formed the upper ; the remainder of the population formed the
lower.

The chieftainship was bestowed on the oldest man of

the four kin groups which were eligible to hold the title.

Newell, as cited by MacGregor, was of the opinion that the high
chief and high priest offices were once combined and later

separated; MacGregor, however, felt that dual division may
have existed throughout Tokelauan history.4^

The village council of elders was the major means of
regulation, integration and control on each atoll.

The chief

did not usually sit on the council, but rather was represented

by the oldest member of the council, who acted as a liaison
between the chief and the elders.
Economic Differentials

The high chief does not seem in any way to have been
the supreme steward of land and sea.

Most of the strategic

resources were controlled by kinship groups.

If a section of

discussed the reallotment of the group’s land, the council hear­
ing all claimants from the village.

Ceremonial Differentials
The greatest differential in the functional aspects

of stratification occurs in the social-ceremonial santifi-

cation of status, although by no means do the differences
here compare with elaborate developments in some of the other
Polynesian islands.

The paramount chief was the direct descen­

dant of the gods; his body was sacred and inviolable; he,

"lived apart from the daily activities of the community at­
tended by his family and household.The high chief occupied

the position of honor at feasts and his death rites were more

A ’chief’s language’ and special
