Summary

Group III societies, Pukapuka, Ontong Java and Tokelau
were relatively unstratified from a structural point of view.

Two status levels, the upper containing very few members, oc­

curred.

Elders and the heads of kinship groups, rather than

chiefs, exercised the greatest prerogatives and played the

dominant roles in socio-economic and political affairs.

Access to resources was mainly vested in the heads of
kinship groups.

Wide powers of stewardship were not held by

chiefs.

In each Group III society there were reserve communal

lands.

These were managed by the group of elders in Tokelau

and Pukapuka, and by the maakua in Ontong Java.

The most gen­

eral tabuing powers were also held by elders in Tokelau and
Pukapuka; on Ontong Java, the methods of laying tabus are not
known.

Violation of tabus on the use of reserve and other

lands were usually supernaturally punished, although in times
of scarcity, death might be inflicted. Death punishments were

carried out by a group designated from the community at large
on Pukapuka and Ontong Java (the guards); who exercised the
authority on Tokelau is not known.

Communal production was

stimulated primarily by the elders in Pukapuka and Tokelau,

by the maakua in Ontong Java.

Craft production was supported

177

by both chiefs and non-chiefs, evidently indiscriminately.

No higher authority directly supervised production of the in­
dividual families, that being done by the family head.

The

Tokelauan chief evidently did not produce an appreciable amount

of the goods he consumed, while the maakua of Ontong Java
evidently did.

Materials on this point from Pukapuka are not

specific.

The paucity of chiefly control over communal and
craft production is correlated with a relatively small role
in the regulation of distribution, especially redistribution.

Most large-scale collections and redistributions of goods
(such occur mainly in Tokelau and Pukapuka) were directed by

elders or males of the group as a whole.

The relatively large

proportion of reciprocal, non-intermediated exchanges between

kin groups, as opposed to redistributions, may be correlated

further with the slight degree of chiefly regulation of dis­
tribution.

Some foods were reserved for chiefs.

The consumption

of non-subsistance goods serving as insignia of rank was lim­

ited to the ceremonial regalia worn by the Ontong Java maakua
and the Tokelauan chief’s coconut wreath.

General statements on the power structure of all three

societies indicate that chiefs did not exercise arbitrary

authority, and on Pukapuka and Tokelau, at least, the egali­

tarian council of elders was the most powerful political group.

Collective kinship retaliations and responsibilities were the
rule in administering punishments.

In a few cases from Ontong

178

Java status differences were manifest in the ability of

"strong" parties to inflict strong punishments; but, gener­

ally chiefs did not administer more rigorous punishments than

others in defense of their rights.

In the three societies

there was a strong dependence on supernatural sanctions.
A tendency toward marriage between parties having
prestige, if not between chiefly families, is indicated.

Ceremonial tabus surrounding chiefs were fairly well

developed on Tokelau and Pukapuka where the chief’s body was
inviolable.

The sacredness of Ontong Java maskua pertained

only when they were officiating at tribal ceremonies.

Elabora­

tion of forms of paying respect is most marked in Pukapuka
among Group III societies.

Methods of honoring the chief in

Pukapuka compare favorably with those in Group lib societies.

Such customs were less pronounced on Ontong Java and Tokelau.

Elaboration in chiefly life crisis rites was not highly
developed.

Special ceremonial forms occurred only in connec-

tion with death of the chief.
%

*

The criteria of stratification are not fine enough to
permit a ranking within Group III of amount of stratification.

In some characteristics, one society appears most stratified,
in other characteristics, another society.

The three may be

considered equally stratified — or egalitarian.

179

CHAPTER VI

STRATIFICATION AND PRODUCTIVITY
The materials presented in Chapters II-V illustrate

the gradient of stratification in Polynesia.

It has been

postulated that the amount of stratification in Polynesian
societies is an adaptive feature related to increasing effi­
ciency of technological-environmental interaction, or for
short, to productivity.

It is our task in this chapter to

classify Polynesian cultures on the basis of productivity and

to relate such a scheme to the gradient in the amount of
stratification.

Measuring Productivity
Not all cultures are equally well adapted to the en­
vironments in which they are situated.

Some cultures ex­

ploit the external world more efficiently than others.

The

ability of a culture to derive need-serving goods from the ex­

ternal world may be called the productivity of the culture.
As the exploitation of nature for energy in the form of food

is the type of exploitation which all cultures must engage in,
productivity is best understood, if cross-cultural comparisons

are to be made, as ability to produce food.

Two types of

phenomena are interacting in any cultural situation to sustain
the human population: the natural environment and the tools

and techniques used in exploiting it, or environment and

180

technology.

To derive indices of productivity for Polynesian

cultures, it is necessary to estimate, in some way, the effi­
ciency of techno-environmental interaction in food production.

There are essentially two types of methods by which

one might arrive at an estimate of efficiency of exploitation.
Firstly by developing some measure which does not necessarily

deal with environment and technology per se—with formal
qualities or properties as irrigation, altitude, horticulture,

etc.—but rather which measures some result of productivity,
as in the form of input-output ratios.

For example, Leslie

White has hypothesized that, other factors held constant, the

amount of need-serving goods and services produced increases

as, "the amount of energy harnessed per capita per year is

increased, or as the efficiency of the instrumental means of
putting the energy to work is increased.Another such meas­
ure, hinted at by White, would be (other factors constant):

the number of man-hours spent in food-getting per capita per
o
year in an economically autonomous group.
This last would be
an input-output ratio based upon the major exploitative ac­

tivity, food-getting.

Neither of these measures can be applied

to the available data on Polynesia, as observers were not
particularly interested in measuring productivity.

But the

concept of productivity, and White's measures as well, have

White, 1949, 368-369# emphasis White’s.
P

White, 1949# 378. Cf. L. A. White, ’’Energy and the
Evolution of Culture,” Amer. Anthrop., 45# 335-356# 1943»

131

one implication that may be of considerable value in develop­
ing a further measure that is applicable.

That is that, other

factors constant, the greater (measured in energy or man-hours)
the productivity, the greater would be the surplus of subsis­
tence goods produced by those engaged in food production.
It may be argued that this statement, as productivity
increases surplus (of the producer) increases, is not neces­

sarily valid—that an increase in productivity might be accom­
panied by a rise in the level of the producer’s wants, and

hence that the surplus would remain constant, even diminish.
From the point of view of the problem at hand, two points may

be made against this argument.

First, as the cultures com­

pared here are closely historically related and have more or
less the same foods and types of distributional systems, it is
unlikely that a serious discrepancy in producers’ wants would

arise from society to society.

Second, and more significant,

the surpluses which are being considered are food surpluses,

and any discrepancy in wants that might arise would a priori
be small; in contrast to many things, the human body can con­

sume just so much food, or just so little--the range is rela­
tively small.

The statement that surplus increases as pro­

ductivity increases may be accepted, especially for Polynesia.
•Surplus’ may be considered in two different senses,

each of which, it will be found, has value in leading to a
measure of productivity applicable to Polynesia.

One type may

be designated ’immediate surplus,’ Si, and refers to the abil­

ity of food producers to acquire, in a single exploitative

182

activity, an amount of food beyond their immediate consumption

needs.

Thus in harvesting a domesticated crop, a large Si may

be produced--much larger, for example, than killing a small
mammal.

Si does not necessarily imply that the producer, in

time, may not be able to consume all that he produces.

A sur­

plus of a second type, Sc, is that food which the producer has

left over after a full cycle, as a year, of production.

The

producer has been able to produce more than he can consume,
and thus food is produced to support other people.

Si and Sc

are not mutually exclusive; a rise in Si may produce Sc or a

rise in Sc.
Polynesian cultures are particularly susceptible to
measures of productivity based on ability to produce Si.

For

it is precisely those foods produced beyond the immediate

needs of the producers which were collected by chiefs and then

distributed in the redistributional system operating in con­
junction with religious feasts and chiefly life crisis rites,

famine distributions, accompanying communal labor, and so forth.

With the exception of those foods siphoned off by the chiefs
to support themselves and craftsmen, the food was redistributed
among the producers.

Therefore, other things constant, a

fairly accurate indication of the ability to produce Si may
be gained by considering the greatest number of people encom­

passed in a single redistributive food network, and the fre­
quency per annum of overall redistribution in such a network.
It should be noted immediately that the size of the unit will

not necessarily be equivalent to an entire island or group,

18,5

but to the number of people in the average economy on any
island or group.

Polynesian cultures are compared below on

this basis.

Sc, the ability of a producer to produce more food
than he needs during a complete production cycle, is also a

concept which lends itself to measures of productivity.

Other

things constant, the greater the Sc, the greater the number

of people in the society divorced from food production.

The

greater the Sc, in other words, the greater the number of
specialized, non-food producers.

Polynesian societies can be

compared on the basis of the degree to which they contain a

division of labor between food producers and non-food produ­

cers.

However, as the data contained in the ethnographic

sources on the division of labor is often ambiguous and nonquantitative, only a rough idea of the degree of specializa­

tion can be discerned.

Consequently less reliance should be

placed on this factor than on the size of the largest redis­
tributive unit.

Nevertheless, such estimates as can be made

will be used as a check on the productivity measure derived
from the size of the redistributive unit.

The second major method for estimating productivity,
as opposed to these more or less "operational" measures,

would be one in which the environmental and technological con­
ditions on each island are considered in light of their formal

characteristics.

Just as a sample : other factors being con­

stant, horticultural activities are usually more productive

than collecting activities, as due to the techniques involved,

134

the reproduction and distribution of the energy source (other
forms of life) is more controlled; hence, the number of man
hours spent per capita in food getting--to relate this to an

operational measure--would be less in horticultural groups than
in non-horticultural groups.

A number of similar features of

the environmental-technological situations can be analyzed in
terms of their productivity implications, and thus by consid­

erations of the specific technologies and environments, a
further check can be made on productivity classifications ob­

tained through the use of operational measures.

Each of the procedures outlined will now be applied to

the available data on the Polynesian islands whose stratifica­

tion systems have been described.
The Network of Redistribution

It has been posited that a measure of productivity

applicable to Polynesian societies is, other things constant,
the number of people encompassed in the largest single redis­

tributive food network and the frequency per annum of overall
redistribution in such a network.

However, if this measure is

to be used cross-culturally, the requirement that other things
be constant must be met.

Specifically, within each society

each individual consumer must receive a proportionate amount
or Mbit” in the redistribution, and the average bit per con­
sumer per redistribution should be equivalent from society to
society.

If, in some societies some people get larger bits

than others on some occasions, and others get larger bits on

185

other occasions, then the range of redistribution may expand
or contract irrespective of Si productivity.

And, if in one

society the customary bit is double that of another society,

then the size of the distributive network will, again, not re­
flect Si productivity.

With reference to the question of proportionate bits,

in many groups some of the food collected by chiefs is used
to support themselves and specialists whom they subsidize, and

the remainder of the food (as will shortly be documented) is
given out in equal or proportionate amounts to the remainder
of the group (the food producers).

The amount of food taken

out by chiefs, however, does not disproportionately affect the

size of the redistributive network.

If it did one would ex­

pect to find that the size of the network decreases as the num­

ber of chiefs divorced from production, the number of special­
ists and the amount of chiefly controlled "public" enterprise,
increases.

In actuality, the opposite pertains in Polynesia.

The size of the redistributive network, the number of special­
ists, the number of chiefs divorced from production and the
amount of public enterprise, increase correspondingly.Dis­

regarding then any amount taken out by chiefs, is the remainder
of the food equivalently distributed in large-scale redis­
tributive activities in Polynesia?

Where such data are

^The data verifying this point may be found as fol­
lows in this work: amount of chiefly segregation from produc­
tion, Chapters Il-V, passim; size of redistributive network,
Table II; number of specialists in the next section of this
chapter; public enterprise in the Appendix.

186

available, the answer is unequivocally affirmative.

In

Hawaii, for example, Malo describes the method of redistri­
bution at the great annual Makahiki ceremony, where in, after

a proportion of the large amount of food and goods had been
held back by the paramount chief, "portions were assigned to

the remaining chiefs and to the different military companies

To the more important chiefs, with many followers was given a
large portion; to the lesser chiefs, with fewer collowers, a
smaller portion."^ Melville’s description of a large fish
distribution in the Marquesas is comparable.

After some fish

had been held back, "the remainder was divided into numerous
small packages, which were immediately dispatched in every

direction to the remotest part of the valley.

Arrived at their

destination, they were in turn portioned out, and equally dis­

tributed among the various houses of each particular dis­

A similar pattern of fish distribution prevailed

trict."

throughout the Cook islands : "The head fisherman took the large
fish of a similar kind and laid out one for each family.

He

then added fish in turn to each heap until the pile was ex­

hausted.

Chiefly families were given priority in distribution

of the better or larger fish, and a family of greater numerical
numbers [sic] might be given a few extra.For Tonga, Mari­
ner describes a great feast following the lifting of a tabu
^Malo, 1951, 143.

^Melville, 1931, 234.
Buck, 1944, 209.

187

placed by the chief of Vavau in which yams, hogs, and other

foods and goods were distributed: "The hogs were disposed of
in a like manner [i.e., similar to the disposal of yams]; the

greatest quantity to the greatest chiefs, who share them out

to the chiefs immediately below them in rank, and these again
to their dependents, till every man in the island gets at least
a mouthful of pork and yam."?

In Pukapuka, each man, woman

and child was entitled to a share of a given proportion in vilp
lage food divisions.
Examples might be multiplied to show

that, as far as the evidence goes, proportionate bit redistri­

bution is a general phenomenon within Polynesian groups.
It is more difficult to document that the average bit

per consumer per redistribution is equivalent from society to
society, as there are simply no figures available.

It may be

pointed out, although certainly not as decisive evidence, that

in all the cultures foods of the same type are distributed in
approximately the same manner on the same sorts of occasions.

Furthermore, the tendency, at least, toward equal bits from
society to society may be inferred from the fact that in no

case in Polynesia was redistribution practiced for political
expansionist purposes; nowhere, in other words, were the bits
made smaller such that a chief might distribute less food per

capita to more people and thus enlarge the number of his sup­
porters .

In the following chapters it is clearly brought out

^Mariner, 1827, i, 120.
g
E. and P. Beaglehole, 1938, 36, et passim.

188

that the Polynesian socio-political organizations are basically

kin organizations, that the scope of distribution is implicitly

defined by the kin relations of the chief and/or those of his
descent group.

For one is chief by virtue of being a member of

certain status in a kin group or line of descent, which line

stands in a traditional and/or genealogical relation to all
other comparable groups.

It is that relation which determines

the range of distribution.

As is documented in the following

chapters, political expansion is brought about through con­
quest warfare—not through enlarging the scope of redistribu­
tion.

The weight of the evidence, therefore, supports the in­

ference that the customary bits received in redistribution were

comparable from island to island.

But since the evidence is

not conclusive, attempts to measure productivity by the scope

of the redistributive network must be, and shall be, checked
against measures of productivity derived by other means.

Table II classifies, in synoptic form the productivity
of the Polynesian cultures considered according to the size
and frequency of use of the largest single redistributive net­

work of food.

Unfortunately no accurate census materials are

available for the period at the commencement of European con­

tact.

The figures listed for size of the redistributive net­

work vary considerably in accuracy.

Where the redistributive

unit was a smaller segment than an island or a group of

islands, the number of people in such a unit may not have been

recorded ethnographically.

In that case, the population of the

189

unit is computed by dividing the total population of the island
or group by the number of autonomous redistributive segments--

hence an average is obtained.

In such cases, when possible,

some estimate of the range of deviation from the mean is made,

based on the available evidence.

The frequency of redistribu­

tion is also relevant, for if the size of the network is com­

parable , that society in which the network is used most fre­
quently is most productive.

The data do not provide quantifi­

cation of frequency of overall redistribution; sometimes the

data do not allow any conclusive statement regarding frequency.
Three categories of frequency may be defined to each of which

an arbitrary factor may be assigned, multiplication of the
size of the redistributive unit by which will yield an esti­

mate of productivity.

The frequency of overall redistributions

is considered "regular” and assigned a factor value of three

(3), If in addition to annual collections and redistributions,
surplus food also passes up and down the redistributive hier­

archy at various times during the year.

If only a few or less

^uinual redistributions take place, the frequency may be con­
sidered "sporadic,” and a factor of two (2) assigned.

If the

interval between overall redistributions of surplus food is
greater than a year, the frequency is "rare” and a factor of

one (1) is given.

In two cases, Tahiti and Samoa, the evidence

does not permit exact specification of the size of the network

of redistribution.

It is difficult to say whether overall re­

distributions in Tahiti were confined to units of the order of
the twenty-odd districts, of which Papara would be an example,

A comparison of Table I (p. 36

) and Table II shows

that a marked degree of correspondence obtains between the
amount of stratification and the productivity of the society,

as measured by the size of the largest distributive network
and the frequency of distribution therein.

Productivity on

those islands containing the most stratified societies,

Group I (Hawaii, Tonga, the Societies and Samoa), does not
overlap at all with the productivity of Group Ila societies

(Mangareva, Mangala, Easter and Uvea).

Group Ila seems to

grade very well into Group lib (Marquesas, Tlkopia and Futuna),
but lack of specific information on the frequency of overall
redistribution on Uvea and Mangala prohibits conclusive veri­

fication of this point.

With the exception of Ontong Java,

where the size of the redistributive unit is in doubt, there
is a productivity break between Group lib and Group III (Ontong

195

Java, Tokelau) societies.
Within Group I, Hawaii was rated as most stratified,

Mangareva was estimated

a rating duplicated in productivity.

as the most stratified of Group Ila societies and has also

the highest productivity in this Group.

Uvea was considered

to be less stratified than other Group Ila societies, but

whether Uvean productivity is lowest of this Group cannot be
ascertained.

Within Group lib, Marquesan society was rated

as most stratified, Futunan least.

By the measure used here,

the Marquesan productivity is highest in Group lib, but Futuna

is not much lower, and does not rank below Tikopia of this

Group.

As the Ontong Java data are incomplete, it is diffi­

cult to ascertain whether the productivity on this island
was actually highest among Group III.

The other Group III

societies, Pukapuka and Tokelau, are very close together
produc ulvity-wise, as they seem to be also in amount of strat­
ification.

In short, the amount of stratification, in terms of

the major groupings outlined in Chapters II-V, is decidedly

correlated with productivity measured in terms of the size
and frequency of use of the redistributive network.

With one

or two exceptions, finer discriminations in amount of strati­

fication are paralleled by minor variations in productivity.
However, it may be wise to refrain from building any conclu­
sions on these results until productivity be checked by the

other methods that have been proposed.

196

Specialization of Labor

The degree to which there exists a specialization

of labor in the social system, it has been postulated, should

serve as an index of productivity.

It was reasoned that the

greater the surplus produced, the more people who would be
divorced from subsistence production and who would be able to

specialize in other types of production or services (other
things equal).

To measure productivity by specialization of

labor then, it would be necessary to know not only how many

kinds of specialists in the society, but also, what percentage
of the population are specialists and whether specializations

were full-time or part-time.

The data on specialization in

Polynesia is pitifully inadequate in this respect.

It is not

only often difficult to determine the number of different
kinds of specialists in the various groups, but usually next

to impossible to determine what percentage of the population

engaged in specialized activities and whether they were em­
ployed full time or not.
the task easy.

Modern ethnographers have not made

The usual Polynesian word for "expert" or

"specialist" is some variant of tohunga.

However, the mean­

ing of the term ranges down to, * one who does a particular
task well.'

Sometimes the ethnographer has gathered a list of

the occupations at which a person may be adept and published
that list as a list of specializations.

For example, Handy

lists twenty-seven different specializations in the Marquesas.^3

Polynesian islands.

Tikopia:

"Such specialization as exists is the

Firth’s description of the tufunga

is worth quoting in full as it probably clarifies the source
of confusion in the Marquesan and other data:

The term tufunga is a generic title for a skilled
craftsman and has attached to it a number of ex­
planatory expressions indicating proficiency in
tattooing, plaiting of sinnet, net-making, working
of bowls or building of canoes. Here again there
is no specific quantitative point of achievement
at which the title may be attained and it is a mat­
ter of degree of activity and quality of the pro­
duct rather than of specialization in the craft.
But there is a difference to be observed. Whereas
every man engages to some extent in agriculture,
fishing, sinnet-plaiting, dancing and minor wood­
work , only a few are tatooers and canoe architects,
though not exclusively. Women are also admitted
to have special proficiency in crafts, particularly
in the plaiting of mats. But the term tufunga is
never applied to a woman.
The proportion of specialists is obviously a difficult mat­

ter to determine and information is lacking on that question,

Technology and Environment
A final check on the productivity indices hereto­
fore derived may be made by a consideration of exploitative

techniques and environmental conditions in the islands with
which we are concerned.

Here formal characteristics of the

techno-environmental situation are being considered.

As a

basis for evaluating the productivity implications of the
qualitative aspects of techno-environmental situations, cer­

tain principles can be adduced.
can be made:

First two major deductions

(1) other factors, primarily environment, held

constant, productivity will vary as the techniques of exploi­

tation vary; (2) the opposite, technology held constant,

productivity varies as the environmental situation varies.
These two statements may be simply expressed as :
P = f (T) , E being constant

and,
P = f (E) , T held constant
where ’P’ is productivity, »T’ is technique of environmental

exploitation and 1E1 is environment.
From these abstract statements a number of more
specific propositions can be derived which would be useful
in evaluating relative productivity.

P = f (T), E held constant.

First consider

One of the principles that may

be deduced from this statement we have already mentioned,

namely, that other factors constant, horticultural techniques

of exploitation are usually more productive than hunting and
gathering.

Although the source of energy exploited in both

cases is living organisms, in horticultural activities, due
to the tools and techniques employed, the distribution and
reproduction of the organisms are controlled; in collecting

it is not so.

The number of man-hours spent per capita in

horticulture, therefore, is less than, and the surplus greater

20 b

than, in collecting.

The presence of domesticated animals

is also important in that respect.

Polynesian cultures vary

in the degree to which they depend on domesticated resources,
thus this variation could be significant productivity-wise.

Other derivations of P = f (T) which may be used are
concerned with the productivity implications of particular

horticultural techniques.

For example, environments being

similar, it is reasonable that the group possessing the great­
est variety of crops will often have the greatest produc­
tivity.

The reasoning is that the group with the greatest

crop range will be able to cope with a greater variety of en­

vironmental conditions.

As Polynesian cultures do vary in

crop range, this feature must also be considered.
Crops differ in the environments they require for
fertility.

If two cultures find themselves in the same type

of environment, that culture will be most productive whose
crops are best adapted to that environment.

Crops, of course,

are somewhat adaptable, especially if there is a selection of

varieties.

But if we take the outstanding Polynesian crops,

we find that they do not all have the same "ideal" environ­

ments.

Coconut, for example, although it seems to thrive in

almost any kind of soil, even in coral sand and brackish water,
56

Unfortunately, the sub-species variation in crops
is difficult to estimate in the literature, often because of
a complete lack of data, and frequently due to the difference
between botanical and native classification of variation.
Consequently such data will not be relied upon.

207

grows best at altitudes between sea level and 2000 feet.

i^7

Once coconut has reached bearing age, it flowers and fruits
continuously, producing up to 100 nuts per annum.

It is a

hardy, long-lived plant; it requires little care at most.

Bananas, on the other hand, require fairly humid climate;
moist, deep and rich soil; protection from the wind; good
<8
drainage; sun and heat."
Yams must have a deep soil, fairly

They are a seasonal crop. They endure drought better
59
than any root crop.
Yams can grow at fairly high altitudes

warm.

and are preservable when out of the ground.

yield.

Taro has a high

It may be planted wet or dry, but needs a fair amount

of water in either case.

Taro bears continuously and with an

adequate drainage situation it can be left in the soil and

harvested when needed, making it a particularly useful crop.
While taro is quite adaptable to sun and moisture conditions,

sweet potato by contrast spoils with too little sun or too
much moisture.

Handy states that it is more valuable than

taro because it can be grown in less favorable localities, it

matures in one-third the time and requires less labor in
planting and careIt does not, however, mature continuously

as does taro, only seasonally.

Breadfruit usually bears

three or four major crops a year, although with a number of

varieties,

the harvests may be staggered throughout a good

part of the year.

Breadfruit seems to flourish best at lower

altitudes (up to 1500-2000 feet).

It requires a warm, humid

climate, heavy rainfall, moist soil and good drainage.

It

is reported that absence of any one of the conditions may be
62
seriously detrimental.
Thus we see that crops vary not only in environmen­

tal requirements, but also in the number of harvests and the

like.

These crop variations should be kept in mind.

Another factor which increases horticultural produc­
tivity is the development of special techniques of exploita­
tion, as irrigation, frequently found in Polynesia.

Irriga­

tion permits almost continuous production on the same land
due to the replenishment of minerals through silt deposits

of the irrigation water--as well, of course, as providing
necessary water.

Irrigation is quite superior as a produc­

tive technique to shifting cultivation.^

course, with a great number or varieties with
different growth rates, the effect may be one of continu­
ous production. However, if a group domesticated a number
oi varieties, they would usually be spread out over the
omain, and as far as any one producer is concerned, it
would be a seasonal crop.

209

Thus under P = f (T), E held constant, we found a

number of variable features of technological systems which
should be noted when considering the formal characteristics
of technology and environment with a view toward estimating
relative productivity: dependence on horticulture; range of

crop variation; adaptability of crops to environment and type

of crop and special techniques of domestication.

Under

P = f (E), T held constant, we can determine a number of sim­

ilarly significant variable features.

For example, the tech­

nologies being similar, the greater the variety of environ­
mental zones, the greater the possibility of wider and richer

exploitation.

High islands offer a marked contrast to low

islands in this respect.

Almost all high islands contain a

greater variety of conditions as well as richer opportunities
for plant growth than low islands.

Different altitudes,

soils and conditions of water supply, in particular, con­

tribute to the rich ecological diversity on high islands.
On low islands, environmental differentiation is practically
non-existent.Furthermore, environmental opportunities
for horticulture on coral atolls are extremely limited.

is a particular problem.

Water

And the loose coral rubble that

goes under the term "soil” is of no help, as it is so porous

that whenever rains do occur, organic materials quickly leach
through.

210

Another feature of environment which varies from
island to island yet which is relevant to considerations of
productivity is the richness of natural exploitable biota.

Availability of edible plants is, of course, important.

But

perhaps more crucial in Polynesia is the magnitude of fish

resources.

There is considerable variation in fishing oppor­

One variation is the general decrease in abundance
65
and variety of fish from west to east in the Pacific.
A

tunities,

more important variation is the presence of reefs, especially

of barrier reefs and lagoons, which offered unparalleled op­

portunities of adding to the food supply through fishing.^
Some islands, as the Marquesas, have no barrier reef or
lagoon, and fishing resources are relatively poor.

All of these variable features of technology and en­
vironment are relevant to productivity differentials.

So

also are the presence and frequency of droughts, hurricanes
and the like which are apt to considerably hamper exploita­

tive activities, if not to produce severe famine.

Other

things being equal, a culture subject to natural catastro­
phes or to gaps in the yearly cycle of production which in­

duce food shortages or dependence on stored, previously

"The greatest abundance and variety of forms prob­

harvested foods, is lower in productivity than the culture
of ari island not affected by such factors.

The population

carrying capacity would tend to be reduced on islands which
are apt to experience famine and consequently, the number of
man-hours per capita spent in food production rises.

A defi­

ciency in storage techniques would have the same result,

other factors being constant.

The Polynesian islands we have been considering
will now be examined from a technological-environmental

point of view to determine if the relative productivities of
the cultures check with the productivities as so far deter­

mined.

67

The significance of gaps in exploitative activi­
ties has recently been pointed out by Bartholomew and Bird­
sell, although they are more concerned with population den­
sity rather than size of the economic unit, as we are here :
Since biological factors vary with time, values for popula­
tion equilibria are not to be measured at a given point in
time. They fluctuate about a balance which is determined,
not by the mean condition, but by extremes. Indeed, one of
the most firmly established ecological generalizations is
Liebig's law of the minimum, which states that a biological
reaction at any level is controlled not by factors which
are present in excess, but by that essential factor which is
present in minimal quantity." (G. Bartholomew and J. B.
Birdsell, Ecology and the Protohominids," Amer. Anthrop.,
55, 481-498, 1953, pp. 487-488.)
--------------------- *
The reason that population density has not been
used here as a measure of productivity is that an increase
in density does not imply an increase in productivity as we
use that term. A coral atoll dependent on coconuts and
fishing may have a relatively dense population (see
Yet hardly any surplus is derived; fishing and coconut
gathering usually are daily activities. And the number of
man-hours per capita spent in food production must be rela­
tively high. This low productivity is reflected by the rela­
tively low population size (see Table II), but not by den­
sity.

ENVIRONMENTAL OPPORTUNITIES
A group of high (highest summit, 7321"),
relatively young volcanic islands, Total lend
area near 600 eq, miles, Islands generally
circular in shape with streams radiating from
central peak or peaks, Mountainous center
often surrounded by maritime flat of rich alluvlfl soil and swamps, often extending up
low gradient valleys to depth of four miles
or more. This flat, due to soil and water,
is extremely favorable to the growth of many
types of crops,
Streams fall fairly rapidly, However,
due to comparative youth, the valleys are not
as deeply cut as some islands, the Marquesas,
for example, There often are fertile flats
in the upland valleys. Soils are numerous in
type and generally fertile. The mountain
soils are thin, A red clay soil is found on
the lower slopes, Irrigation ie most practi­
cable in inland valleys, but wet taro can also
be grown in swamps on maritime flats,

A heavy cover of bush and trees is found
upland, the particular type of vegetation
varying with altitude and allied factors of
rainfall, humidity and the like, The distri­
bution of rainfall varies, in fact, with al­
titude and windward vs, leeward sides of is­
lands. The streams are permanent, A two
season tropical chute prevails,

The Islands have fringing and barrier
reefs, the latter often one to two miles out,
The lagoon, the open sea, the reefs, fresh
water lakes and streams abound with water life,
A number of wild plants are edible ineluding wild varieties of cultivated ones as
well as Tahitian Chestnut, candlenut, panda*
nue, Morinda cltrlfoha, various berries,
fruits, etc, Edible plants are found in lowland, valley and upland regions,

There were quite a few edible birds.
Tropical storms and hurricanes are not

The area around the coast offers the best food in the
exploitive possibilities. Hillsides slope more diet,
moderately here and svamps are formed betveen
the back of the beach and the interior hills.
Further inland the mountains become rapidly very
steep, larger streams floving to sea form vide
valleys at their mouths, The lagoon formed by
the barrier reef contributes to the rich diver­
sity of this coastal area as fish abound in the
lagoon,
Hurricanes occur at regular intervals.

There is much variation from Island to is­
land, but as a general rule, ecological opportu­
nities are limited to the coastal area and its
environs, Diversity of ecological zones seems
more restricted than in Havail, or at least have
not been exploited by native or European and
thus not noticed in the literature,
A dense tropical forest cover over most of
the islands, Edible wild flora include: vild
taro, vild yam, Tahitian Chestnut, tree fern and
seaweed. There were also vild birds.

CHART I MAHWA, mmOLCG! AUD WIM!«

raom oppoHiusiiiis
Four mall (11 iq. miles) volcanic
lilanii surrouoitd by reef, Highlit lumlt
1427 fiet, The encircling reef contai»
some coral islets valuable ai access points
to reefs and fishing grounds,
Each main Island traverse! by a razor
back ridge along its long axis, Secondary
ridges run perpendicularly to coast where
they end in steep promontories, Between
them are bays forming valley mouths, The
valleys extend inward from tea with a vary­
ing usually small, amount of flat land,
sometimes swampy, These valleys are the
most cultivable parts of the islands, given
the native techniques,

The climate is rainy but (probably due
to the relatively low height of the Islands)
there are no permanent streams, although
there are various springs and seepages on
high and low land,
The soil is often rocky, The native
flora Is sparse, Forest cover consists
mainly of clumps at valley heads and cliff
bases, and cant growth on the ends of main
ridges.
The lagoon, reefs and open sea abound
in fish, shell fish, and other sea life,
Edible wild flora include: Norinda
cl trifolia, Cucinus sg., Portulaea lutea,
Tely hrosla purpurea, and others,

M 5, ÏASffl ISLAND, DOLOGÏ AMD ÎMTIH0M1W

SOURCE OF
raONMBL OPPORTUNITIES

Volcanic island, about 62 eq, miles, Highest
summit, 1^0 feet, Triangular in shape with three
volcanoes at angles,
The island lacks permanent rivers, is
therefore not rugged nor deeply eroded, Rainfall
averages about $0’ per annum, falls heaviest
during the Mar,-Oct, period of the two season
tropical climate. The soil is fertile, especially when deep, However it is generally porous
and rain runs rapidly through, Much of the interlor is a plain composed of soil of varying
depth and rock outcrop, Evidently much of the
deep soil is near the coast, affording the best
agricultural land, The only interior water supply
is in three crater lakes,
The coast itself is jagged, in some places
abutted by an escarpment of 50-100 feet, Sandy
beaches are few,
There is no barrier reef and fishing oppor(unities are, by consequence, limited,

Grass is the most common cover on the Island,
Forests were very limited, securing mostly near
crater lakes, indigenous flora was on the whole,
extremely limited in variety, Bible plants were
few,
Aside from fish and water life, tea birds
and rats were exploitable fauna,
Short droughts secured, The water supply was
limited, Veils were necessary, The crater lakes
are largely inaccessible,

Thomson estimated that about square miles
are suitable for cultivation, It should be men­
tioned that there is a great deal of variability
in early accounts as to the fertility tf tester
Island (see Métraux, WllS-Uit Thomson, 188?:
k55f.)

CM I, MANIA, TECHNOLOGY AND «NM

miBONm OPPORTUNITIES

Small (17,500 acres) relatively low
(55h') volcanic Island,
A narrow coast running against a raised
coral limestone shelf, makatea, which encircles
the Island, The shelf, several hundred yards
to 1| miles wide, terminates abruptly at its
interior border, dropping about 150 feet, At
inner foot of the makatea lie swamps up to 500
yards wide wherever streams, running off a
central plateau, abut and eventually seep
through the shelf, This swamp land is called
puna and Is immensely valuable for the growing
of taro, Sauli pockets of friable soil are
also found on the makatea and are useful for
certain crops. Beside the makatea abounds with
edible candlenut,
The upper reaches of the streams cut
narrow valleys upland suitable for some crops,
The relatively low height results In a definite
dry season, July to December, In which streams
are apt to be impermanent, and the puna lands
unsuitable for taro,
There is a surrounding fringing reef,
80 to JOO yards wide which is always below
water, although the outer rim protrudes at low
tide, Fish fairly abundant, especially season­
ally,

The volcanic slopes are covered with dense
fern and casaurla, Thickets of trees and scrub
in stream valleys, Beside candlenut, pandanue,
and a number of other edible, uncultivated
fruits and berries found, and native chestnut,

Hurricanes were rare, about once every
20 years,

There are few beaches and no barrier reef, limiting
fishing to the deep sea variety, and cutting down the
relative abundance of fish,
Most streams are intermittent at their lower reaches
and may go dry in periods of drought,

Rainfall usually varies little seasonally, but
varies much with altitude and windward or leeward sides
of islands, High altitudes are wetter, and leeward
sides of islands are dry, often exceedingly co, Fur­
thermore, rainfall may vary from year to year, producing
cycles of drought and plenty within a human generation,
The drou^t years are hard on vegetation and may produce
severe famine,
The soil is generally porous and fertile, but it is
limited in agricultural possibilities, A lack of level
ground hampers the development of large scale irrigation,
even where water supply is adequate,

Three zones of vegetation can be described, altitude
being the delimiting factor, The uppermost areas are
cloaked in rain forest; the other areas also have thick
indigenous cover except on the leeward which may be des­
Bleated,

No severe storms occur, but tidal waves can flood
valleys up to j mile inland,

™ 10, IIïOPIA, TECHNOLOGY AND WIMMI

ENVIRONMENTAL OPPORTUNITIES

Stall (J iq, al.), volcanic Island. An ancient
crater rim with highest point 2000 ft,, enclosing a
crater lake, On the southwest, a flat plain about
1 mile at widest points, with a large natural
swamp, A narrow strip of beach and a fringing reef,
Land on the sides and crest of the old crater
is primarily composed of volcanic rock with areas
of soil, especially on the crest, which is worn
flat at places, The flat land plain is composed
of alluvial soils near mountain, sandier soils near
coast, The soil is fertile, Springs and pools run
down slopes from the crater lake,
Coastal vegetation is sparse, but it increases
further Inland, Several wild plants (i,e,, not
found in plantations or gardens) are of some value
as food, including arrowroot, Cordyline terminally
and pandanuy,
The interior crater lake, the fringing reef
(especially in south, it is narrow in north) and
open sea abound in fish life seasonally. But,
"the absence of any extensive reef system prevents
any large constant supply from being obtained, in
a period of rough weather the people may taste no
fish for several weeks at a time, and then live entirely on vegetable food," (Firth, $0-1$ M7),
Birds of a number of species occur, as do eels,

A two season climate prettily, Severe gales
occur in the early monsoon, sometimes becoming
hurricanes, Droughts occur on occasion,

220

Charts one to thirteen present the data on the tech­

nological-environmental conditions of the Islands (Tonga ex­
cepted).

It was postulated that consideration of the formal

characteristics of technology and environment would provide

a qualitative check on the relative productivities as they

have already been assigned.

It seems indisputable that, in

the main, the data do illustrate relative productivities

(assessed according to the principles outlined above), that
correspond to productivities measured by other means.

As a

shorthand method for the further comparison of productivity
with the amount of stratification in different Polynesian

islands, let us consider the productivities as they may be
derived from the charts in terms of the categories (Groups I,

Ila, lib and III) which we have outlined as representing the
amount of stratification.

In the process, the relation of the

qualitative assessment of productivity to other methods of

discerning productivity will become apparent, as the latter

have already been found to correspond to the stratification
groupings.
Group I societies , Hawaii, the Society Islands and

Samoa (Tonga is excluded here as no chart could be made),

were all characterized by a marked emphasis on domesticated
food resources coupled with exploitation of rich supply of non­

cultivated foods.

Each had a wide range of crops (12 to l^)

and the crops were used in a number of different ecological

niches to which they were adaptively suited.

Among the special

techniques of domestication, irrigation is outstanding in

227

Hawaii and to a lesser extent, in Tahiti.
lands were drained.

In Samoa, swamp

In all three areas, fish were abun­

dant—especially in Samoa and the Society Islands where bar­

rier reefs and lagoons are found—and were exploited by a

wide variety of techniques.

Edible wild plants were numerous,

although not heavily relied upon.

Hawaii and the Society Is­

lands presented a great diversity of rich environmental

zones to which crops could be adapted.

Samoa also had environ­

mental diversity, but it was somewhat more restrieted--at
least in area.

In each locale, due to the height of the is­

lands , water supply was not a problem.

Droughts were infre­

quent; there was little seasonal variation in productivity

level; and, due to the wide crop range, hurricanes could not
do severe damage.

Furthermore, the major crops could also be

stored; taro in the ground, breadfruit in pits.

The overall

picture of the Group I technologies and environments is one of
extensive and intensive exploitation of a variety of condi­
tions , sometimes by specialized techniques, at a continuously
high level.

With respect to differences of productivity within

Group I, Hawaii with its large-scale irrigation techniques

and conscious and effective utilization of small ecological
niches (such as the use of irrigation banks for subsidiary

crops), stands above the field.

Thus in general the techno­

logical environmental situations of Group I societies afford

a picture of productivity commensurable with that derived from
the other measures used.

228

A striking feature of the environments of Group Ila

societies, Mangareva, Mangala, Easter Island and Uvea, is the
relatively low height of the volcanic summits on each island.
As a result, precipitation in high altitudes is definitely

seasonal, and the drainage systems impermanent (or practically
non-existent in the case of Easter Island, where soil porosity

is also a factor).

This not only prohibited the development

of irrigation on a large scale, but due to the seasonal char­

acter of the water supply—now solely dependent on trade
69
winds--probably
made for some seasonal declines in produc­

tivity.

Even storage techniques do not make up for these

declines when compared to Group I societies where production

was high the year around, and only a small part of the crops

need be stored; most could be immediately distributed and con­

sumed.

The range of crops in Group Ila societies was gener­

ally less than Group I, although Mangareva compares favorably
with Group I.

The crops were used in particular environmental

situations, but environmental diversity does not compare with
Group I Islands—hence the scope of exploited environments

was more restricted.

Irrigation was practiced on Mangala and

Mangareva, but the extent was limited, as noted.
swamps were drained for raising taro.

On Uvea,

On each island the

amount of very fertile soil was restricted to one particular
area: the Mangarevan bays, the Uvean and Easter Island coastal

areas and the Mangalan puna lands.

The data are not complete.

Natural biotic conditions

varied significantly from the very rich fishing resources

on Mangareva and Uvea, to the more restricted resources of
Easter Island and Mangala.

There was a greater abundance of

edible flora on Mangareva and Mangala than on Easter and
Uvea.

In all islands, however, domesticated resources were

more relied upon than non-domesticated.

In sum, Group IIa

societies rate definitely lower, according to the evaluating

principles outlined, than Group I.
Within Group IIa, the evidence from the formal char­

acteristics of the technology and environment supports a judg­
ment of greater productivity for Mangareva.

The greatest

range of crops by far was found there, and fishing resources
were very good.

Definite, prolonged lean seasons, famines

and a restricted range of crops in Uvea, and the small range

of crops and relatively poor fishing conditions on Easter
Island, indicate a lower productivity on these islands than

others of Group IIa.

Uvea definitely shows technological-

environmental situations approaching those found in Group lib

as regards productivity implications.
In Group IIb, Tikopia, Marquesas and Futuna, techno-

environmental conditions show only a mediocre productivity
level, although very close to some Group IIa societies, as in­

dicated.

The Futunans used a considerable range of crops (10),

but mostly in a restricted area, the shore strip.

The in­

terior of the islands were not fully exploited, especially—

due to the acidic and impervious nature of the soil--on

Futuna proper.

Furthermore, other things being equal, the

lack of an abundant fish supply is important in Futuna
vis a vis other islands.

On the other hand, although sea­

sonal declines in productivity are indicated, severe famines
were rare and a special technique of domestication, irriga­

tion, was found in Futuna.

In the Marquesas, the lack of

level land, and small distribution of fertile soil--both re­

lated to the deeply eroded nature of the islands--seriously
limited technological developments, especially of irrigation.
There is, as in Futuna, no barrier reef or lagoon, hence

fishing possibilities are restricted.

Finally, although sea­

sonal declines in productivity are not clearly indicated,

severe famines, occurring periodically, are.
of the Marquesas is similar to Futuna.

The crop range

On Tikopia, long

seasonal drops in carrying capacity occurred.

Again, a

lagoon and barrier reef are absent, although the fringing
reef was extensively utilized for sea hunting.

The range of

crops is comparable to those of Futuna and the Marquesas.
Environmental diversity was extensively utilized on Tikopia,
but every type of land was very restricted in area.

To sum­

marize, Group lib societies, although having a fairly wide

crop range, were limited in productivity by relatively poor
fishing resources as compared to islands with lagoons, and by

severe seasonal or cyclical declines in productivity (which
can be traced to environmental factors).

Beside, in each

case, exploitation was spatially limited.

The three islands

seem about equal in productivity level from these considera­

tions.

231

The Group III societies, Tokelau, Ontong Java and
Pukapuka, may be clearly differentiated from Group II soci­

eties in productivity as revealed in the technologicalenvironmental situation.

atolls.

All three were found on coral

These atolls uniformly are characterized by soil

and water conditions not conducive to horticulture and by a
lack of environmental diversity and edible wild flora, as

already mentioned.

Furthermore, drought and famine, constant

threats, reduce the productivity to almost pre-agrlcultural

levels for long periods.

Only the abundant fish supply

makes such islands at all inhabitable by non-industrial
peoples.

In each case it may be seen that the range of crops

is far smaller than that of other societies considered.
There is little to choose between Ontong Java, Pukapuka and

Tokelau in the way of most or least productive.

The tech­

nologies and environments are almost identical.

Summary

The social systems of fourteen Polynesian islands
were classified, according to the amount of stratification
they contained, into four groups (from more to less strati­
fied): I, Ila, lib, III.

The difference in stratification

between Tia and Ilb was found to be less marked than between

I and Ila and between lib and III.

It was postulated that

the amount of stratification varies with the productivity of
the system of exploitation of environment.

If this hypothesis

2^2

be correct, a correlationship should be found between the

groupings of the amount of stratification and measures of
productivity.

The measure of productivity primarily used

was : the greatest magnitude and frequency of the collection
and redistribution of the same goods--as in Polynesia, sur­

plus production is distributed from a central agency.

This

measure was checked by a subsidiary measure based upon the

degree of non-strategic specialization, and by consideration
of the formal aspects of the particular techno-environmental
situations in terms of their productivity implications.

All measures of productivity were found to correlate
with the classification of the amount of stratification on
the basis of four major groupings.

The smaller difference

in stratification between Ila and lib than between I, II and
III showed up in the measures based upon the size of the dis­

tributive unit and the properties of the techno-environmental

conditions.

The four major stratification groupings, however,
were also to an extent subdivided, albeit the measure of
stratification was not too refined.

Hawaii was distinguished

among Group I societies as being the most stratified.

In

terms of the network of food redistribution and in formal
characteristics of the techno-environmental interaction,

Hawaiian productivity was found to be greatest of the Group

I productivities.
In Group Ila, Mangareva was estimated as most strati­
fied, Uvea as the least--with the other two, Easter Island

233

and Mangala, about equal.

Evidence from the scope of the

redistributive network, the amount of specialization and the

formal aspects of Mangarevan technology-environment, indi­
cates that Mangareva had the highest productivity in Group

Ha.

That Uvea had the lowest productivity in this Group

cannot be documented as the evidence is incomplete—although
the paucity of specialization and techno-environmental condi­

tions are suggestive.
In Group lib societies, Futuna was classified as

least stratified, the Marquesas as most.

In productivity

as measured by redistribution, the Marquesas rank highest,

but Futuna appears to have had a higher productivity than
Tikopia, also of this Group.

No further clarification was

forthcoming from other productivity measures.
Ontong Java, Tokelau and Pukapuka, Group III soci­
eties, appear to have been equally stratified.

Productivity­

wise, Ontong Java ranks highest by scope of redistribution,
but the figures on Ontong Java may be seriously inaccurate
70
due to acculturation influences.'
In other measures of

productivity no significant differences within Group III can
be detected.

In short, the evidence corroborates our deductions,

at least as the evidence may be grossly measured.

The

amount of stratification is correlated with productivity of

techno-environmental interaction.
70

See pp. 381ff.

The role of the chief as

234

regulator of the redistributive system, with its implica­

tions for the extension of authority into other domains of
culture, may be seen as the link between the co-varlance of

productivity and stratification.

Thus differences in the

amount of social stratification in Polynesian cultures can
be understood as adaptive variations.
The possibility exists that other factors were af­

fecting the amount of stratification in Polynesia--factors

operating independently of the cultural adaptation to en­
vironment.

The relationship between stratification and the

history of the cultures should be examined.

ject-matter of the next chapter.

23b

CHAPTER VII
STRATIFICATION AND HISTORY

In studying differences in social stratification as
adaptive variations, Polynesian cultures were chosen, be­

cause these cultures have common historical traditions and

origins.

It is not a matter of ethological dispute that the

Polynesian cultures have a common history.Consequently
Polynesian culture as a whole can be considered as a single

species undergoing adaptive radiation in particular habits
and social stratification can be seen, as illustrated, to be
related to various features of the technological articulation

of the cultures to these habitats.

However, in Polynesia,

two diverse historical currents have been recognized for some

time, and the possibility exists, therefore, that stratifica­
tion is more a part of one rather than the other, irrespective
2
of adaptive aspects.
We refer, of course, to the differen­

tiation of western Polynesia from central-marginal, or eastern

Polynesia.

This historical divergence apparently occurred

fairly far back in aboriginal Polynesian development and may

That Ontong Javanese culture is primarily related
to Polynesian cultures rather than to Micronesian is a mat­
ter of dispute (see p. 402f).

represent the diffusion of cultural elements from two centers
of "radiating influence"--Flji in the west, the Society Islands

If stratification were more closely allied to one or
another of these historical entities, then we should expect
to find a higher development of stratification in either

eastern or western Polynesia.
this proposition.

However, the data do not support

Of the islands considered, Samoa, Tonga,

Tikopia, Uvea, Futuna, Tokelau, Pukapuka and (with reserva­
tions ) Ontong Java, are western; the Societies, Hawaii, Mar­

quesas, Mangala and Mangareva, are eastern.

All four cate­

gories of stratification are represented in western Polynesia:

Group I by Tonga and Samoa; Group Ila by Uvea; Group lib by
Tikopia and Futuna; and Group III by Tokelau, Pukapuka and

Ontong Java.

Groups I, Ila and Ilb are represented in eastern

Polynesia by Hawaii and the Societies, Mangareva and Mangala,
and the Marquesas, respectively.

The fact that no eastern

Polynesian atoll was considered may account for the lack of
Group III stratification there.

The historical difference be­

tween western and eastern Polynesia, therefore, does not account
for the degree of stratification in the various cultures.

the contrary, the amount of stratification is seen to be an
adaptive variation when the cultures are classified on this

historical basis.

On

We have not exhausted the possible ways in which his­

torical tradition may influence the amount of stratification

in a particular culture irrespective of the cultural adapta­
tion.

In any particular culture, either the entire stratifi­

cation system or some aspect of it may be elaborated beyond
the stratification expected if all other factors save histori­

cal influences are held constant.

Unfortunately, neither the

criteria of stratification nor of productivity are fine
anough to show exact proportional relationship between these
variables and thus clearly Indicate where such “unexpected”

results turn up.

But here and there in the data, certain

features of the amount of stratification in a particular

island seem incongruously elaborated relative to other aspects
of the stratification system.

Such features may be non-

adaptive "survivals,” vestigial forms.

A good example is the

relatively elaborate sanctification of the high chief of

Pukapuka.

Despite the fact that the community elders were the

most potent socio-political force in Pukapuka, the high chief

was surrounded by a fairly complex mana-tabu system and was

paid respect through forms of obeisance comparable to those

existing in more stratified societies.Similarly, the cere­
monial sanctification of Tokelauan and Uvean chiefs was greater

than one might expect relative to the roles of these chiefs in

social and economic processes.^

^See p. l£6ff.
^See pp. lUiff; 170ff.

In cases such as these we see clearly the operation
of non-adaptive survivals in the stratification system.
Both Tokelau and Pukepuka were settled from high islands

where the degree of stratification was much greater than
Pukapukan or Tokelauan life warranted; and both were settled

within the last six hundred yearsUvea was settled around
11^50 a,D,—again relatively late—from more highly stratified
7
Tonga, according to linguistic and genealogical evidence.

Non-adaptive survivals can affect aspects of the degree of
stratification in particular cases,

in the examples given

these survivals were persistences in the ideological sphere of

culture.

Such survivals could occur in social and economic

spheres also, and may be revealed in Polynesia when finer

measures of stratification can be developed and applied.

But

the most striking "incongruities" In this sense are very prob­
ably most frequent in ceremonial life.

It has long been recog­

nized that ideologies, in particular religious beliefs and
practices, survive long after the particular forces which have
brought them into being; this is the "cultural lag" concept.

Tt is not surprising then that ceremonial aspects of the de­
gree of stratification are "over-elaborate" in some cultures

of Polynesia--especially those which have diverged but a short

time ago from more highly stratified cultures.

In short,

Puka-

puka is elaborated beyond other differential aspects of the

status system, a fact related to the relatively non-adaptive

character of ideology and thus understandable in terms of pre­

vious situations of the cultures, rather than their present
adaptations.

%

This brings to a close Part One of this work.

Vari­

ations in amount of social stratification in Polynesia have

been shown to be correlated with variations in technoenvironmental interaction in terms of productivity.

Differ­

ences in social stratification are thus relatable to the
types of technological articulations of Polynesian cultures

to their environment; hence, these differences are cultural

adaptive varieties.

The second half of our task is to outline

the particular forms of social organization and social strati­
fication in Polynesia, and to see then if the forms as well
as the amounts of social stratification may be considered to

be adaptive variations.

THE FORM OF STRATIFICATION

240

CHAPTER VIII
RAMAGE SYSTEMS OF SOCIAL ORGANIZATION
AND SOCIAL STRATIFICATION

In this and the following chapters of this work, the
forms of social organization and social stratification in
Polynesia will be outlined.

An attempt will be made to re­

late these forms to aspects of the various technologies and

environments.

Three different forms may be delineated:

ramage, descent line and coral atoll organization.

These will

be described and analyzed in that order.
The Ramage Organization in Polynesia

Despite the general similarity of form in the basic

kinship structure in the high Polynesian Islands or groups of
Hawaii, Tonga, the Societies, Mangareva, Mangala, Easter Island,
Tikopia, the Marquesas and New Zealand, the literature on these
islands bears no agreement on what this basic form might be

called.

Apparently what is one and the same thing, structur­

ally, has been designated "clan," "tribe," "lineage," "descent

group," or by a native term, as hapu among the Maori.The

The reasons for this terminological confusion are not
completely unintelligible. When the aboriginal social sys­
tems of Polynesia were being studied in the late 1920's and
early '30's by trained observers, there simply was no wide­
spread recognition of and terminology for any social organiza­
tion other than those occurring among the American Indians,
particularly the North American Indians. And since the only
example of a social organization among the North American In­
dians which could have stimulated a new terminology applicable
to Polynesia--that of the Northwest Coast Indians--were so
little understood at the time, ethnographers studying Polynesia
could do little else but apply the old, Indian terminology.

241

publication of Firth’s, We, The Tikopia in 1936, however, was
the beginning of a terminological revolution in Polynesian

studies.

Although he had used the word "clan" to describe

Tikopian groupings, Firth recognized that he was dealing with
something basically different from what was usually called
2
clan.
This Tikopian clan was a unilateral descent group,

but it was not exogamous, a feature which heretofore had been
the hallmark of clanship.

Furthermore, there was not merely

belief in common descent, but an actual genealogical reckoning
of patrilineal descent which served to delimit one Tikopian

clan from another as well as to differentiate people and
groups within the clan according to their nearness of descent

to the common ancestor.

The entire system can be pictured as

a genealogical tree with groups branching and rebranching
from a major line of descent.in such a system, naturally,
great importance is attached to seniority.

Firth proposed

that the term "ramage" be used to designate kinship of such

nature since,

This term has the advantage of suggesting im­
mediately by its etymology the branching process
by which these groups attain individuality and
yet keep their connection with the parent stem.
It is also consistent in a metaphor with the

^Firth, 1936, 367-372.
Ibid., 371. As Firth points out, the tree meta­
phor is often used by the natives themselves.

expression "genealogical tree.” The process can
be correctly described as one of ramification.4

A "ramage” then can be described as a social group re­
lated to a number of like groups in that all have branched off

from a single genealogical source.

Firth does not make clear

exactly what degree of departure from a common ancestor is

necessary to make a ramage.

Hence the order of size of the

unit which is to be called "ramage" is left in doubt.
Tikopia it would be the "clan," he implies.

In

Yet Tikopian

households whose members are descendants from the common clan
ancestor might actually be called ramages.

are ramage groups of one member.

Even individuals

We feel this conception is

quite proper in terms of the process of ramification.

The

order of size of a ramage depends, in actuality, on what is
demarcated as a kin group among people having ramified social

systems. Thus the Maori hapu are ramages of the "tribe" (ivi)
and the whanau are ramages of the hapu.^ Similarly the Tongan

"lineage" is a ramage of Tongan society (which is a magnified
genealogical tree), and within lineages are units of varying
order of size.^
^Ibid., 371.

At approximately the same time that

Firth applied the term ramage to this distinct type of organi­
zation, Kirchhoff in a brilliant paper had isolated ramage or­
ganizations and called them "conical clans" in contrast to
the ”equalitarian clans” as one finds, for example, among the
Iroquois (Kirchoff, ca. 1934 ms). Firth’s terminology is
adopted here as it is more descriptive than Kirchoff’s and is
already widely known.
2 vols.

See E. Best, The Maori, Mem. of Poly. Soc.. 5, 192^,

Gifford, 1929.

243

The ramified organization is characteristic of abori­

ginal, Tikopia, Hawaii, Tonga, the Societies, Mangareva,
Mangala, Easter Island, the Marquesas and New Zealand.

The

basic feature of ramified social systems is the organization
of the kinship groups along the lines of a genealogical tree.
Each has, as does the society at large, a leader, or head,

and perhaps the best point of departure for an understanding
of these systems is the customary mode of succession to the

position of headship.

Barring defects in personality, the

mode of succession is primogeniture; the oldest son succeeds

to the position of his father.

Certain variations from island

to island are occasioned by the differential role of women in

the ramified system.

But with few exceptions, effective lead­

ership is vested in the male, even though he may have an older

sister.

For this reason the position of women and their role

in the ramified system may be neglected in this general dis­
cussion.

Given primogenitural succession to positions of lead­
ership in the society certain consequences follow which are

essential to an understanding of ramified social systems and
the ranking systems which they produce.

The major conse­

quence is that an eldest brother in a family is differentiated

from his younger brothers on the basis of this succession to

the headship.

This is not only a differentiation in prestige

but it also permeates the childhood and entire life cycle of

these individuals.

The life crises rites of birth, puberty,

marriage and death of the eldest son are of an entirely

244

different order of elaboration from the rites de passage of
his brothers.

Training for leadership, both formal and in­

formal, also mark off the eldest son.

Not only is the eldest

son differentiated from his younger brothers, but so also is
every brother differentiated from each other in accordance

with the respective order of birth and the consequent prospect
of succeeding to the position of the father.

Thus is every

brother ranked by a principle of seniority.
The seniority principle in the family is the micro­

cosm of the ramified social system.

This extension of ranking

within a family to ranking within the entire society is well
7
described by Gifford.
As a consequence of seniority, the
descendants of an older son or older brother rank higher than

the descendants of a younger brother.

In any given group

which is descendent from a common ancestor, we can distinguish

a senior line from a number of junior lines.

Consequently,

every individual within this group of descendants from a com­

mon ancestor holds a different status which is precisely in
proportion to his distance from the senior line of descent in

the group.

The Polynesian societies considered here are often
single large size ramages.

The entire society can usually be

analyzed as composed of sections of a single genealogical sys­
tem at the apex of which stands the paramount chief.

This

chief is the direct descendant on the senior line of the reputed
7Ibid., 19-20.

245

founder of the society.

Other members of the group are

ranked in proportion to the closeness of their relationship
to the main line of descent.

Accordingly, people related to

branch-offs from the common ancestor of the society from a

remote period are lower in rank than those descendent from a
more immediate relative of the chiefly line (see Diagram 1).
People with the lowest status are those who have descended

from younger brothers through younger brothers ad infinitum.

The process of prImogen!tural succession and its consequent
implications of seniority result in a ranking structure which
encompasses the entire society (see Diagrams 1 and 2).

This

does not necessarily mean that every individual kept a full

genealogical record back to the common ancestor of the group.
On the contrary, usually only the paramount chief kept such a
record.

However, the remembered ancestor of lower ranking in­

dividuals or groups of individuals was usually a junior rela­

tive of the ancestor of a higher ranking person or group.

In

this way the whole community formed a unified ramification

structure.
The position of the paramount chief is reinforced
through deification of his ancestors, themselves former para­

mount chiefs, and through connecting this main line of descent
to the important god or gods of the group.

By consequence of

divine descent a certain degree of sacredness and power, mana,
is felt to be inherently a part of chieftainship.

Often the

sacredness of the chief is associated with supernatural power

over the fertility of the land and people.

And the chief is
