248

surrounded with a number of tabus and the like which vary in

elaborateness from island to island.

As we have seen, these

tabus reinforce the position of the chief at the apex of the
ramified system.

The usual exogamic line in these societies is the
second cousin.

Chiefs, however, marry closer in, thus tend­

ing to strengthen socio-economic prerogatives among the higher
social ranks.

Certain sections or branches of the genealogical

hierarchy are grouped together into delimitable units, ramages.
Basic to all these societies is one particular ramage, which
outside of individuals forms the unit of smallest size and is

the building block of larger ramages.

hold.

This unit is the house­

It is usually composed of a patrilocal extended family,

often a stem family rather than a joint family.

In accordance

with ramification principles, the household is headed by the

male who is senior in descent and everyone in the household is
ranked in relation to his nearness of descent to him.

If there

are larger order units, as the Tikopian "clan," the Tongan

"lineage" or the Mangaian and Easter Island "tribe," they are
composed of a number of households.

The household heads are

ranked vis a vis each other in the "clan" or comparable ramage

according to their descent.

Heads of this ramage are similarly

ranked in the larger group.

Members of each household take

their status, in larger groups, from the status of the house­

hold head.

This principle, however, does not ideally extend

beyond the household level, that is, there is no superstratifi­
cation of larger ramages.

Rather, members of

different ramages

249

of comparable order of size generally hold equivalent status

if they are proportionally distant from the main line of
descent within their respective ramages.
The lack of superstratification of one large ramage

over another is partially understandable in terms of another
characteristic of ramified systems—flexibility.

Flexibility

refers to changes from the ideal genealogical pattern through
ramage realignments of families or individuals, or through

the rise in power of a junior line and its usurpation of the
position of a senior line.

There seems to be a certain in­

herent flexibility in all ramified systems.

This is related

to the fact that there are more people descendent from collat­
eral relatives of remote paramount chiefs than from collateral

relatives of recent paramount chiefs.

Yet it is precisely

descendants of the latter who rank highest, theoretically, while
descendants of the former rank lower and lower.

Accordingly

there seems to be a periodic "shedding off” of high order
ramages whose heads claim their status through descent from a

remote relative of a remote paramount chief, and the creation
of new ramages around people who claim descent from more recent
offshoots of the chiefly line.

The result is ramages of more

or less comparable size under people descendent from ancestors
of more or less comparable status.

The shedding-off process must have occurred, for ex­

ample, in Tonga.

Diagram 2 is a genealogical record of the

ancestors of the high order Tongan ramages, haa.

The ramages,

by and large, descend from relatively recent collateral relatives

250

of the chiefly line.

Descendants of collaterals of a more

remote period have evidently been regrouped into the present

haa.

Gifford’s discussion of the haa ("lineages,M he terms

them) is instructive in this respect:

The whole system of lineages may be likened
to a tree with trunk, limbs, branches and
twigs. ... Everything points to the necessity of
a line of powerful chiefs for a nucleus about which
the lineage groups itself. Without such chiefs
it appears to wilt and die and its membership
g
gradually aligns itself with other rising lineages.
The process of shedding-off of ramages, or flexibil­
ity, is related to and operates in conjunction with another

necessity for ramage realignment, namely that occurring

through a differential increase of families and ramages.

A

large family puts pressure on familial land resources and some

means of alleviating this pressure results.

A number of mech­

anisms are used to facilitate flexibility of ramage realignment

in Polynesia.

Outstanding among them are internal disputes and

struggles, adoption into new ramages and alignments with the

mother’s father’s rather than with the father’s ramage.

Alignments of the last type may be effected through rearrang­

ing the genealogies or through residence after marriage in the
mother’s father’s household.

This type of alignment has often

been called ’matrilineal descent.’

The use of such terminology

however, obscures the basic fact that descent is traced through
a woman to her father, not her mother--the descent line reached

is still patrilineal.

8

Ibid., 30. .

Dr. Elman Service has suggested, in

261

private conversations, that the term •ambi-patrilineal descent’

he applied to such phenomena, and we feel it an appropriate

one.

Consequently we shall hereafter designate as ’ambi-

patrllineal* any pattern of tracing descent through a woman

into a patrilineal descent group.
As Firth pointed out, one of the most important fea­
tures of ramages "is the principle of fission and dispersion
9
in the creation of them. "
The genealogical tree metaphor

gives the clue for ramage growth.

As the branches diverge

from the stem and the twigs from the branches, so does one

household, as it grows in numbers, split from a parent house­
hold.

The point of split is usually between two brothers,

the younger often moving off with his offspring.

If this con­

tinues for a sufficient length of time, a new large order

ramage may be created.

The fission process results in a cer­

tain characteristic of territoriality.

Ramages, even of large

order, are more or less localized groups.

The relationship

to a certain territory is not always exact, there is usually
only a fairly good correlation between ramages and certain
districts of an island.^ A perfect correlation is often pro­

hibited by the tendency for the group which has budded off to

move into any available uninhabited area.
9

By consequence a

Firth, op. clt., 370.

10This relationship has apparently confused many eth­
nographers who have emphasized the district-territorial aspect
of ramified groups rather than the genealogical aspect (Of.
Buck, 1934: Burrows, 1939).

252

certain intermingling occurs between groups budded from dif­

ferent ramages in such areas.

The ramified system, as we have seen, is a framework
of social stratification.

Differential status is inherent in

the very process of ramification.

In every ramified society

each person has in fact a different rank, theoretically.

In

actuality one can recognize two or three groups of statuses,
"status levels" as we have called them, which are functionally

significant in terms of differential socio-economic preroga­
tives.

(See Chapter 1.)

The position of the upper status

level is intensified by a tendency toward status endogamy.

But since there is gradation of status, it is often difficult,

for a native or an ethnographer to determine where one status
level ends and another begins.

Seniority is the principle by

which priests are ranked as well as chiefs.

In all ramified

societies considered the priestly hierarchy follows the
genealogical hierarchy.

High priests come from high ranking

families; low priests come from families of lesser status.
The economic structure of ramified societies is iden­

tical to the ramage organization itself.

In land tenure, for

example, that system which we have designated "overlapping

stewardship" is found.

Every individual has an enduring usu-

fructory right to a section of the resources of the society.

But the head of every ramage is the manager or steward of all
lands used by individuals of his ramage.

Ramage heads, for

example, have a primary say in determining what is to be
planted or worked on, or by the power of tabu, what is not to

2b3

be planted or harvested.

In this system, lands of small

order ramages are incorporated in the lands of large order
ramages; and as the steward of the low order ramage manages
his lands, so does the head of the higher order ramage manage

the lands under him.

The system extends up to and includes

the paramount chief in whom is vested the stewardship of the

entire domain.

What Firth wrote of the Maori is true of rami­

fied Polynesian societies in general: "The greater social
11
group incorporates the rights of the lesser."
Thus a para­
mount chief can, for example, tabu any part of his domain--

that is, prohibit the harvesting of certain crops, planting in
certain land, fishing in the sea and the like.

The actual

power to make and enforce such decisions varies with the amount

of stratification in the society.

The ramified system is also the framework of the or­
ganization of production.

Management of lands and the power

of prohibiting access to certain of the resources exercised by
the ramage head, or steward, implies a certain control over

production.

As most production of food and goods is usually

by households, most of the regulation is by household heads.

There usually are, however, certain communal activities involv­

ing supra-household labor.

Such activities are initiated by

the chief of the ramage involved, by the society’s paramount

chief if the manpower of the entire group is to be mobilized.

Firth, Primitive Economics of the New Zealand
Maori, 1929, p. 3U3»

2b4

The demand for labor is passed down the hierarchy of group

heads until it reaches the household level.

As this is a

kinship society, such demands for labor are not regularized
and specified by law, and the obligation to render service is

an obligation toward a relative superior in rank, not to a

government.

In islands with high productivity such tradi­

tional obligations are often implemented by latent or actual

physical force brought to bear by high ranking chiefs.
Chapter II.)

(See

The support of supra-household labor is provided

by the ramage head who has initiated the undertaking.

The

role of ramage heads in the distribution system, as will

shortly be described, allows them to accumulate the necessary

food.

Even specialized production seems to be organized
along ramage lines.

Where there are many specialists (usually

they are not full-time professionals) the specialty is usually

maintained within households or related households.
The system of distribution is again carried out in a

ramage framework.

The type of distributional system is the

redistributive system by and large.

In the islands mentioned,

surpluses of food and other resources are constantly or per­
iodically (depending on the crop or time of production)
passed up the social hierarchy from household heads to heads
of larger ramages and often, to the paramount chief himself.

Heads of ramages thus form the apex of an organization of
accumulation; the paramount chief the apex of an accumulation

system of the entire society.

These accumulations are then

255

periodically redistributed among producers.

The periods of

distribution (and often of accumulation) are occasioned by the

many large religious ceremonies and feasts, life crises rites,
by intertribal visits and the like.

The scale of collection

and redistribution correlates with the status of the presiding
chief.

For example, the entire society usually attends, con­

tributes and receives food and goods if the life crises are in
the high chief's family.

A smaller gathering will mark a

rite de passage in a family of lesser rank.

It is for pur­

poses of accumulating the wherewithal to celebrate such

occasions that stewards exercise their tabu powers with re­
spect to the resources of their steward areas.
Accumulated foods and goods are also used by ramage

heads and the paramount chief to support extra-household
labor.

Because of their social status and role in the redis­

tributive system, it is usually ramage heads again who contact

specialists and support them during the periods in which they
12
engage in specialized production.

As a rule, the function of ramage heads as regulators

of production and distribution implies a certain liberation
from actual food production.

Variations around this rule

have already been discussed.

Several points may be made to differentiate this
economic system from those occurring in state-organized soci-

do not mean to imply that all distribution is
of a redistributive nature. There are many exchanges such
as those between affinal parties at life crises ceremonies
of commoners that can be called reciprocal.

25b

eties with feudal or money economies to which these Polynesian examples are, unfortunately, often likened. 13 The

ramified system is first and foremost kinship organized, this
is not true of the organization of feudal or money economies.

All obligations, such as those of rendering up surplus in the
accumulation-redistribution system are phrased on a kinship
basis.

For this reason there is no specified kind nor amount

of goods or services demanded by the ramage heads from their

lesser ranked kinsmen.

Producers are expected to give only

the kind and amount of goods they can spare.

"Tax," "tribute"

and "corvee" if they are to be applied to ramified Polynesian
societies and economics must be used then, only with reser­

vations .
Nor are there specified punishments for refusing to
render up surplus production.

As we have already noted the

general underlying scheme regulating the application of sanc­
tion to "wrongdoers" is not law, in the Austinian sense, but

collective retaliation.

Punishments by necessity vary with the

kinship backing and support musterable by the respective par­

ties involved.

Naturally a high chief, by virtue of his posi­

tion at the head of a large ramage, can muster a larger fight­
ing force than a man low in rank, and therefore inequalities

are apt to arise.

But the paramount chiefs had no monopoly of

13
The Polynesian type of socio-economic system de­
scribed here has often been called, misleadingly, "feudalism."
The terms "tax," "rent," "tribute," "king," "lord," "propri­
etor" have often been indiscriminately applied in discussions
of Polynesian economics.

257

coercive power and were not always able to punish recalci­
trants with impunity, especially if the recalcitrant was him­

self head of a large ramage.

And there were many mechanisms,

as we have seen, for preventing abuse of power by men of high

rank.
Thus the "political" organization follows closely upon
the genealogical framework of the society.

Although the actual

amount of authority exerted in the regulation of interpersonal
affairs varies from island to island, it is usually true that
ramage heads, in informal consultation with ramage members,
determine the course of action of the ramage as in punishing
a wrong inflicted upon them.

In matters concerning the entire

society, the paramount chief meets in informal council with the
men of high rank, the heads of large ramages.

If a course of

action is decided, such as a plan of warfare, the ramage heads

instigate the mobilization of the society by informing heads

of smaller ramages in their domain.

The process continues un­

til the household and individual levels are reached.
The religious aspects of ramage organization have al­

ready been indicated.

The position of ramage heads and es­

pecially of the paramount chief in the social hierarchy is
duplicated in beliefs of their divine descent, in their mana

and tabu elaborations, in the ornateness of their life crises
rites and the like.

The priesthood itself is a ranked struc­

ture closely duplicating secular statuses assigned through

descent.
This is the outline of the ramified social system and

2b8

its economic, political and religious correlates as it may

be generalized from its occurrence in the islands or groups of
Hawaii, Tonga, the Societies, Marquesas, Tikopia, New Zealand,

Easter Island, Mangareva and Mangala.

In each of these

islands there are variations around this pattern--some quite

The remainder of this chapter is given to brief out­

marked.

lines of the ramified systems in the islands mentioned.

The

role of the ramification system in economic, social and poli­
tical processes need not be detailed for each island as this

can readily be seen in the chapters (II-V) on the amounts of

stratification in the different islands.

Particular attention,

however, will be devoted to factors of flexibility of ramage

organization and the emergence of status differentials out of
ramified social structure.

Some suggestions will also be made

as to factors possibly operative in the development of local

variations in ramified systems.
Variations of Polynesian Ramified Systems

Tikopia^
Tikopia is the type site of ramified social struc­

ture .

Raymond Firth, in his brilliant study of Tikoplan

sociology, was the first field worker succinctly to isolate
ramage organization as a distinct form of kinship grouping and
is
to give it the terminology we have adopted here. " Most of

^^rlncipal Sources î Firth, 1930-1931a; 1930-1931b;
1936; 1940; 1950.
^Firth, 1936.

259

the usual characteristics of ramification, as they have been
discussed, were found in Tikopia.

There were three basic ramages, the household, the
multi-household paito and the multi-paito kainanga ("clan").

The paito was composed of a number of households tracing descent

from a common ancestor, and was headed (with the usual excep­

tions) by a male senior in descent.

There was always a ten­

dency, after the death of heads not on the senior line, to re­
vert to that line at the next headship, if possible.

The

paito was not a residential unit; it was scattered in various
villages, although any given village had a preponderance of
one paito.

But the household site of the common ancestor was

the ritual center of the group.

Paitos were created through

a process of fission in response to increase in numbers.

It

is impossible to specify the exact moment when paitos split

off from one another.
selves as one.”^

"Related paito frequently speak of them­

There were three types of paito; they all

could be found in the same kainanaga: offshoots of chiefly

houses, descendants of immigrants who married local people and
descendants of single local individuals.

stock were known as chiefly paito.

Paito from chiefly

However, only recent branch­

ings from chiefly lines were considered 'chiefly.'

"After some

time representatives of junior branches of the chiefly families cease to be considered as chiefly families (paito arlkl)."
16Ibid., 355.
17Ibid., 357.

2b0

Paito were composed of households bearing ancestral

names or names of a family group, and living on similarly

named plots.

Although the nuclear family was central to the

household, it often contained more relatives, as aged parents,
and may be best described as a patrilocal stem family.^

Households were formed by fission.

In most families, younger

brothers remained in the paternal household while older
brothers set up their own.

In chiefly families the opposite

situation pertained.

Kainanga were composed of a number of paito.
were four kainanga on Tikopia.

There

They were headed by chiefs,

ariki, who were, with a few (genealogical) exceptions, the
descendants of eldest sons through eldest sons of the senior
19
line of the kainanga.

The majority of members of each kainanga are of
common descent, their paito being offshoots in
various generations from the original stock....
Other paito have been incorporated into the clan
by assimilation, usually through the marriage of
an orphan or an immigrant from another island
with a daughter of the reigning chief. There are
many examples of this in the traditional litera­
ture.^

Kainanga had a common deity, a temple, and totemic tabus.
Each kainanga was associated with a definite crop and the

chief of the kainanga was responsible for the fertility of

that crop.
18

19

20

Marriage within the kainanga was permissible.

Cf. Ibid., 126.

See genealogies, ibid., 347-3^2.
Ibid., 362.

2bl

The second cousin on the maternal and paternal side was the
usual exogamic line.

Chiefs sometimes married nearer relatives.

The four kainanga chiefs were related and their rela­
tionship may be genealogically traced.

But Tikopian society

was limited in overall political centralization and this fact

is reflected in the genealogical reckoning of chiefly relation­

ship as well as in the land tenure and religious systems.

One

of the four ariki was considered senior in rank to the others.

This seniority extended to stewardship control over the in­
terior lake and all canoes on Tikopia.

Contrary to our gener­

alized description, however, the overlapping stewardship of

this chief did not extend to land.

The highest order of stew­

ardship in land extended only to the four ariki.

In ceremonial

matters, the senior chief was believed to control the fertility
of the land and sea, and his ancestral god was the highest in
the pantheon; yet each chief, as we have indicated, had cere­

monial control over the fertility of one crop—a ceremonial

division of labor not harmonious with centralized political

control.

This only partial emergence of centralized political

control is manifest in genealogies by the fact that although
the chiefs are related, the relationship (as far back as it is

traceable) is an affinal one.

They were not considered descen­

dants of brothers of which the ancestor of the chief senior

in rank was the eldest.
Nor in the actual regulation of interpersonal affairs

did the senior ariki exert greater influence than any other

ariki.

Firth believes that the seniority in rank of one ariki

262

resulted from a centralization of ritual functions in one

of a number of immigrant groups which populated Tikopia.

As

an historical fact this may be true.
Another departure from the ideal ramified pattern oc­
curred in Tikopia.

There was a relative dearth of flexibility

in the ranking structure.

Differential increase of ramages

was allowed; the four kainanga numbered respectively (in 1926):

443, 3^5# 384 and 89.

Neither ambi-patrilineal descent nor

military force was used to rectify the differential increase
of ramages or to equalize the number of followers under men of

equivalent status.

Women usually do not appear on genealogies,

and there was a relatively low frequency of adoptions which

resulted in severing connections with an original ramage.

It

is notable that the custom of allowing free use of available
garden lands to any member of the island community is a mechan­

ism by which population pressure resulting from growth in
familial size could be remedied, without necessitating change

in the ramage structure.

The genealogical system was the framework of social
stratification.

The two status levels we have described,

ariki and commoners, were brought about through the operation
of the seniority principle.

Some commoner families seem to

have enjoyed greater ritual prestige than others.

These are

families said to be autochthonus in origin, yet integrated into
the kainanga structure.

263

New Zealand
The Maori are a well-defined ramified social struc­

ture ,

The clarity of the ramified system is enhanced by the

close correspondence of ramages with localities.

Villages were

the rule here, and villages were usually inhabited by large

order ramages, hapu.

This correlation of kinship and terri­

tory perhaps caused Burrows to hold the opinion that ramifica­
tion was most developed in New Zealand of all Polynesian
22
areas.

The usual aspects of ramification as primogenitural
succession (with the customary exceptions) and its implications

for seniority, growth by fission, and so forth, were present.

The ramification system pervaded the entire social group with

the exception of war captives.
Three major ramage-orders may be distinguished: the

whanau, the hapu and the iwi.

The whanau was a patrilocal

extended family, sometimes a fraternal joint family headed by

the eldest brother, and sometimes a stem family headed by a

patriarch.

It was the household group ; it ranged up to ninety-

two members at the extreme upper limit.

Whanaus budded off

petition Among Primitive Peoples (M. Mead, ed.), 1937;

264

from each other.

A number of whanau comprised a hapu, a

group led by a chief, aho ariki, who was the direct descen­
dant through eldest sons of the common ancestor of the various

whanau.

Hapu contained at least several hundred individuals.

The largest ramage, politically and economically, was the iwi,
or "tribe," composed of a number of hapu.

The iwi was led by

the aho ariki who was the direct descendant of the founder of
the tribe, a member of an immigrant canoe party.

Tribes

descendent from members of the same canoe were called waka and
occasionally formed military alliances, but the bond between

them was often more sentimental than political or economic.

Tribes (iwi) were the political units and also, as the group
in which accumulation and redistribution were coterminous, the
economic units. They numbered, very roughly, 3 >500 on the
23
average.
Both the hapu and the iwi were called by the name

of the common ancestor prefixed by Ngati, Ngai, or Ati, mean­

ing 'descendants of.'

The ranking hapu in an iwi therefore

had the same name as the iwi.

Both hapu and iwi tended to be

endogamous thus preventing dispersal of land interests to
strangers.

Marriage was generally prohibited between persons

three generations or less removed from a common ancestor.
Differential availability of land in ramages result­

ing from disproportionate ramage growth was evidently a frequent
23

The native population of New Zealand was near
100,000. Best estimated that there were 18 major tribes and
numerous triblets.(Best, 192^, 1, 354*)

265

possibility.

By consequence, ramage realignment effected

through tracing descent to the mother’s father’s ramage
(accompanied often by ambi-patrilocal residence) was frequent.

There was a general custom of tracing descent both to a
mother’s and father’s ramage.

Depending on the availability

of land an individual would be aligned primarily in one or
the other, and eventually connection with the ramage whose

lands were not used nor resided in would be dropped.

Firth

calls this ambilateral descent; the term evidently refers to
the phenomena we are calling ambi-patrilineal descent.

The

frequency and institutionalization of tracing descent to the
mother’s father’s ramage may have been greater than in most

ramified systems.

The mechanisms of change in ramage composi­

tion evidently usually solved the problems of differential

growth of families, and thus maintained the ranking system
status quo.

Sporadically, however, a higher order ramage ex­

panded in power at the expense of other groups.

Buck cites a

case wherein one hapu rose in power within a tribe and assumed

In the process the tribe was renamed by the
211
name of the rising hapu.

leadership in it.

The ramified system formed the basis of rank differen­
tial and status levels emerged from it.

The top level was the

chiefly one composed of hapu and Iwi leaders and their immedi­

ate relatives, rangatira.

A second level was composed of more

distant relatives of the chiefly line who had diverged from it

^Buck, 1949, 335-36.

through the normal process of primogeniture and ramification.

This, level tutua or ware, contained the great majority of

Maori.

Prisoners of war were used as menials.

However, they

were quickly absorbed into the tribal structure, as the females
were taken by tutua as wives and by chiefs as secondary wives,

and offspring of such unions were assimilated as tutua.

Since

all free members of the iwi were delicately graded on a gene­

alogical hierarchy, it is difficult both for the ethnologist
and native to discriminate the point of difference between

rangatira and tutua status.

Best reported: "Inasmuch as all

members of a tribe are connected with well born families, then
it becomes a difficult matter to define the ware or tutua
class. ... Never have I met a native who would admit that he
25
was a member of that class."

Ramages split off from one another by fission.

The fundamental unit (called (lineage”) was, accord­

ing to Gifford, the large order ramage with the title, ’family
descendant from so-and-so.’

The usual name for this ramage,

of which there were thirteen in late times (averaging about
1800 members each) was haa.

The haa were headed by the man on

the direct line of descent from the common ancestor of the haa.
Furthermore, the various haa traced descent to ancestors who

were genealogically related (see Diagram 2).

Thus the entire

society could be placed in a genealogical framework.
Major haa were split into smaller branches; their
heads usually traced relationship to brothers.

These smaller

branches were further subdivided into yet smaller, but still
28
probably supra-household, ramages.
The sub-haa ramages are
called haa, faahinga or matakali.

The referents of these terms

today are not ramages of particular orders of size, although
Gifford holds the opinion that such might have been the case

in aboriginal times.

extended family headed by the senior male.

Ramages tended to be localized in a number of hamlet-communi­

Gifford also feels that the large numbers of

Ambi-patrilineal descent (with ambi­

tion, especially by the father’s sister, were the major mechan­

isms by which ramage realignment was effected.

Flexibility out­

In an

2b9

The ranking system and its crystallization into sta­

tus levels was in the main determined by seniority of descent.
The paramount chiefs, the heads of the various haa and per­
haps of the major haa divisions formed the eiki, the chiefly
status level.

There were, at the time of contact, three para­

mount chiefs.

The highest, the Tui Tonga, was the direct

descendant of the gods.

Gifford and Williamson both find evi­

dence that the Tui Tonga once exercised secular authority, at

least over his home ramage on Tongatabu.

At about 1470 A.D.

he lost his temporal functions and became instead a highly
sacred priest-chief.^ The temporal powers were delegated,
according to belief and the genealogical record, to a younger

brother of the Tui Tonga who was given the title of Tui Haa

Takalaua, and from whom a haa sprung.

This office and line

later gave rise to yet another office delegated to a younger

brother, the Tui Kanokupolu, from which sprung another of the

modern haa.

The Tui Kanokupolu title gradually usurped the

temporal prerogatives of the Tui Haa Takalaua, a process which
continued under European influence.

The situation has many

analogies with the dual stratification system of Easter Island

and Mangala where a militarily dominant chieftain held
supreme temporal power quite outside of the genealogical rank­
ing structure.

Here, however, the division of sacred and secu­

lar power was maintained, purportedly, within the genealogical
^Ibid., 56f; Williamson, 1924, 1, 167-68.

270

system.

The situation in Tonga is made even more intriguing

by the fact that the office of Tui Haa Takalaua was created

precisely at the time that Tongatabu was expanding into Samoa,

Futuna and other islands and unifying the entire Tongan group
politically.3"

Aside from the chiefly status level, one can distin­
guish two others, tua, "commoners” and matapule, "chief’s
attendants."

The tua status was created through the normal

process of primogeniture and the progressive lowering in sta­

tus of descendants of younger brothers.

The majority of the

population were tua.

Matapule occupied an intermediate status

between eike and tua.

All matapule were outranked by eike,

although matapule were ranked among themselves according to

the chief they served.

It seems that the ranking of matapule

only partly derived from seniority, as Gifford finds evidence
that matapule almost always are descendants of immigrants from
36
other islands who attached themselves to chiefs.
Mariner

reports, however, "[Matapule] rank is from inheritance; and
they are supposed to have been, originally, distant relatives
of the nobles, or to have descended from persons eminent for
37
experience and wisdom...."
Mariner also reports the status
of mua, junior relatives of matapule and senior relatives of

tua.

Gifford, however, was unable to corroborate Mariner.

Marquesas

39

Primogeniture, elaboration of the ceremonial position

of the first-born male, rank based on seniority, growth of
ramages by fission, and other like characteristics of the
ramified social system, were found in the Marquesas.

The household and the tribe were the major ramages;
there evidently was no ramage of an intermediate order.

The

household was composed of a patrilocal extended family headed

by the male senior in descent.

Households of senior lines in

the tribe were larger than other households.

The "tribe" con­

sisted of a number of households tracing descent from a common

ancestor of which the chief, hakaiki, was the direct descendant.
The tribe, about 1,500 people, thus formed a genealogical sys­

tem.

It was the usual economic and political unit.

In some

cases, as on IJa Pou, there were evidently supra-tribal groups.
But on the southern islands, from which most data comes, the
tribe was usually the unit.

All Marquesan tribes traced their

descent to the same ancestor, but this elaboration of the
genealogical reckoning was not reflected in political or

economic life, and is an indication, rather, of a common

Gifford, op. cit., 109.
39
Principal Sources: Drioult-Girard, 1940; Handy,
1923; Linton, 1939; Williamson, 192^.

272

historical origin.

There was no exogamy or endogamy pertain­

ing to ramages per se, although the tribe was usually en­

dogamous.

Marriage with parallel cousins was prohibited, with

cross cousins, preferred.
The ramage structure showed a great deal of flexi­

bility.

Individuals switched ramage allegiance through adop­

tions as well as by ambi-patrilineal descent.

The frequency

of permanent alignment with the mother’s father’s ramage was
evidently comparable to its frequency among the Maori, for
here also was connection with the mother’s group regularly re­
membered and recognized.

increase in the size of households led to increased

productivity and distributions and by this means, increase
in prestige.

Unlike most other islands considered in this

chapter, however, increased size of households did not result
in physical struggles for ascendancy.

Rather the increased

prestige seems to have been manifested in rearrangement of

genealogical reckoning.

Due to widespread tracing of descent

and the maintenance of connections with the mother’s father’s

ramage,
.. there was always some point in the line of
descent at which one of the ancestors was higher
than a contemporary ancestor of some other indi­
vidual. Genealogies could thus be used to justify
actual social relations. The various households
went up and down in social prestige and position,
and household heads selected which of the possible
relationship terms they would use on the basis of
which was superior or inferior. Because of the in­
breeding [i.e., intermarriage within the relatively
small "tribes"], it was always possible for a person

273

actually on top to find a line of descent that was
higher at some point than any other line.4^
There was a mechanism for dealing with households

threatening the supremacy of the chiefs.

The heir of the ris­

ing household was either adopted by the chief, or the eldest
daughter was married to the chief's eldest son.

This usually

dissolved the upstart group and the status quo was maintained.

It is noteworthy again that all these rearrangements resulting
from increased size took place peacefully and within the gene­

alogical system; a junior line did not seize the power of a
senior line, but became senior through manipulation of familial

connections--if the situation called for it.

The status positions arose out of the ramified struc­
ture.

Two levels can be demarcated.

High chiefs (hakaiki)

and their near relatives formed the chiefly level; the rest of
the populace, more remotely related to the main line, formed

’the people,1 mata-ei-nana.

Refugees were absorbed, not en­

slaved, and captives were eaten--thus a third level was absent.

Hawaii^

found in Hawaii including primogeniture, elaborated life

and junior relationship and descent lines, and the like.
The basic ramage was the household, usually a patri­
local extended family, apparently somewhat larger than usual

in the case of chiefs.

The household was probably headed by

the male senior in descent.

Beyond the household, the number

of higher order ramages is uncertain.

The largest order ramage

was the island led by a chief, alii nui, who was the direct
descendant of the founder or ancestor of the group.

was usually the political and economic unit.

genealogies all the way back to this ancestor.

Island popula­

tions averaged twenty to thirty thousand or more.

An idea of

the complexity of the ramage organization may be gained from

Malo’s breakdown of nine steward areas of diminishing order of
size between the island community and the household.^

These

of households tracing common descent.

On the other hand, due to the large populations involved, and
the flexibility of the ramage structure, genealogical connec­

tions may have slipped into the background.

Such indeed seems

to be implied by Handy when he writes that the "tribalism" of

Ka’u was unique in Hawa 11.4-7

No ramage seems to have been

endogamous or exogamous per se.

The usual restriction on

first-cousin marriage may have generally prevailed, although

high ranking people married closer in.

The paramount chief

often married his sister or half-sister.
Flexibility in ramage structure is indicated by the
custom of adoption into ramages and by methods of tracing
descent ambi-patrilineally (revealed by the presence of women
on genealogies).

Structural flexibility was in fact an in­

stitutionalized aspect of the ranking system.

At the acces­

sion of each paramount chief (theoretically, at least), con­

trol over steward areas and over the ramages occupying them
was redistributed.

This distribution extended to relatives

of the paramount chief within ten generations of a common

ancestor.^

Those who found themselves under a head eleven

degrees of consanguinity from the paramount chief, of necessity
were realigned under a new alii or konohikl.

In actuality, this

distribution did not result in marked changes in the ranking
structure as most konohikl remained in their previous posi­

tions.^

But warfare between high order ramages, even within

an island community, was a frequent occurrence and could cause

drastic changes in the ranking system of the defeated groups

and thus the island.

Stewardship and positions of authority

Ibid., 172.
^Malo, op. cit., 192.

^Dole, op. cit., 6.

277

over vanquished peoples were distributed among the conquer­
ors .

Thus here a conquest hierarchy was superimposed over a

genealogical hierarchy.

This type of warfare may have

affected succession to the paramount chieftainship, but it did
not result in separation of temporal and sacred authorities,

or the abandonment of an inheritance succession pattern to the
position of the temporal paramount chief.

Conflict in lesser

order ramages between junior and senior lines, occasioned by
differential increase of families, does not appear to have been

very frequsnt--nor to have produced a great deal of felxibility

in rank in these lower levels.
The genealogical structure was the framework on which

were hung various positions of status, and differential privi­
lege.

Three broad status levels can be distinguished out of

the overall gradation implied by seniority.

The highest status

level was composed of the alii, the paramount chief and his

younger brothers and sisters, extending perhaps to those re­
lated within two or three degrees of consanguinity to the

paramount chief.

Beyond alii, those related within ten de­

grees of remove from a common ancestor with the paramount chief
formed, as we have seen, the households of the konohiki, a

middle status level.

The third, largest and lowest status

level was occupied by makaainana, "commoners," descendants of
long lines of younger brothers.

Society Islands

A ramified system with primogeniture and its associ­

ated implications of seniority, the use of the genealogical
tree as the framework of social rank, elaboration of life
crises rites for first-born children and the development of

ramages by fission were all present in the Societies.^

The numbers and sizes of ramages is best known for
Tahiti, but here the documentation is poor.

Mataeinaa was

the name of the two largest ramages and of the districts in

which they were located.

There were three large mataeinaa on

Tahiti, averaging about 60,000 people each.

Each of the three

was heàded by the senior male of the area.

There were twenty-

8lower order mataeinaa, about 7 »000+ people on the average,
grouped into the larger mataeinaa.

The twenty-six were the

political units and for most purposes, the accumulation­
redistribution units.

The larger mataeinaa, of which the

Teva division of Tahiti would be an example, functioned mainly

as warfare confederations; whether or not they represented co­
terminous accumulation-distribution networks is uncertain.

Ramification is evident in relationship terms.
Handy’s explanation of the extent of the term matahiapo
gives a clue to the entire ramified framework of the soci­
ety: First born, male or female. Also applied to all re­
presentatives of a family stock descended in the line of
first-borns. Thus, if I belong to a matahiapo family it
means that at least one parent was a first born right back
to the god Taaroa. My first born will carry on the matahiapo
teina ’younger brother’ or ’ junior in rank'.”

Within a smaller mataeinaa, as Papara, were yet smaller units

called amulraa ("bunch") or pupu ("group"), and headed by
chiefs succeeding by primogeniture.

There was also a ramage

which Handy calls an "estate," which was evidently a section
of an amulraa; it was called ala or ala tupuna.

Handy be­

lieves that the word ati signified the extended family group
$4
among the lower status levels,
although it is our opinion
that this may have been the general term for the household.

Ramages, as implied, were localized, especially large order
mataeinas.

Villages, however, do not appear to have existed

qua villages but as communities of dispersed hamlets.

The ramage system was reflected in the system of an­

cestral marae or temples.

Each family had a marae, while the

large ramage—district marae, the temple of the senior family,
was considered the parent marae from which the others branched

off.

There was an institutionalized method by which marae

branched from each other which illustrates the growth of ramages
by fission.

When a household or family divided and the ramage

lands were partitioned between them, a stone from the old

marae was used as a cornerstone for the new.

The new marae

was consecrated to the same god as the old, while the new
ramage head took an hereditary title associated with the older
marae.
There is some evidence of flexibility of ramage

^Ibid., 11.

280

alignment of individuals.

Williamson cites reports which in­

dicate flexibility of patrilineal descent, perhaps what we have

called ambi-patrilineality.

Also, females in the absence of

male heirs, could carry and pass on titles and land, indicating

that their children probably would align themselves with their
mother’s father’s ramage.

Finally, adoption into ramages was

practiced.
But flexibility of this type did not check the differ­

ential expansion of ramages, especially high-order ramages.
Military ascendancy of one group over another could result in

the subjugation of the defeated, a process calling for re­
arrangement of the ranking structure.

It is Handy’s conten­

into power through conquest in Tahiti and in several other

Easter Island D

A system of ramages encompassing the entire island
with the usual customs of primogeniture, seniority of the

line of eldest sons, elaboration of life crises rites for
eldest sons, growth of ramages by fission, were all present

on Easter Island.
Several orders of ramages, growing through the usual
process of segmentation can be distinguished.^ The smallest

was the household, a patrilocal extended family probably

headed by the senior male and composed, at present, of about
nine people.

Aboriginally, households of thirty or more were

apparently not uncommon.

A number of households tracing

descent to a common ancestor formed a "sub-tribe" headed by
the direct descendant of this ancestor.

A number of simi­

larly related "sub-tribes" formed the tribe, mata, of which
there were about ten, averaging 300 to ^.OO people each.

Most

of the population traced descent to a common ancestor as many
tribes were considered to be offspring of sons and grandsons

of the first settler and chief.

It is both Métraux* and

Williamson's opinion^ that those mata that were not descen­

shoots of mata that were, so that eventually the whole mata

system could be phrased on a genealogical basis.

No ramage

(with exceptions in the tribe on the main line of descent,

see below) was endogamous or exogamous, the usual rule was
prohibition of marriage between relatives closer than second

cousins.

This ramified structure was the basis of the gene­

alogical ranking system.

The mata descendent on the direct

line from the first settler supplied the paramount chief of
the island, the ariki mau, in the person of the eldest son of

that line.

Other members of the chiefly mate were for the

most part similar in status to members of comparable ramages,
although they appeared to have enjoyed some greater prestige

from their descent than members of other tribes.

Other chiefs,

on the main line of their respective mata, shared the upper

status level with the paramount chief.

opinion that a professional class of warriors, matatoa who,
as distinguished from mata chiefs, exercised the real power
in Easter Island.^ The assertion that there was a profes­

sional class of warriors rests on tenuous evidence.

71

The matatoa referred to then might have been leaders of

The dual ranking system in paramount

chieftainship was manifest in the institution of the bird72
man cult.
At yearly intervals there was an institutionalized

scramble among the leaders of the militarily ascendent tribe
for the egg of a certain bird.

The finding of this egg by

one of these men or his servant meant that the man became
bird-man for a year, the incarnation of the god Makemake, and

the holder of many economic, social and political privileges.
The paramount chief, head of the genealogical hierarchy of the

island did not participate in the egg hunt.

The position of

the bird-man, therefore, was one of coexistent supremacy with
the arikl mau.

It is the contention of Williamson and of many others
cited by him that the bird-man competition was the method by

It seems unjustifiable to reject

After all, the

giant stone heads or the bird-man cult itself do not occur
elsewhere, yet certainly they existed in Easter Island.

The

reductio ad absurdum of Metraux' argument would be that any
cultural item or institution could not exist on any Polynesian
island unless it also existed on some other Polynesian island.

Absolutely no room is left by such reasoning for Independent

development.

that emerge from warfare and the military dominance of certain

The usual aspects of ramification, primogeniture,

rank distinctions based on seniority, and the like, were
present in Mangala.
The basic ramage was the household, a patrilocal ex­

tended family termed matakainanga.

although many exceptions to genealogical succession resulted

The tribes probably averaged about two or three

An unusual feature of the MangaIan ramage organiza­

tion was exogamy of the tribal groups.

Buck feels that this

custom arose through a process of institutionalization of the
custom by which the tribes of the first settlers, Ngarlki,
supplied wives to immigrant groups.

Another feature in which

Mangala departs from the ideal ramified system is the lack of
genealogical system embracing all the large-order ramages, the

tribes.

Certain tribes traced descent to the reported origi­

nal settler, and supplied the two paramount priest-chiefs of
the island; but others maintained traditions of descent from
immigrant groups.

The lack of genealogical unity is reflected

in lack of overall overlapping stewardship (in name, at any
rate), despite the fact that the island formed an economic unit.

The tribes were more or less localized in the districts

of the island of which they were allegedly the original inhabi­

Warfare seems to have been a mechanism by which dif­

War leaders

What is probably

chosen as a ramage head in preference to a poor warrior, de­

Thus Buck writes:

A hereditary chief could not rely on his senior­
ity alone, but, to maintain his power in the
tribe, he had to be a warrior as well. Te Uanuku
of Ngati-Vara held the mangais through his per­
sonal prowess as much as through his being the
first born son of Mautara. None of his descen­
dants displayed military leadership, and though
his family is recognized as the kiko mua, the
leadership in the tribe passed to junior branches
of the family.

Tribes and families rose and fell, fought and grabbed suprem­
acy outside of the system of genealogical priority.

In this

sense is the prestige of toa best understood, and truly, as
Buck wrote:

The power of a chief was influenced by the num­

pended on the male increase in his particular fam­

could support in his establishment. Seniority of
blood, though revered, was somewhat theoretical as
compared with the practical advantages of number.$3

manaia, or TLM as we have abbreviated the euphemistic title,

On the upper levels of the ranking struc­

ture, as in Easter Island, was found coexistence of the system

of genealogical priority with one based on military might.

At

the top of the genealogical system stood the two priest-chiefs,
members of the Nagariki, tribes descendant from the original

settlers of Mangala.

At one time, according to Buck, the

Ngariki held supreme secular authority as well, but this

authority waned and the secular leadership was made one of the
spoils of war.^4

Succession to the TLM (marked by an elabor­

ate installation) went to the leader of the tribe which had
come into military prominence through conquest of another tribe.
The title was only temporary; the rise of another tribe brought
in a new TLM.

The longest recorded reign was twenty-five years.

The dominant tribe took possession of conquered lands and
assumed supremacy over its remaining inhabitants.

Lands held

by other tribes and the supremacy of their chiefs were not af­

fected, although all recognized the suzerainty of the TLM.

In the early nineteenth century, however, something
radically different happened.

The two dominant tribes divided

the island into six districts and assumed positions of authority

The division took place under the

Pangemiro* s actions are peculiar

when viewed against previous Mangalan institutions.

Not only

was there a sudden centralization of the secular domain under
him, but also in the sacred realm, for the two high priest­
chief titles were combined.

Pangemiro acceded in 1814 with­

out battle; when an advantageous alliance was made by him,
the chiefs quit the previous TLM.

After Pangemiro had quelled

an internal revolt in 1823, his position as TLM was secured;

the division of the realm was apparently not made before this
date.

The abrupt centralization of power makes one suspicious

that European influence might have been involved.

The suspi­

cion is heightened by the fact that Cook visited Mangala in

1777•

Furthermore, a whaler’s captain landed at the beginning

of the century, and another in 1814.

Finally, the year 1823

marked not only the year when Pangemiro consolidated or rein­

forced his TLM position, but within two months after this
event, missionaries came to Mangala.
That centralized power administered on a territorial,

non-tribal basis was an autocthonus development is, therefore,

a moot point at best.
The dual stratification system based on genealogy on

one hand, war on the other, was the framework out of which

distilled social status.

Summary and Interpretations

The ramified system has been described as a gene­

alogical social system operating, through prImogenitural suc­
cession and its implications for seniority of descent, as a
framework of social differentiation.

The ramified systems on

a number of Polynesian islends have been outlined in some
detail.

Departures from the ideal ramification pattern have

been noted.

In this section those variations will be summar­

ized and reasons will be advanced toward an explanation of them.

One set of variations revolves around the existence
of a single genealogical tree structure which encompasses the

whole socio-economic system.

On Tikopia and Mangala where

the island was the economic unit, ramages did not all trace

descent to a single common ancestor.

In Tahiti the common

ancestor of the ruling group, aril, was apparently different

from the ancestors of the raatira and manahune.

On the other

hand, the unit economic system in Tonga, Hawaii, Easter Island

301

New Zealand, the Marquesas and, with qualifications, Mangareva,
was also characterized by genealogical unity.

The most obvi­

ous explanation of these differences would be an historical
one, namely that Tikopian and Mangaian ramages were not of
unitary origin but descended from a number of immigrant

groups, as did the Tahitian aril, while on the other islands
the populations descended from just a few people who all immi­

grated at the same time.

This seems, in fact, to be close to

the true story as it is revealed in native traditions and
genealogies found in the ethnographic reports.
But there are exceptions to this rule.

We know that

in many islands immigrants have been incorporated into the

existing ramage structure, as Tongan matapule descendants were
incorporated, without setting up discrete ramages.

The limi­

tations of the historical explanation are highlighted by con­

sideration of those islands populated from a single migration,

yet which are composed, at the time of discovery, of a number
of discrete social and economic groups with unrelated common
ancestors.

New Zealand is an example.

not have overall genealogical unity?

Why did New Zealand
The answer to this ques­

tion would, it is submitted, contribute to our understanding
of the lack of overall ramification on Tikopia and Mangala,

for the answer must lie in the conditions dictating the separa­

tion of Maori into discrete autonomous units of about 3,500
people, the ivl.

One factor, it may be postulated, is that

the productivity was such that coterminus accumulations and

redistributions of strategic goods was limited to a group of

302

about that size.

The probability of this hypothesis is raised

by the fact that populations of horticultural Maori tribes

were more numerous than those of tribes dependent on the less
97
productive techniques of collecting and fishing.
As large-

scale redistributive activities were usually carried out along
ramage lines, the productivity of the society may be seen as
a conditioning factor preventing the development of all encom­

passing genealogical structures—coupled with an original
diversity of origin of the ramages.
In the Society Islands, genealogical diversity was

of another kind.

There apparently was a separation in descent

between the ruling ramage of aril and the ramages of subordi­
nate groups headed by rangatira.

an outcome of aril conquest.

Handy interprets this to be

We do not feel this to be a com­

plete explanation, for in other islands, as Mangareva or New

Zealand, conquered people and their offspring were eventually
absorbed into the genealogies at the lowest level.

However,

we have not been able to discern any particular characteristic
of Society Island culture which might throw light on the matter.

A second series of variations within the ramified sys­

tems revolves around the matter of flexibility of ramage struc­

ture and alignments.

All ramage organizations, with the excep­

tion of Tikopia, show the type of flexibility in which families

or individuals in ramages headed by men of low status switch
allegiance by various means to ramages under men of high status.
Best, Maori Agriculture, Dornin. Mus. Bull.. 9,
1925.

303

There was not only a structural necessity operative here,
there was also an economic necessity--namely that as more
people are descendent from remote relatives than from recent
relatives of the chiefly line, those people in ramages under
men of low status undoubtedly would be exerting pressure on

land resources.

Ramage realignment through adoption, ambl-

patrilineal reckoning of descent and the like were peaceful
means of solving this problem.

In some cases, as we have

seen, more war-like methods were utilized to remedy the in­
equities of differential familial growth.

As a result the

genealogical ranking structure might be upset, not only at

the bottom but at the upper levels.

A number of questions

arise out of these variations in flexibility which might be
considered here :
1)

Why did differential familial increase lead to in­
ternal disputes over succession in low order ramages
in Mangala, Mangareva and Easter Island, whereas

peaceful means were found for coping with this prob­

lem elsewhere?
2)

Why were changes in the top order of the ranking
system most frequent in Mangala and Easter Island,

and why the dual stratification system on these
islands and as well in the Teva district of Tahiti?
3)

Why was there a relative lack of flexibility in

every respect in the ranking structure of Tikopia?
These questions will be examined in order.

304

One would suspect that internal disputes between

junior and senior lines on lower ramage levels resulted from

differential familial growth in areas of limited land and
sea resources.

greater than on other islands.

2)

The rise of a large-order ramage in some islands

resulted in a forceful usurpation of power from the ramage

at the top of the genealogical hierarchy.

In Easter Island

and Mangala this was a frequent occurrence and the head of
the rising ramage was recognized as the temporal ruler of
the island, while the head of the ramage directly descended
from the founder and gods of the island retained his sacred­

ness and supremacy in the religious realm.

A similar event

apparently took place several hundred years ago in Mangareva

but the new secular head also appropriated religious supremacy.

In Hawaii a rising line could assume united rule over an
island as in Mangareva, while sporadic instances of the same

also happened in New Zealand.

It did,

There was then a general tendency for large order

In Mangala and Easter Island this ten­

the temporal, military aspect was an impermanent office as

As already indicated, the cus­

the structure, in that there were no struggles over available

land.

to Mangareva.

Both were crowded areas with population den­

per square mile.

In Mangareva there was fre­

lands—in Tikopia, none.

The two patterns seem alternative

solutions to a single adaptive problem, that of high popula­

tion pressure.

The existence of one particular localized type

of land much more valuable than all others may have influenced

Mangareva toward the solution adopted there.

Good land, while

scarce in Tikopia (the island was only three square miles),
was scattered, and fertility differentials between the scat­
tered areas were perhaps not so great as in Mangareva.

The

Tikopians were well aware of population pressure and used

every means short of warfare to curb quarrels and struggles

Again this was not the case in Hangareva.

The ramified system has been outlined as a general

pattern.

Variations around that pattern have been described

in the local Polynesian ramified social organizations.

Some

suggestions were made that might contribute to an understand­

ing of these local variations.

Particular attention has been

given to aspects of ramification relevant to the emergence of

social stratification structures.

It remains to examine other

forms of social organization in Polynesia from a similar view­

point.

CHAPTER IX
DESCENT LINE SYSTEMS OF SOCIAL ORGANIZATION
AND STRATIFICATION IN POLYNESIA

The aboriginal social organizations of Samoa,
Futuna and Uvea offer a contrast to the ramified systems

discussed in Chapter VIII.

To our knowledge, the contrast

has been largely overlooked in the ethnological literature
of Polynesia.

As distinguished from ramified systems,

the social organizations of Samoa, Uvea, and Futuna were

marked by a system of titles held by members of a number
of more or less discrete patrilineal descent groups or

descent lines, but operating in localized territorial frame­
works as villages and districts.

Status did not depend--

or better, did not ’depend1 in the same way as in the rami­

fied system--on distance from the main line of descent, but
on the traditional position of one’s title in the village
hierarchy of titles.

We propose to call this system a

”descent line" system of social organization and social

stratification.

As only three variants of the descent line

system are considered here, each will be described in de­

tail without first describing the system as a general pat­

tern with variations.

310

Samoa
Samoan social organization was characterized by a

system of titles which, although accruing to patrilineal

descent lines, took on hierarchial significance in the local
context of the village and of larger territorial groups.

To these titles accrued privileges of sitting on certain

councils (fono), a certain position of precedence in cere­
monial kava servings, and a certain position in the order of
title-calling which served as a customary preface to impor­
tant social activities.

As Margaret Mead has described this

titular system:

Names and their rank, not individuals are units
of construction. ... The individual may move
about at will, but the name, the post, the posi­
tion in the kava ceremony, and the right to make
speeches, are constants, sustaining a fixed re­
lationship to all other names in the series.2
A significant difference from ramified systems is connoted

by this passage, for in ramified systems it is precisely the
individual which is "the unit of construction" and the rela­

tionship between titles is dependent upon the genealogical re­
lationship between people.

In Samoa titles were held in paternal descent lines,

groups of people tracing descent through a particular line of
^Principal Sources: Buck, 1930; Buelow, 1902;
Coulter, 19U1; Keesing, 1934» Kraemer, 1902; Mead, 1928,
1930, 1950; Schultz, 1911; Schultz-Ewerth, 1929; Stair,
188?; Turner, 1884; Williamson, 1924*

2Mead, 1930, 11.

311

males.

Unlike the ramified systems, succession was not neces­

sarily primogenitural, but could go to any son or brother of
the former holder of the title.

Suitability for leadership

and propinquity of residence seem to have been the final cri­
teria of selection.

Occasionally the title would go to a

person of a different descent line, as a sister’s son, in

which case ambi-patrilineal reckoning of descent was resorted
to.
Titles conferred positions in the village council or

fono.

Only title holders, matai, could sit on this council

and each held a rank in the council based on the traditional

relationship of his title to others of the fono.

Position of

the title in fonos was thus the ultimate determinate of sta­

tus.

Titles were of two types: chiefs (alii) and talking

Talking chiefs were the ceremonial atten­

chiefs (tulafale).
dants of chiefs.

Each talking chief was attached to a

specific chief and took his status according to the title of
the chief.

Therefore, among matais, chiefs and talking chiefs

held every grade of status; chiefs did not as a whole outrank
talking chiefs as a whole.

Talking chiefs were the speakers

of the chiefs, kept their genealogies and acted as distribu­

tors in ceremonies.
The descent line system of Samoa and its hierarchy
of titles may be considered from two angles, from the view­

point of the structure of kinship groups and secondly, from

consideration of local groupings and fonos.

Samoans were

grouped into families, mostly composed of patrilineal

31Ü

relatives and their spouses.

The families were called aInga,

a term which might have more of a bilateral emphasis, or sa,

"family of," having the 'line of patrilineal descent’ conno­

tation.

A descent line usually had a number of sections

scattered in several villages.

Each sa carried certain

titles in fonos which were bestowed upon the heads of local
sections of the sa.

A sa section in a village was composed

of fifty or more people grouped into a number of households
of patrilocal (with some exceptions) extended families.

A

sa of a village might be divided into named branches, although
the terminology of reference for these smaller sections is

not clear.

The head of the sa held the leading title which

the line could bestow.
Any given line having sections in a number of vil­

lages gave the appearance of a number of ramages of varying
size with heads of varying rank.

In truth, the ranking of

titles within a sa probably stems from the division of the

group into sections when one village branches off from another.
However, this relationship of rank within the sa was not
based on seniority of descent and the development of senior

and cadet branches through primogeniture.

The connection be­

tween sections of a descent line was variously phrased gene­
alogically, even affinal connections could be made; descent

of related sa sections was not necessarily traced through a
3
pair of brothers.

^Cf. Williamson, op. clt., 11, 3f.

313

More significant, in distinguishing descent lines
from ramage organization, is the fact that the relationship

in rank between village or district titles held by different

sa, or sections thereof, was not based upon differences in
seniority in the descent of the different lines demonstrable
on some genealogical tree.

Rather, the different lines were

more or less distinct genealogicallyPriority on the basis

of age of the sa sections might have been the determining
factor as to which section held which local title of what

rank, but the local hierarchy of titles involved more than
offshoots of a simple sa.

Local hierarchies were established

in traditional, pseudo-historical, mythological terms.
A title was traced through a line of male title hol­
ders to a god or legendary incident.

ders was the descent line.

This line of title hol­

Unlike the ramified system again,

it was the title whose descent was reckoned, not individuals.
Samoan kinship structure was then--not an overall system of

interconnected ramages operating as a framework from which
statuses emerged—but rather a number of more or less dis­

crete descent lines holding status titles which could be
traced through paternal ancestors to certain gods or legen­
dary incidents.

A corollary is that whereas in ramified sys­

tems, each household is ranked according to the genealogical

priority of its head—in Samoa a man of chiefly descent only

holding members of the household of a high matai did not
rank higher than members of a low matai*s household, and

there was no periodic "shedding off" of ramages in order to
realign ramage strength.

On the other hand, the possibility that the descent
line system developed out of a system of genealogical pri­
ority cannot be overlooked.

There was a myth to the effect

that the god Pili, a descendant of other gods, divided
Upolu among three of his sons.

In two of the autonomous

Upolu divisions the high chiefs trace descent from Pili.
Furthermore, some traces of primogeniture were found by Dr.
Mead, and she believes that primogeniture, formerly present,
5
had disappeared.
Among the evidence of former dependence

on primogenitural succession, and perhaps ramification, was
the elaboration of crises rites regarding the birth of the
first child of which Dr. Mead writes : "The birth of the first

child was regarded as much more important than any subse­

quent birth. ... A greater emphasis upon a first birth is one
of the few Samoan remnants of the prestige of the first born."^

The sa were composed primarily of tama tane, descen­
dants of males through males.

The females of the group,

515

however, and their immediate descendants through females,

formed a distinct organization called tama fafine which had
many ritual functions as well as economic prerogatives in

affinal gift exchanges and the power to veto the disposal of

land.

This group was usually headed by the oldest sister of

the male head.

As females usually married out, the tama

fafine lacked the cohesiveness, length and breadth of member­
ship of the tama tane.
A fuller understanding of the descent line system

in Samoa is gained when these lines are viewed in their local

setting and the organization on a territorial basis is exam­
ined.

The aboriginal settlement pattern was one of villages,

usually located near the sea coast.

A village was composed

of a number of different sa, usually more than five.
village did not exceed ^.OO people.

The

Each sa in the village

held one or more titles in the village fono.

Not all of the

possible titles in a village were necessarily operative at a

given time.

Usually one title was highest in rank; the hol­

der of such a title was the village chief.

The village with

its fono was the socio-political unit.

The village fono contributed representatives to fonos
of a district of several villages.

High-ranking village titles

conferred the prerogative of sitting on the district fono.

The multi-village fonos in similar manner contributed repre­
sentatives to larger, multi-distrlet fonos.

These were the

"division" fonos, which for most purposes and at most times

were the fonos of highest order in Samoa.

Division fonos

gib

corresponded to what was usually the largest social, politi­
cal and economic unit in Samoa, such as the divisions of

Aana, Atua and Tuamasanga on Upolu, or the Manuan group as a
whole.

All fonos ratified titles of the members, installed

For all in­

the high chiefs and made preparations for war.

tents and purposes the district and division fonos functioned

solely in these activities,

The high chief, the man with the

highest title in each division, usually had the title tui

followed by the name of the division—as tuiaana, tuimanua,

etc »

Such a chief usually traced his title to an illustri­

ous god through a line of males.

These chiefs were the head

of certain sa which had representatives in many of the vil­
lages, and thus the division gave the appearance of being

under the dominion of one particular family.

However, a great

deal of power was held by a number (usually under ten) of

other high-ranking men in each division known as the
tulafale alii, the orator chiefs.

Genealogical connection

can sometimes be demonstrated between the high chief and the

tulafale alii, but the connection is often affinal.

More

significant, as a contrast to the ramified system, such con­
nections refer to the far distant past.

And furthermore,

near-relatives and heads of known branches of the high chief’s
7
sa may rank lower than these tulafale alii.
Occasionally one high chief gained control of the

517

titles of the three great divisions of Upolu.

Such a chief

became tafaifa, the high chief of all Samoa except Manua.
His reign was only temporary, and although the precedent was

established before contact with Europeans, the position was

not a permanent Samoan institution.

The paramount chief's

duties were primarily socio-economic, as leader of the infre­
quent fono of all Samoa (save Manua) and as head of an occa­

sional redistributive network of that scope.

The situation

offers a parallel to that on Mangala, Easter Island and

Tikopia, and may be similarly related to sporadic production

of surplus sufficient to encompass the group in a single

accumulation-redistribution activity.

The parallel becomes

even more striking in relation to Mangala and Easter Island
when one notes that the position was a spoil of war, going
9
to the leader of the militarily dominant division.
Succes­

sion to this title was a case of high level felxibility in the

ranking system, similar to such instances in certain ramified

systems.
Flexibility was, in fact, an outstanding feature of

the Samoan ranking system.

Warfare does not seem to have

operated frequently as a mechanism of flexibility except in
the superstructual spheres.

On the other hand, a liberal

amount of adoptions and alliances with the mother's father's

The social organization showed great adaptability in reflect­
ing the differential growth of families and their differential

ability by consequence, to produce and distribute a large sur­
Through lavish distribution the head of a sa section

plus .

gained prestige, and with this prestige came a movement of
his title upward in the system of titles in the fono.

With

the aid of manipulations by the talking chiefs a new title

could be created, or a title could be raised in the hierarchy,
reflecting increasing economic importance.

Then again, the

opposite might occur, reflecting the economic decline of a

title.

As Dr. Mead has acutely noted:

The title held by the poor or undistinguished sinks
gradually into disrepute. The lesser title held by
the rich and the ambitious rises in the scale;
myths of its origin are manufactured, the gene­
alogies are rearranged, the phrasing of the [order
of titles] altered slightly; and a different set
of fixed and unalterable relationships is estab­
lished.10
Or again: "A shift in power from one house to another means
a shift in the genealogy oftenest on the lips of pedantic
talking chiefs."^
Dr. Mead has also given us a graphic idea of the fre­

quency of shifts and the amount of flexibility:

"So flexible

is the social structure, so minutely adapted to manipulation,

that it is possible to change the appearance of the fono in
—

Mead, op. cit., 21-22.
nibid., 13$.

319

twenty years.”

It is in fact this flexibility and the

creation of new titles around rising kin groups which gives
a clue to the process of sa growth and separation, and thus

points out a marked departure from the ramified system.

The

sa has a constant tendency to break up into new and entirely
distinct descent lines, to fragment along the line created by
the establishment of a new title :
The Samoan tendency is continually to break up old
lineages into smaller ones. Each generation adds
a few new eponymous ancestors of small descent
groups. An emphasis upon village organization,
upon the creation of new titles, and upon the house­
holds surrounding each title accounts for this fea­
ture. ... The existence of a large number of small
titles of recent origin or low prestige amply pro­
vides for this multiplication of family groups.
For example, the family of Asoao in Faleasao may
control half a dozen small titles, but all of these
title holders and their children may prefer to lay
claim to being members of the Sa Asoao. But when
the family becomes more numerous, the small title
rises in prestige through the united labor of
large descent groups, and some of the branches of
the family will cease to comment upon their remote
relationship to Asoao and use the new title with
the prefix, ’family of.’

This is a crucial difference between Samoan and ramified sys­
tems.

When fission occurs in ramages, it occurs between

descendants of brothers but the common ancestor and unity of

the group is maintained.

In Samoa, fission results in frag­

mentation of a group and the loss of ties between two lines

of descent.
The appearance of a given sa in a number of village

320

fonos is intelligible in terms of village growth and fission:
a given segment of a village (or fishing settlement attached

to it) containing households of several of the village sa
grew in size; the local bond was emphasized and carried on in
fono life until the fono itself split into two fonos, hence
the village into two villages; the split was rapidly codified

by the creation of a ceremonial pattern for the new fono by

its talking chiefs, undoubtedly based on the pattern in the
old village.

Thus the sa are reduplicated.

Undoubtedly the

entire history of Samoa is intelligible in terms of the frag­

mentation of sa and the fission of villages.
Status levels, of course, emerge out of the descent
line system and its titular emphasis.

Those with titles are

markedly differentiated from those without in socio-economic
and religious functions, privileges and obligations.

The

group of titles, the fono composed of matai, was the dominant

force in Samoan life.

Thus two distinct status levels

emerged, those with titles and those without.

Of course,

every title occupied a different position and rank, and high

titles can be further distinguished from low titles.

This

distinction had some functional significance with regard to

differential cultural roles, but not as great (or perhaps not
as well documented) as that between "titled" and "untitled."
The different roles of the status levels have already been

outlined.it may be noticed that in contrast to the rami­
fied systems, power is vested in councils rather than in

not on the chiefs as such.

as opposed to a relationship emphasis in the ramified sys­

Futuna^
The social organization of Futuna was very much like

that of Samoa.

The principal difference is that the system

of titles was much smaller in scope ; the only titles were

those of the village chiefs (one in every village) and of the

district chiefs and functionaries.

Most descent lines were

very short and carried no titles whatsoever.

The smallest social unit was the household, a patri­
local extended family called the kainga composed of eight

persons on the average, and twenty-five on the outside.

The

kainga was often coextensive with the kutunga, the counterpart
