Village chiefs (aliki f enua) and one or two other function­
aries formed the district fono, a council solely of titled

men and headed by the district chief, aliki sau.

The dis­

trict council titles were arranged in a definite hierarchy
which was symbolized in kava ceremony seating and name­

calling arrangements.

The descent line of the district chiefs

traced to various gods.

The lines of village chief titles

were also elaborated, but other genealogies were much shorter.
Succession to title was, as in Samoa, patrilineal but

without a definite rule.

The succession was apparently quali­

fied by warfare or internal struggles.

In one district, for

example, succession to the district chieftainship alternated
between two rival kutunga.

Flexibility in the ranking struc­

ture occasioned by differential military power is thus ap­

parent .

Burrows states, furthermore, that the establishment

of precedence between titles, "has uniformly been by prowess
19
in war."
Certainly, as the succession pattern indicates,
the hierarchy of titles did not depend on seniority of descent

as it did in ramified systems.

There is no evidence for gene­

alogical connection of any sort between the kutunga which sup­
plied the various titles in a district.

Neither do the para­

mount chiefs of the districts trace descent to a common ances­

tor.

Legends of early settlers do not even pretend to account

for the population of the districts.

Yet there are evidences, perhaps vestiges, of rami­
fied practices.

The birth of the first male child was elab­

orately celebrated.

More significant is a pattern of steward­

ship over land accruing to, "the senior man of the [descent
20
line] ... called taokete (elder son) or pule (master)."
As noted, however, primogeniture was not the rule in succes­

sion to title, and the hierarchy of titles was not based on

seniority.

The stress placed on the eldest son was precisely

in the area of social organization, therefore, where status
was of least importance, in kutunga without titles.

It might

be a survival of times when primogeniture and seniority were

significant principles of ranking which pervaded the entire
society--a situation which was certainly not true at the time

of European contact.

There also was no pattern of overlapping

stewardship, except insofar as the fono might be called the
supreme steward of its territory.

Flexibility of the ranking structure, as mentioned,

was frequently effected through warfare.

Differential in­

crease of families was in the main unchecked, despite adoption

(which was infrequent) or alignment with a mother's father's

of titles.

As in Samoa, and as was generally char­

holding a title did not automatically bestow high rank.

Other

characteristics of descent line systems found in Futuna, but

not in ramified systems, were emphasis on territorial units

and the regulation of affairs by chiefs in a council rather
than by a chief with counselors.

Uvea21
Uvean social organization is difficult to classify.

There were many aspects of ramification in Uvea.

Among them

were emphasis on the ceremonial celebration of first births,
gradation based on seniority in households, and succession of
the first-born son (with exceptions due to unfitness) to the

headship of the household and stewardship over its lands.
There is no precise information on the pattern of succession

to important titles, but what there is suggests that primo­

geniture played some role, except in the case of the highest

titles on the island which often changed hands in warfare.
In a personal communication, Dr. Burrows informed us that

primogeniture was, in fact, the major criterion determining

Other shifts in precedence have occurred with respect to
other titles; some "ministers" (island high chiefs) which

outrank village and other ancient chiefs have new titles.
One line, Hoko, now that of a village chief, formerly, accord­
ing to tradition, supplied the high chief.

Native traditions

are full of stories of factional rivalry, and this probably
was the major mechanism accounting for shifts in the titular

hierarchy.

Adoptions, some ambi-patrilocal residence and

ambi-patrilineal descent, indicate that changes in descent

line alignment occurred on all levels of the society, prob­

ably occasioned by differential family growth.

The status levels crystallize clearly from the

descent line and title systems.

Two levels are easily dis­

Unlike

Samoa and Futuna, and probably related to the marked number

to have conferred extra prestige.

Summary and Interpretations

The major characteristic of descent line systems in
Polynesia, as exemplified in Samoa, Futuna and Uvea, is dis­
crete groupings of patrilineal kin-groupings which confer
titles in local administrative units such as villages and dis­
tricts.

The titles are arranged in a traditional order of

precedence symbolized by seating position in administrative
councils or fonos, and in the order of kava serving at the

meetings of such bodies and other ceremonial occasions.
A number of variations are apparent in the descent

line systems considered.

One set of variations is concerned

with the elaboration of the titular system itself.

Futuna

had the least complex hierarchy of titles ; there were only

village chiefs, one to each village, and the paramount chief
Although the island occasionally partici­

of the district.

pated in a single distributive activity, there was no island

supreme chief.

Uvea had a separate set of island chiefs as

well as a chief in each village and some matapule titles.

In

Samoa, every descent line in a village held at least one

title making a complete system of titles in each village.

The system was also extended to the district and division
levels, and occasionally to all Samoa; there being a supreme
chief for each such regulatory segment.

Furthermore, the

Samoan titular system was divided into two categories, chief
and talking chief.

These variations in elaboration of the

titular hierarchy can be correlated with variations in

efficiency of technological exploitation as Futuna, Uvea and
Samoa rank in that order of technological-environmental effi­

ciency as indicated by size of distributive network. (See
Table II.)

Elaboration of the titular hierarchy is, in fact,

an elaboration or increase in the amount of social stratifi­

cation and is thus co-adapted to technological-environmental

efficiency (as are the other aspects we have considered in
Chapters I-VI).

Another set of variations in the descent line sys­
tems considered here is the type of flexibility in the sys­
tem.

On all three islands differential growth of descent

lines results in changes in the order of precedence in the
titular hierarchy.

In Samoa these changes were accomplished

in a peaceful and institutional matter.

The fono patterns

and genealogies were rearranged by the talking chief in accord­

ance with changes in the ability of descent lines to make
lavish distributions of food and goods.

In Futuna and Uvea,

on the other hand, internal struggles, assassinations and war­
fare were the major mechanisms involved in rearrangements of

the titular hierarchy.

One factor which may be adduced to

account for this difference is the presence of greater amounts

of available, unused land in Samoa, but evidently less in

Futuna or Uvea.

The arable, valuable land in Samoa was never
on
fully utilized by the aboriginal population.
One possible

551.

reason for this demographic situation is the fact that land

plots generally decrease in size and to some extent in
agricultural value as one moves inward from the sea^ mak­
ing it difficult for an inland dwelling population to pro­
duce a surplus to exchange for sea products to which it would

have little or no direct access.^

only a large family then

could divorce part os its labor resources from the rich sea
areas and take advantage of this freely available land.

A

greater surplus could be produced by such a family, hence a

greater distribution and greater prestige.
Thus, the peaceful flexibility of the Samoan strati­
fication system maj be related to the relative abundance of

unused land on those islands.

In Futuna, and to a lesser

extent Uvea, there does not seem to have been such a propor­

tionately large quantity of unused land.

Most of the Futunan

interior was unarable due to desert and heavily acidic soils,

(see Chart 9), while on Uvea, though the interior was arable

in spots, it seems to have been fairly well occupied by the
various kainga as testified by numerous plantation sites.

Coulter, op. cit.

/

33
_ . .n
Coulter noted the sad plight of a village on
Tutuila which had used up all the good taro lands in the
and was pushing up the forested slopes such that,

^Burrows, 1937, 12-13.

Furthermore, whereas interior land in Samoa is well watered

and suitable for continuous, albeit limited, taro production,
that in Uvea and, of course, Futuna is not. (See Charts 3, 6,

9.)

In sum then, the greater restriction on unused land in

Futuna and Uvea may, with the differential growth of families
and villages, have produced internal struggles which resulted
in changes in the hierarchy of titles.

Whereas in Samoa,

with relative abundance of free land, the same results seem

to have come about through peaceful expression of the ability
of a larger family to produce and distribute a greater sur­

plus than could a smaller family.
A final variation in descent line structures is the

degree to which ramified characteristics are incorporated in
them.

This question will be taken up in the following chap­

ter which is a discussion of possible explanations of the dif­
ferences between ramified and descent line systems.

334

CHAPTER X
RAMIFIED AND DESCENT LINE SYSTEMS:
INTERPRETATION

Ramified and descent line systems have each been de­

scribed in some detail.
ber of contrasts.

The two organizations present a num­

The ramified system, operating through

primogeniture, is characterized by a seniority principle which
pervades the entire society; thus the status of any given

person is directly proportional to his distance from the main
line of descent of the community.

The descent line system

also contains inherited statuses, but primogeniture is not
necessarily the mechanism of inheritance.

The ranking prin­

ciple in descent line systems is a traditional, mythological

order of precedence, an order having outward manifestations in
kava ceremonies and other activities.In ramified societies,
kinship groupings, ramages, are in the nature of groups of

descendants from common ancestors related to other similar
groups in a larger ramage descendent from a common ancestor

of further remove.

Smaller groups are thus incorporated in

larger groups headed by higher and higher relatives, the whole

Symbolization of status in kava ceremonials or other
similar activities can also occur, of course, in ramified
systems as the Tongan kava ceremonial and fono arrangements,
or the seating arrangements on the ancestral marae of the
Society Islands.

Ü55

giving the picture of a genealogical tree.

The paramount

chief, the direct descendant of the gods stands at the apex

of the society.

By contrast, in descent line organizations,

kinship structures are groups of relatives tracing common

patrilineal descent.

Often such groups are no larger than

moderate-sized patrilocal extended families.

These descent

lines confer various titles, the importance of which does

not rest on the seniority of the line—rather the importance
of the line rests on the position of the title in the social
hierarchy.

Both ramified and descent line systems have terri­
torial as well as kinship bases.

Large order ramages are

usually more or less localized in given territories.

With

the exception of the Maori, the localization was not exact;
and in all cases, it was the ramages which were the most im­

portant political, administrative units--territorial bonds be­
ing subsidiary.

In the descent line systems, by contrast,

political action is carried along territorial lines--a corre­

late of the fragmentary nature of the kinship groupings.

And

along with emphasis on locality goes government by councils
of representative heads of the descent lines.

One of the

titles in each council was always supreme and thus there were

high chiefs.

In ramified systems, regulatory functions are

concentrated in the high chief who may or may not consult in­

formally with his inferior relatives, the lesser chiefs.

These differences between the ramified and descent

line systems are also correlated with differences in the forms

of economic and socio-political processes,

In the land

tenure structure, for example, the ramified systems are asso­
ciated with the pattern of overlapping stewardship, a pattern

in which the supreme chief is the supreme manager of the
societies' resources, while the heads of ramages form subor­
dinate stewards managing the lands of their ramages.

In

descent line systems, the head of the descent line is steward

of the descent lines'holdings, but all such holdings are sub­
ordinated to the local council of titled chiefs,

"Overlapping

stewardship” can be extended to cover descent line systems as

well, but high order stewardships are held by councils, not

by individuals.
Similarly, in distributive activities there are con­
trasts.

In both cases the distributive system is mainly of

the "redistributive" form, that is, a system in which surplus
production is constantly or periodically passed up the social

hierarchy and is redistributed on various occasions.

But in

ramified systems, the focal points of accumulation and redis­
tribution are ramage heads.

In descent line systems small­

scale redistributive activities are undertaken by descent
line heads, but activities involving more than just a few
descent lines are usually organized and undertaken by councils

of titled men, and thus the organization follows local lines.

The organization of production, especially communal
and specialized craft production, also varies from ramified to

descent line systems.

In ramified systems, communal and craft

specialized production is usually organized along ramage lines

^37

and initiated by ramage chiefs or heads.

On the other hand,

communal production in descent line systems is organized on
a territorial basis, usually the village, and is initiated
and directed by the local council.

Craft specialization, as

Samoan carpentry, is organized along lines of descent, just

as in ramified systems.

In both cases, the holding of craft

skills in particular kinship groups gives the craftsmen a more

or less cohesive organization.

Such organizations have been
2
called "guilds" by Polynesian ethnologists.
They differ

from medieval guilds in two significant ways.

First of all,

they were organized on a kinship basis; they are kinship

groups.Secondly, the "guild" was not a price-fixing organi­
zation, as there were no prices and the economy was not of

the market form.

It is true that in certain cases, the

"guild" would walk off the "job" if it appeared that the

workers would not be well fed or given ample reward for their
services, and that no member of the "guild" would work further
on the project.

But this did not involve a failure to live

up to a stipulated price for a stipulated service

For a description of one so-called guild see Sir
Peter Buck's discussion of the Samoan carpenters, the Sa
Tangaloa, "descendants of Tangaloa." (Buck, 1930, S^fTT

^For example, a proficient carpenter in Samoa who
was not a member of the descent line of Tangaloa was
adopted into it. (Buck, 1930, 81[. )
^Turner wrote of the Samoan custom of pledging to
work and to support, on the part of craftsmen and "entre­
preneurs" respectively, that: "Nothing was stipulated as to
the cost; that was left entirely to the honour of the em­
ploying party." (Turner, 1881*., 157-156.)

538

In socio-regulatory matters differences between rami­

fied and descent line organizations can also be seen.

Poli­

tical actions as the punishment of wrong-doers, are usually
taken up by the fono in descent line systems, but by the in­

dividual chiefs in ramified systems.

Similarly in decisions

concerning peace and war, the chief usually initiates the pro­

ject in ramified systems, albeit he consulted with lesser
chiefs, in particular instances.

In descent line systems,

however, war and peace are phrased as fono decisions.
In sum, the two systems, ramified and descent line,

differ significantly, not only in the nature of kinship

structure and in the principles dictating the order of pre­

cedence of status, but also in co-adaptive processes in eco­

nomic and social life.

Two types of factors, broadly speak­

ing , historical and adaptive, can be adduced to account for
differences between ramified and descent line organizations.

Historical Background
Ramified and descent line systems may have historical
bases.

Islands having one or the other might have common

histories, might have come from common sources.

The two forms,

in other words, may have developed in separate centers and

spread into particular, exclusive areas.

Tracing the distinc­

tion in social organization to different sources, of course,

does not fully explain this cultural variation.

On the other

hand, the possibility of distinct histories must be taken into
account in order to fully understand the role of adaptive

359

factors in an explanation of cultural difference.
For some time ethnologists have differentiated two

spheres of exclusive cultural influence in Polynesia, a
western and a central-marginal or eastern (see Chapter VII).
The regional variation in the distribution of aspects of
material culture, kinship practices (not including ramified

and descent line systems) and mythology have led to the con­
clusion, primarily through the detailed comparisons of Pro­
fessor Burrows/ that these two areas were, in fact, histori­

cally diverse--perhaps as a result of the dissemination of
Recently this conclusion
7
has found further corroboration from linguistic analysis.
culture from two distinct centers,

Among the island groups represented here, Samoa, Tonga,

Futuna, Uvea and Tikopia, would be included in the western
group, while Hawaii, the Societies, New Zealand, the Mar­
quesas, Mangala, Mangareva and Easter Island, would be eastern.

It does not seem unreasonable that the geographical
distribution of descent line and ramified structures might

correspond to the west-east historical dichotomy.

is actually some positive correlation.

And there

Futuna, Uvea and

Samoa are all western and all examples of descent line sys­
tems—while all the eastern groups have ramified organizations.

However, Tonga and Tikopia are both ramified and western.
^Burrows, 1938b, 1940.
6Cf. Buck, 1938b.

^Elbert, 1953*

340

Furthermore, according to linguistic evidence, Tikopia, a
ramified island, was settled from Samoa, a descent line sys8
tern.
And also from linguistic evidence, Futuna and Uvea
9
are more closely related to Tonga than to Samoa, all of

which is not what it should be if the historical factor were

the only factor.

Certainly if a group came from an island

with a particular type of social organization, it would tend

to recreate that organization if it settled in an uninhabited
area.

But other elements beyond historical similarity or

diversity are needed in any attempt to explain these varia­

tions in social system.

Descent line and ramified systems can

also be understood as different types of socio-cultural adap­

tations to the natural environment--as adaptive variations.

An explanation of organizational differences from this point

of view may shed light on our problem.
Ramified and Descent Line Systems as
Adaptive Alternatives
Leaving aside, temporarily, the question of histori­
cal relationships, these Polynesian societies can be consid­

ered as members of a particular culture species adapting to

various local habitats.

The two systems, the ramified and the

descent line, may represent differing adaptations related to

variations in technological-environmental conditions.

In

g

Ibid., 16U» Firth, however, records traditions of
Tongan contacts in Tikopia. (Firth, 1936, passim.)

^Elbert, op. cit., 163, 16U*

341

particular, the distribution of natural resources, i.e., of
the zones of exploitation, may have been a factor prejudicing
the development of ramified or descent line systems.
The crucial operational difference between ramified

and descent line systems is whether or not kinship relation­
ship is maintained between households or patrilocal extended

families which have segmented due to increase in size.

In

both organizations large groups of this nature divide, giving

rise to two distinct alignments of patrilineal kin.

In rami­

fied organizations, the split usually takes place between the

families of two brothers.

The relationship between the house­

holds is maintained and the two households are ranked according

to the seniority of the brothers.

In descent line systems the

connection between segmented families may be remembered for a
time, but gradually the tie weakens and dissolves, and two

discrete groups are formed.

Practically all other differences

in the two systems are related to this difference in the seg­

mentation process.

In economic matters in ramified systems,

such as land tenure and redistributive activities, the divided

households also remain interconnected; the senior household
head exercises supreme stewardship and is the focal point in
cooperative accumulations and distributions.

Similarly, pro­

ductive activities involving the labor of the two households
would be led by the head of the senior household.

And such

productive and distributive activities might encompass a large
order ramage, no matter where its diverse branches were lo­

cated.

In descent line systems with discrete groupings, the

42

head of each group exercised such regulatory economic func­
tions, but if distribution or production involved many groups

or descent lines, the heads of all met in council.
The maintenance of kinship ties between two groups

derived from a common group seems to be an adaptation to

economic necessities of cooperation.

In particular it may

be postulated that ties will be maintained between two branch­
ing households if there is a tendency for such households to

become specialized or semi-specialized in production of sur­
plus commodities.

Thus if one household produces a surplus

of fish—or might be able on some specified occasion to pro­

duce such a surplus—while another, due to its inland situa­
tion, could more easily produce a surplus of a cultivated

food, some advantage would certainly be served if these house­
holds could maintain their kinship bonds.

By means of redis­

tributive activities in which one household acts as a focal
point of accumulation and redistribution or by means of direct
exchange, such inequalities or potential inequalities in con­

sumables might be remedied; the surplus prodiction of dif­
ferent foods or goods might be equitably distributed.

It may be further postulated, therefore, that should
a budded-off household tend to move into or gain access to a

resource area which could be efficiently exploited to produce
strategic surpluses different in kind from those produced by
the original group, there would be a tendency for ramified

organization to develop.

We submit that the ramified system

is adapted to the exploitation of different, widely-spread

343

resource areas, which adaptation on an economic level becomes

manifest in sporadic, seasonal or total specialization of fam­

ilial production.

On the other hand, if in the process of

fission, the household or family which buds off tends to move
into an area identical to that of the parent group as regards

the type of strategic goods producible, the kinship tie be­
tween the two groups might weaken and dissolve.

Common ex­

ploitation of similar resources is co-adaptive with descent
line systems.

But familial specialization of resource produc­

tion might produce distributable surpluses at various times
in the annual cycle, and the ramified system, as a distribu­
tive network is a mechanism that could cope with such prob­

lems .

If families, however, are inhabiting a common resource

area, then any surplus which is produced by one would be more
or less contemporaneously produced by all.

Any variations in

the amount of familial surplus might be equalized by a com­

munity distribution involving all the families of a locale, and

a territorial distributive network would be advantageous.
if these deductions are correct, empirical evidence
should show (other factors being constant) ramified systems

on islands in which there is a variety of scattered resource

zones differentially exploited by families or small groups of

families.

Or from another viewpoint, ramified systems would

be expectable on those islands where a single patrilocal ex­

tended family could not exploit the total range of available
resources.

On the other hand, where resource areas are clus­

tered in time and space such that a single familial group could

544

adequately cope with the total range of available exploita­

tive techniques, descent line systems would be most fre­
quently found.

As a further corollary, we should expect to

find ramified systems often in association with a scattered,

hamlet type of settlement pattern, while descent line systems
might frequently be found with a nucleated, village type of
settlement arrangement.

The data on 'the ecology’ of the

ramified and descent line systems may now be reviewed from
this point of view.

Much information on the distribution of

resource areas is already contained in the productivity
charts in Chapter V, hence our descriptions will be relatively
brief and of a summary nature.

Hawaii (Ramified)
A tremendous variety of widely scattered environ­

The range

There were about twenty-five groupings of

1,300 was divided.

Firth calls these villages; they were

assemblages of about fifty people each.

Apparently a degree

of familial specialization in agriculture was compromised

with direct access to reef resources resulting in this settle­

ment pattern.

The "villages" might contain people from a

number of different ramages, although usually members of one
39
large-scale ramage ("clan") predominated in each village.

New Zealand (Ramified)
Any particular tribe would have one predominant and
several subsidiary subsistence modes among the following:

agriculture, fishing and forest hunting and collecting.

Each

such subsistence mode would embrace a variety of activities,

The

Tuhoe, for example, a forest tribe, lived on rats, birds,

ferns, berries, bulrush and other roots, fresh-water fish and
hl

As many of these activities

Such tasks as bird­

groups of the hapu, a surplus being taken and preserved.

Despite this more or less rudimentary familial special­
ization, the Maori were concentrated in villages much of the

year.

Such villages were composed, as we have already seen,

of a single large ramage, the hapu.

The individual household,

however, might move out to the forest, sea or garden land dur­

ing the proper food-getting seasons.^

On the other hand, the

New Zealand has more of a continental than

an island topography.

Unlike smaller Polynesian islands,

exploitable zones, if scattered, would not be distributed at

various spots running from the sea inland to a mountain, but

might be scattered in all directions from a given habitation
point.
Easter Island
The distribution of resource areas in Easter Island

evidently was a less scattered one than prevailed in other

islands with ramified systems.

Summary and Interpretations

The differences between ramified and descent line
systems have been outlined in some detail.

Evidence has been

adduced to show that these differences are related to some
^Burrows, 1938a, 217.

62

Personal communication.
6Burrows, 1937, 12.

3cl

extent to diverse historical traditions.

But because some

islands with common histories manifested different forms of
organization, it was suggested that the forms also be con­

sidered from an adaptational viewpoint—that they are perhaps

associated with particular types of exploitative techniques
operating in specific environmental situations.

The line of

reasoning adopted was that connection between segmented house­
holds would tend to be maintained if, in the process of seg­
mentation-, the new household was apt to move into a new re­

source area and thus specialize in a particular type of
strategic production (the surplus of which could be used in

exchanges or redistributive activities).

Maintenance of con­

nections would result in the growth of interconnected ramages
and a hierarchy for purposes of effecting equitable distribu­

tion of goods, both by reciprocal and redistributive methods.

If, on the other hand, a new household moved into an area
identical in exploitable opportunities to that in which the

parent household was situated, connection between the two would

weaken and dissolve and descent line systems result.

As a

corollary, one would expect descent line systems to be asso­

ciated with concentrated or village settlement patterns and
ramified systems with scattered settlements.

In at least ten of the twelve societies examined this
postulated relationship was found to hold entirely.

Only in

Easter Island, among the ramified systems, and Uvea, among

the descent line systems, was there room for doubt that the
social organization was adapted to the "proper" technological-

environmental situation.

However, on both islands the

techno-environmental conditions themselves are not defi­
nitely committed either toward prohibiting or promoting fam­

ilial specialization.

On both islands the coastal areas

were most promising in exploitative possibilities, but in­
land cultivation and diversity of familial production were

also possible.

On Easter Island there is some definite

evidence of familial specialization.

But both Easter Island

and Uvea seem to be either-or situations; little selective

pressure in favor of either the ramified or descent line

system is apparent.

In the case of Easter Island it would

seem that its historical alignments to eastern Polynesia
would prejudice the outcome under such conditions of slight

selective pressure.

However, this interpretation would not

hold for Uvea, which evidently was settled from Tonga, a
ramified island.

That would account for the presence of

many ramified elements in Uvea, but to all intents Tongan ori­

gin should also have produced a complete ramified system in
Uvea.

An alternative explanation would be that descent lines

are a breakdown of Uvean organization under European influ­

ence, especially the clustering of settlements in coastal vil­
lages or that ramification developed in Tonga after the Uvean

emigration.

A speculation that arises out of the presence of
^Elbert, 195>3, 163, !6^; Burrows, 1937, 18.

ramified elements in all the descent line systems is that

the descent line system is an offshoot or divergence from
the ramified organization under particular selective condi­

tions .

The proto-Polynesian culture may have been ramage-

organized with an emphasis on seniority and an organization

of ranked, interconnected segments.

Various customs found

in descent line systems may be, as Dr. Mead pointed out,

vestiges of an emphasis on the first-born and hence of a ram­
ified structure.

Such an interpretation, however, should be

considered only as a reasonable speculation based on the
evidence examined so far.

CHAPTER XI
SOCIAL ORGANIZATION AND STRATIFICATION ON
POLYNESIAN ATOLLS

The social organizations on the Polynesian atolls
show a marked degree of variability, not only among themselves

but also in comparison with the descent line and ramified sys­
tems of the high islands.

One of the reasons is perhaps the

different selective factors encountered on low islands.

In

contrast to volcanic islands, adaptive variation is not a
response to problems engendered by large surpluses.

In con­

trast to many of the localities considered heretofore, the

atolls have : low productivities and relatively small and
sporadic surpluses, frequent famines, high population densi­

ties in very small areas and peculiarities of resource dis­
tribution.

All of these factors throw light on social forms

in low islands.

Beside, variations in place of origin, de­

gree of isolation and length of occupation have contributed to

the social differences between them.

^In contrast to the low islands, settlements of
the higher islands studied, according to the length of gene­
alogies and historical traditions, with the exception of
Uvea, seem to have been occupied for at least several hun­
dred years before European contact. Information to this
effect may be found in the historical sections of the Im­
portant ethnographies of the various islands.

In short, the organization of interpersonal affairs
on atolls may differ from such organizations on the high

islands and vary among themselves.

Five atoll cultures will

be examined in detail in this section: Manihiki-Rakahanga,

Tongareva, Tokelau, Ontong Java and Pukapuka.

An attempt will

be made, at the end of the chapter, to consider possible ex­

planations of these varieties.

The atolls are described in

the order of divergence from the main types of social organi­
zation already encountered.

Examples of Atoll Organization
Tongareva

2

The social organization of Tongareva was of the
typical ramage type.

The diagnostic characteristics of ram­

ified societies, primogeniture, an overall seniority system
with a subtle gradation in status, an emphasis on ceremonial

observances in the life of the first-born, overlapping stew­
ardship corresponding to a system of interconnected ramages,

and so on, were all present in Tongareva.

There were three

major lines, from one of which (or all three through inter­
marriages) each individual traced his descent.

The existence

of three, rather than a single line is related to the political
2

Principal Sources: Buck, 1932a; E. H. Lamont,
Wild Life Among the Pacific Islanders, 1867; S. P. Smith,
"tongarewa, or Penrhyn Island, and its People," New
Zealand Inst, Trans., 22, 85-103, 1889; Williamson, 1921^,

separatism (see below) on the atoll.

O

The major ramages were the household, and the tribedistrict.

There is no definite name for the household group,

which consisted of a patrilocal extended family, including
up to three or four generations of patrilineally related

relatives.

As with all other Polynesian groups, bilateral

cousin exogamy was the rule.

In this case third cousins

were permitted spouses, second cousins were not.

The first

sons often established new households if many children were

born in a single family, so that headship of an original

household might pass to a younger son.

However, the social

position of the father and his rule, if any, over a number

of households passed to the eldest son.

As implied, house­

holds segmented under the pressure of increasing members.
A number of households tracing descent from a com­

mon ancestor were united in a larger group, a "tribe," we

might call it.

According to Buck, general kinship connec­

tions along the three major lines of descent were "rele­

gated to the background"; instead, the tribes tracing
descent to more recent ancestors, formed independent groups
inhabiting particular islands or sections of one of the lar­
ger islandsThe tribe had a name, in an early period that
of the common ancestor, although, according to Buck, this

It may also be related to economic differentiation
of groups, but unfortunately, data on distributive activi­

^Buck, op. cit., 26, 45.

later was changed to territorial names referring to the islets
in which the tribes were found." Moss, according to William/

son, estimated eight tribes, which, with Buck’s estimate' of
the pre-contact population at about 2,000, makes an average
of 250 people per tribe.

The senior family of each such

group supplied, by primogenitural succession, the tribal high

chief, ariki.

As each tribal group represented a segment

which had budded off from one of the major lines, the tribal
chiefs were ranked on the basis of seniority, and this rank­

ing was of importance in determining the leadership of tribes
allied for military purposes.$
The organization of regula­
tory functions was based on the ramified framework of the

tribe; the chief was the tribal leader and the household

heads comprised an informal discussion group consulted by him.
The status levels, two in number, crystallized out

of the genealogical system.

The tribal chiefs, ariki, and

their households may be distinguished as an upper status level.
The rest of the populace, related to the chiefs, can be placed

in a second level.

Manihiki-Rakahanga

9

As Tongareva, Manihiki and Rakahangan culture can
be classified with the ramified systems.

Succession to sta­

tus was through primogeniture and the differentiation between

senior and junior lines pervaded the entire group.

Any per­

son's status was proportional to the closeness of his rela10
tionship to the main lines of descent in the society.
Knowledge of the overall genealogical record was poorly pre­
served by most people, a fact attributable, according to

Buck, to the original population of the atolls by members of
a single biological family, headed by a defeated warrior,

whose immigration was unaccompanied by priests.

The result

was that, although the people knew the story of settlement,
"only a few could trace a connected line from Toa [that is,
the original settler] to themselves.

Most could trace pedi­

grees to ancestors through six to twelve generations but
11
could not connect them with the main lines."
However, a

skilled native genealogist could demonstrate the inter­
relatedness of all families.

The household group, a patrilocal extended family in
most cases, was the fundamental ramage.

An understanding of higher order ramages is best

gained from an exposition of the history of Manihiki-Rakahangan
organization as reconstructed by Buck from the native tradl12
tions and modern organization.
Traditions, as indicated,

trace the colonization of Manihiki and Rakahanga to a single

biological family.

Buck considers Manhiki-Rakahanga organi­

zation to have developed from this family into a number of

"tribes" which segmented into sub-tribes.

The first division

was between the families of two brothers, although the groups
remained in a unified polity.

In the eleventh generation

after settlement the two lines diverged due to a quarrel, and

two arlki (high chief) titles were established.

The two lines

then each divided into two sub-lines led by the younger

brothers of the arikls; and thus, at the time of contact,
there were two groupings of two tribes each.

The largest segments of the ramage structure were
then the super-tribal groups, each led by an ariki with a

title, and each composed of descendants of a common ancestor.
Each super-tribal group was divided into two tribes.

The

tribes were headed by chiefs, senior members of the senior
line in each.

Tribal chiefs were called whakamanu.

largest recorded population of the two atolls totals less than

than 900 persons,
on the average.

The tribes were known as matakeinanga;

they also had names which may have been the names of their
respective common ancestors.

The tribes were split into

smaller ramages, tukuwhare, composed of a number of house­
holds tracing common descent.

Such sub-tribes usually traced

descent to brothers, often those of the first ariki of the

super-tribal groups.

The tukuwhare contained between thirty

and fifty persons.
Most of the duties of the two arikis were priestly;

they hardly interfered in secular matters, which were car­
ried out by the papa, executives attached to the ariki, and

by the tribal chiefs and sub-chiefs.

The papa, tribal and

sub-tribal chiefs formed the hui rangatira, the assembly of

chiefs, which acted as a consulting group to the ariki.

This

group of chiefs and their households may be distinguished as
a separate status level from the remainder of the population.

crucial points are whether or not the kindreds maintained

Genealogies were kept that reached further back

than the common ancestor of the kindred, and kinship was

Turning

because he was the oldest member in one of the four lines

descendent from the brothers who reputedly sired the popula­
tion of that atoll, an indication of underlying ramification.

All of the evidence in fact, shows at least "underlying" rami­
fication, coupled perhaps with an egalitarian emphasis on age.
But we found no clear statement to the effect that all lines

of descent were interconnected and their heads graded on a
genealogical basis.

Gérontocratie succession would actually

be incompatible with that type of structure.

We thus have no

assurance that a genealogical framework was the basis of rank.
Furthermore, MacGregor’s interpretation of men’s house organ-

ization suggests that it was not (see below) and that the

Each atoll had a village, and each village had sev­

On Fakaofu two of these houses served as

garrisons for the village guard.

Although one of MacGregor’s

In support of this

in Samoa, appears in many of the names of the men’s houses.
He writes that the men's house, therefore, can be considered
as the method of preserving the wider common kinship among
kindreds which had diverged from a single kindred.Even

given this as true, the fact that there were seven on Fakaofu
would argue for a discreteness in kindred organization.
Regulatory functions were carried out by a chief and
a council in the village of each atoll.

was the head chief of the three atolls, but this arrangement

The stratification structure was extremely simple.

Two non-egalitarian status levels can be distinguished; an

upper level consisting of the village chief and presumably,

in earlier times, the village priest; and the remainder of
the population in a lower level.

The basic kinship groups in Ontong Java were the

This "household" is actually

In Hogbin’s time (1927) there

were two villages on the atoll.

These were permanently in­

during the tribal ceremonies.

For the rest of the year most

cultivated.

On the islets people lived patrilocally, prob­

The joint family was known by the same name as

The joint families of Ontong Java were grouped into

Each

The tribe took

Each tribe

Succession to this newly formed paramount chief­

It was finally stabilized in one family in which the oldest

That each tribe was headed by its oldest male who

Pukapuka^

The social organization in Pukapuka was the most

structurally complex (in the sense of having the most differ­

ent kinds of organizations) of all atolls considered here.
It is also the most difficult to classify.

Every individual

belonged to a household, a matrilineage, a patrilineage, an

age grade (for males, at any rate) and a village organization.
The household was often based on the nuclear family,

with additions, most frequently of patrilineal relatives, and
also of matrilineal relatives.

The household was called

ngatuwale, and contained between six and seven people on the
average.

A number of households living together and tracing

descent from a common ancestor comprised a ylkuanga, a patri­

local extended family.
The ylkuanga was a unit of the po or kainga, a patri­
lineal grouping.

There were seven such groupings in Puka­

puka; they averaged less than one hundred members each.
Each po was divided into sublines which further segmented

into smaller groups under the pressure of increasing numbers.
Each subline had a chief or head, langatila, and the lineage

as a whole had a chief who was either 'chief' or 'high chief,'

^Principal Sources: E. and P. Beaglehole, 1938;
E. Beaglehole, 1944; MacGregor, 1935*

384

aliki or aliki wui.

The division of these lineages into

branches and sub-branches strongly suggests a full-blown ram­

ified system.

The suggestion becomes even stronger when we

are told that succession to chieftainship is passed from

father to son.

Unfortunately, we found no reference pro or

con on primogeniture, and the genealogies which indicate sue cession4"

do not clearly show primogeniture either--as only

the succeeding son is diagrammed in most cases.

However,

primogeniture, and an overall ranking structure in the line­
age based on seniority of descent, are clearly implied by
the Beagleholes when they write, of the succession pattern,

"direct male issue might be passed over in favor of someone
from a junior line of the lineage, who would be adopted into
the main line of descent before induction into office.

Stewardship over land by both lineage head and household head

is also reported--a ramified practice.

It seems reasonable

to suppose that the patrilineage organizations were of the
ramage type.

The different lineages, however, do not fall

into an overall ramage organization, since they trace their
descent to fifteen different men who headed the families left

after a great tidal wave had depleted the population some
350 years ago.

Beside the ramified patrilineal structure there was
also a system of interconnected matrilineages called wua.

—
E. and P. Beaglehole, op. cit., 21^1,
^Ibid., 2hO.

385

Each wua was composed of several maternal sublines called

keinanga.

The maternal lines traced their descent through

females to certain animal offspring of human beings--a sug­
gestion of totemism.

From a consideration of myths and tradi­

tions of the matrilines, the Beagleholes suggest that the

original division was into two maternal moitiés which later
subdivided.^

At the time of study there were two maternal

moitiés, each subdivided into two lineages, each of the lat­
ter further subdivided into three to five exogamous sublines.

Again there is a definite suggestion of ramification in this
segmented organization.

The suggestion is further stimulated

by the fact that, "Informants tended to visualize the matter
in terms of a tree with spreading branches and limbs.As

further proof of the process of ramification one of the sub­
lines in each lineage bears the same name as the lineage as
a whole.

The process is ramified certainly, and the various

segments maintain their relationship of common descent.

Yet

the matrilines lack one essential aspect of ramification, and
that is seniority of descent and its implication for relative

status.

Succession to the headship of the matrilineages, and

presumably to the sublines, was determined solely by age;
they were headed by the oldest male or female in the group.

Therefore, while the maternal organization seems based on a

ramified model, it is not a full-fledged ramified system.
^Ibid., 224.

the contrary, with the exogamy of the sublines and the empha­

phratries and moleties, as are found in many American Indian
groups. 49
There was also a system of age grades, primarily re­

ferring to males.

The Pukapukans recognized eight different

life periods, not all of which can be included in the age
grade system.*^ In actuality, the important feature of age
groupings was that they provided the young men’s fishing

group and the elders, the tupele.

The young men’s group in­

cluded all those born in a six-month period, and was led by
the person first born in that period.

They formed a deep

sea fishing organization and were replaced by a new group

similarly constituted at periodic (usually six months, longer
if the number in the new group was too small) intervals.

The

old men formed a council (see below), probably the most im­
portant regulatory body on Pukapuka.

The people lived in three villages.

As residence was

generally patrilocal, one was usually a member of one’s
father’s village.

As a matter of fact, a patrilineage was

generally localized in one village.

The village had a coun­

Interpretations
Variations in the forms of social organization and

social stratification on the atolls, and the differences in
these phenomena as they appeared in atolls as opposed to high
islands, are, we submit, intelligible in terms of the selec­
tive factors of atoll environment, and the kinds of adapta­

tion that the historically diverse cultures effected.

The

selective factors encountered on atolls and the methods of
coping with these factors in terms of the organization of in­
terpersonal relations were different from those found in high

islands.

Cultural adaptation and adaptive variations on the

high islands were found to be related to the spatial distri­

bution of resources and the degree of familial productive
specialization.

The essential adaptive problem met by the

forms of social organization in the high islands (the rami­

fied and descent lines systems) was that concerning the

economic distribution of large amounts of surplus production.

In low islands, by contrast, the selective factors were not
so much the spatial distribution of resources and the adapwive problem, the distribution of surplus — —more important was

the problem of keeping a dense population alive in an area of

sporadic or limited surplus production.

389

A glance at the productivity charts of the low
52
islands considered here
will reveal several factors rele­

vant to an understanding of selective pressures and hence
socio-cultural adaptive variation on the atolls.

First of

all, every atoll is characterized by a lack of environmental
diversity and opportunity.

There are hardly any differing

soils (really, hardly any soil), any water, any climatic vari­

ation or other environmental variations on atolls.
a paucity even of biological variation. J

There is

Coupled with this

was a general limitation on the types of exploitation.

Fish­

ing, and the cultivation of but a few crops at best, were

the major subsistence activities.

Another feature was the

often heavy population density.

High density on atolls is

directly attributable to the coconut which takes up little

room, but with fishing, is capable of providing a more or less

continuous supply of food.^4-

Little surplus is derived from

coconuts, however, as the nuts ripen individually, and with
the lack of other foods, must be consumed constantly.

There

was, in fact, little and only sporadic surplus produced on
atolls, a feature related to high density populations living

in areas of restricted exploitation; and the possibility of

starvation through famines resulting from lack of rainfall,
hurricanes and the like, was always imminent.

It may be pos­

tulated that the limitation on surplus production is the cru­

cial selective factor on atolls.

With sporadic and limited surpluses, organizational
features of the atoll societies cannot be understood in the
same terms as on high islands.

ferent.

The adaptive problem is dif­

Social groups are not intelligible as adaptations to

the problems of distributing large surpluses.^

For one

thing, instead of the development of stratified hierarchies

for regulating surplus distribution, one would expect instead

cult to estimate because of insufficient data on land area.
Tokelau seems to fit into a range of 100-200 per square
mile. On one of the Tokelau atolls, Atafu, the density was

391

a trend toward egalitarianism.

When surpluses are relatively

small and deficiencies liable to arise in different localities,
a premium is put on direct or reciprocal types of distribu­

tion, not types that travel up and down hierarchies.

Kinship

organization on an egalitarian basis would be of selective

advantage.

A direct relation between groups of people on an

equal and reciprocal basis allows goods to be moved freely
between any two points on the islet or in a group.

Thus,

associated with low productivity and sporadic surplus, an
element of egalitarianism can be expected in the atoll groups.

Such an element has already been demonstrated in Part I of
this work.

Also of significance here are other implications of
production and distribution in an area of low productivity.

Since every single type of productive endeavor would be
vitally important to the entire society, one would expect
that the organization of personnel would be very closely at­

tuned to the productive demands of the environment and the

exploitative technology.

A highly adaptive social organiza­

tion in an area of limited and sporadic surplus production

would be one in which there was a particular alignment of
people exercising control over each production resource and
engaging in particular productive operations—one particular

organization for each task.

Each type of resource or area of

resources might then be exploited to the fullest and every­

one could share the fruits of the labor.

However, with the

limits on surplus production on the atolls, such organizational

29^

concentrations could not afford to specialize completely on
one product or in one area.

vation.

Specialization would mean star­

But if each and every individual was connected by

some alignment principle—descent, age grade, residence, or
whatever--to each and every type of socio-economic group-­
then everyone might share in the distribution and consumption
of what is produced.

The manifestation of the process of

adaptation in social organization is toward a multiplicity
of social groups, each connected with a given productive ac­

tivity or exploitation of a particular area, and to each of
which every individual belongs.

In the sense of a greater

number of different kinds of groupings, more intricate and
complex societies may be expected on coral atolls than on
high Islands I

Limited exploitative possibilities on the coral

atolls place a premium on the organization of personnel—the
more diversified these organizations become, the better

adapted is the group.

Other things being equal then, coral

atoll organization should show greater intricacy with respect
to social alignment principles than on high islands.^

But things are not always equal in coral atolls and

hence the great diversity in organization on the atolls con­
sidered.

Crops and resource areas vary from group to group.

^The same explanation may hold for the intricate
social organizations found among the Ge tribes of South
America.
I am indebted to Mr. Andrew P. Vayda for many use­
ful Insights into this problem which he has contributed
in the course of long and illuminating discussions on the
matter.

393

Also important are places of origin and types of organiza­
tion which the first immigrants carried with them to their
atoll homes.

The atolls were settled from elsewhere and it

is reasonable to suppose that methods of organization were

carried along with the settlers.

In groups settled from

Polynesian high islands one should expect to find elements
of ramified and/or descent line systems.

Even though such

systems are mechanisms of surplus distribution on a hierarchi­
cal social pattern, the social interconnections they imply

are capable of being used as a basis of production organiza­
tions, or ‘production concentrates‘ as they may be termed.

And connected with the place of origin and the original organ­

ization, a most significant factor in understanding variations
between atolls is the length of time of occupation.

If our

interpretation is correct, the greater the length of time of
occupation of an atoll, the greater should be the multiplicity
of the social groupings and alignments, and the further re­

moved from organizations as found on high islands (other fac­
tors equal, of course).

Again in this connection, the relative

degree of isolation would be a factor.

Frequent and diverse

contacts allow for the introduction of social elements which
can be utilized as bases and principles of group formation

perhaps more readily than such principles could be developed
internally, and almost certainly, of a different kind than in­
ternal developments might produce.

Each atoll will now be briefly considered in terms
of adaptive variation and the history of variation.

394

Tongareva

Tongarevan social organization was typical of the

There was no development of other kinds of

ramified type.

social grouping.

The only discernible aspect of Tongarevan

life which fits our ideal, highly adapted atoll culture is
the relatively small degree or elaboration of social stratifi­
cation on Tongareva.

It is noteworthy that along with

ramage organization, Tongareva retained the dispersed settle­
ment pattern.

Relatively large (for atoll) islets made this

possible, for the total land mass on Tongareva was almost

63 square miles, comparable to that of Easter Island.

Fur­

thermore , the population density was perhaps the lowest of all

atolls, about 32 per square mile.

The amount of surplus pro­

duction may have been somewhat higher than on the average
atoll.

The apparent non-development of diversity of inter­

connected productive concentrates and the ramage organization
seems at least partially intelligible in terms of the rela­
tively late date of settlement of Tongareva by immigrants
from an island having a ramified system.

the origin of the reputed founders of the community finds one

Manihiki-Rakahanga was much like Tongareva in having

a ramified social structure.

The only additional social

grouping on these atolls was village settlement, a grouping
probably related to necessities of cooperative exploitation.

The settlement pattern had once been scattered, but at an
early date, according to the native history, the scattered

Aitutaki in all probability had a ramified sys­

Tokelau

The productive concentrates on Tokelau were the kin­
dred and the village.

The household group, which tended to

be matrilocal and composed of a matrilocally extended family,
rather than a patrilocal one, may have been a partially
formed concentrate.

Whereas residence centered in the vil­

lage, the food plantations, especially coconuts, were found

on the outlying islets—often six to eight miles from the vil­
lage.

The outlying plantations were controlled and exploited

by the kindreds.

Two or three times weekly the adult males

and boys of the kindred visited the plantations to harvest

coconuts.

Some coconuts growing near the village were picked

daily for household use.
Village life is partially an adjustment to fishing
requirements, as the village is located, on each atoll,
where canoe landing and access

sea is most advantageous.

through the reef to the open

Males of the village often fish

communally or in fleets, especially in the hazardous deep sea

expeditions.

Villages provide the framework of this organi­

zation; islet occupation could not.

Wells dug on the village

islet also contributed to stabilizing village organization.

Ontong Java
The joint families, the matrilocal residence groups

and the village organizations were production concentrates.
Every individual belonged to each type of organization.

The

joint family, especially the males thereof, formed fishing

groups and also exploited islets of the atoll for coconuts.
Taro gardens, however, were worked by women and their use
was matrilineally inherited.

Most taro gardens were situ­

ated in the vicinity of the village.

Thus the temporary

matrilocal alignment in the village was associated with taro
production and consumption.

The village itself participated

in periodic harvests of reserve lands.

The village guards

were an adjunct of this organization concerned with super­

vising the use of village reserves.
These distinctions in productive concentration were
not only based on differing types of production but also on

the spatial distribution of resources.

Pukapuka

The patri-ramages, the matrilineages, the system of

age grades and the villages with their guards, were produc­
tive concentrates on Pukapuka.

The bachelor and spinster

houses may have been adjuncts of the age-grade system.

The ramages controlled strips of land of indetermi­
nate width across the islets.

Most of these strips skirted

the excavated taro beds and were used in the production of
coconuts.

It is noteworthy in this respect that the gather­

ing of nuts was usually men's work.

The excavated taro pits were localized on the islets,
and most were controlled by the maternal lineages.

All the

work concerned with the planting and harvesting of taro,
with the exception of excavating the beds, was done by women.

The age grades were primarily a male organization.
The major productive function of the age-grade system was the

deep sea fishing done by the young men's group, the tangata.
All the men born within a given period, usually six months,
were organized into a deep sea fishing group under the leader­

ship of the first man born during that period.

The system of

age grades also was a social and political division of labor,
the tupele or elders being the primary regulatory group on

the atoll.
The villages were organizations which provided the

framework for communal activities, especially fishing.
village controlled a section of reserve lands exploited

A

404

periodically for both cultivated and wild foods.

The village

guards supervised the use of reserve land for the benefit of

all village members.

The village was also the major organi­

zational unit in a political sense, for the councils, although
sometimes composed of descent group heads, governed village
and inter-village activities, rather than kinship group ac­
tivities per se.

The patri-ramages had no councils.

Each of these productive concentrates was also a dis­

tributive unit.

The goods, especially foods, produced were

shared out among the membership and beyond, if possible.
There were patri-ramage distributions, maternal lineage dis­
tributions and village food divisions.

The young men’s group

supplied the old men with fish; any surplus being widely dis­

tributed.
The Beagleholes place the origin of Pukapukan history

about 600 years before 1938, making it one of the (if not the)
79
longest continuously inhabited atolls considered.
The fact
that European contact was not prevalent before 1857, a date

one to three generations later than other atolls, makes the
80
period of internal development perhaps even longer.
About

350 years ago, as the story goes, a catastrophic seismic wave
reduced the population to fifteen families and the resources
to next to nothing.81 With such small population and limited

E. and P. Beaglehole, op. cit., 378.
8°Ibld., 5.
81lbld., 386, et passim.

405

resources the development of intricate organizational align­

ments may have been furthered.

The myth of origin points to

Tonga as the ursprung of Pukapukan settlement.

But the myth

is so couched in esoteric phraseology that it may not at all
be historically accurate.82 Pukapuka has not been isolated.

Native records report migrations and visitations from Tokelau,

migrations from a place called Yayake, invaders from a place
called Tongaleleva, contact with Nassau Island, migrants from

Manihiki and voyages made by Pukapukans to numerous places
both east, and more frequently, west.

The Tokelaus, Samoa,

Tonga and Niue were often reached.

Summary

The atolls have been shown to have organizations often
different from the descent line and ramified systems of high

islands.

Aside from a trend toward egalitarianism, it was

postulated that the development of a number of interlocking
social groups, each dedicated to the exploitation of a parti­
cular resource or resource area, might be expected on atolls

as part of an adjustment to selective pressures limiting sur­
plus production.

By means of such groups, organizations for

particular tasks and the distribution of particular strategic
goods might be brought into being without productive speciali­

zation.

Every member of the community, as a member of each

82Ibid., 375-377.
8^Ibid., 370f.

406

type of group, might share in the products controlled and
worked by some or all of the membership.

The particular prin­

ciples of organization would, of course, depend on the his­

torical tradition of the people.

It was further postulated

that the longer the time of occupation and the more frequent
the external contacts, the greater the complexity of social
structure in these terms.

These hypotheses are, in the main, substantiated by
the evidence.

Although the range of variation in years of

settlement is not great, still the longest occupied atolls

had the most complex organizations.

Tongareva and Manihiki

were settled relatively late and are not very different in

organization from the islands from which they were populated.

The major migration to Tokelau—which was somewhat more diver­
sified than these islands in terms of types of social group­
ings—was also fairly late; but there was an earlier population
also.

Ontong Java and Pukapuka were the earliest settled of

the atolls considered and also the nearest approximations of
our ideal fully adapted type.

Aside from late settlement, the uniquely low popula­

tion density and the large land area which made scattered
settlement pattern permissible, if not desirable, also con­
tributed to the retention of old social forms in Tongareva.

Without outside contacts, Manihiki-Rakahanga*s adjustment to­
ward multiplicity of groupings was probably delayed, although

the late settlement again was a primary factor.

The dependence

upon only two productively distinct activities, fishing and

407

tree culture, undoubtedly set a limit on the amount of dif­
ferent types of groupings in Tokelau.

Ontong Java, and es­

pecially, Pukapuka, begin to show an intricacy of organiza­
tional principles beyond that of the high islands, one that is
associated in each case with different productive and distribu­

tive activities, and in the case of Pukapuka, with relatively
frequent outside contacts.

A correlation between increase in complexity and in­
crease in flexibility of group affiliation may also be noted.
Unfortunately, the data on flexibility were too skimpy for

comparative purposes.

It should be pointed out, however, that

Pukapuka, with the most complicated organization, evidently

showed the greatest frequency in flexibility, mostly in the
form of adoptions.^ It would be expected that when the

organization of personnel becomes more finely adjusted to the
business of exploiting the external world, there would be a

greater necessity to maintain the proportions of people in
various groups--hence a greater flexibility of individual

alignments.
