CHAPTER XII

CONCLUSION: ADAPTIVE VARIATION AND THE
EVOLUTION OF SOCIAL ORGANIZATION
One of the most engaging as well as pressing problems

of anthropological studies is the formation of explanations
of cultural variations.

The scientific aims of the study of

culture are served by reducing the masses of heterogeneous
materials on different cultures to propositions, empirically
verified, which illustrate variable relationships between

differing sets or types of phenomena.

Such a goal requires

the development of concepts of cultural process which seek

to make cultural variations intelligible.

It has been posited

in this work that the concept of adaptive variation is such

a concept, one that can lead to statements of a general na­

ture about the relationship between different aspects of cul­
ture .

A simple truism underlies the concept of adaptive

variation, that exploitation of the energy resources of the
natural world for the purpose of sustaining human life is a

requirement which all cultures must meet.

With this in mind,

attention is focused on the environment in which a society
is situated and the technology by means of which the culture

is articulated with the natural world.

The interaction of a

particular technological system with a given environment is
the basic adaptation of a culture.

It is held that the basic

409

adaptation effected by any culture will be reflected in the
social structure, due to the organizational requirements of

manipulating the technology and distributing life-sustaining
goods.

And, if cultures are in any way cohesive wholes, it

is expectable that corresponding ideological sanctions of
the prevailing social and technological conditions will be

found.

Thus, if variations occur in technology or environ­

ment , or both, concomitant, adaptive variations should be

found in other aspects of culture.
Polynesian cultures were chosen in this study to

illustrate the concept of adaptive variations.

Polynesian

cultures afford an example of a number of historically re­
lated groups found in areas of environmentally diverse condi­

tions .

They are thus members of a single cultural species

which have undergone adaptive differentiation.

Indeed, in

view of the historical process and the range of differences

in Polynesian cultures, 'adaptive radiation’ would not be an
inappropriate term.

Of particular topical interest have been

differences in social stratification in the Polynesian islands.
It is this feature of culture, both in its formal aspects and
the degree to which it exists, which has been related to the

types of adaptations present.
In this chapter the major results of the study will
be reviewed with the aim of obtaining general statements about
the relationship between cultural phenomena considered.

These

statements or propositions will then be used, along with the
data gathered, to formulate some further statements about the

410

evolution of social organization in general.
Adaptive Variations in Social Stratification
in Polynesia

The social stratification systems of Polynesia have
been considered as adaptive variations in two different as­

pects î first from the point of view of the amount of stratifi­
cation in the various islands; and secondly, from the point
of view of the forms of the stratification systems.

It was

posited that, both in degree and in form, social stratifica­

tion could be related to the differing techno-environmental

conditions.

The Amount of Stratification
Based on a lead provided by Kirchhoff’s discussion
of clanship in human societies,fourteen Polynesian cul­

tures were ranked in a classification of the amount of

stratification.

The criteria of amount of stratification

were the degree to which there existed status differences

between groups of individuals not based on age, sex or per­
sonal attributes, and the degree to which these status

levels played differential roles and exercised differential
privileges in the economic, social and ceremonial realms of
culture.

The economic differences afforded a clue to the

relationship between social stratification and the tech­
nological adaptation to environment.

^Kirchhoff ms.

In particular it was

411

found that a distinction existed in many of the societies
between those who only produced need serving goods, and those

who regulated the distribution of such items.

And this dis­

tinction often corresponded with the social difference, chief
and non-chief,

The predominant distribution process was that

called by Professor Polanyi, "the redistributive form of

economic integration,"

Strategic goods not consumed by the

producers were often accumulated by chiefs and periodically

redistributed among the producers of the goods, and as well
among specialized and communal laborers.

Based on this dif­

ference in distribution functions, it was advanced that the

amount of social stratification is related to the cultural

adaptation in terms of productivity of the exploitative ac­
tivities,

Considering productivity to mean the ability of

the wielders of the technology to produce strategic goods

beyond their consumption needs, the co-adaptive character of
stratification became clear.

The greater the productivity

the greater the differentiation between distributors (chiefs)

and producers (non-chiefs) in other aspects of culture.
The presence of a redistributive system was also of
great value in solving the problem of measuring the produc­

tivity of the society for the purpose of comparison with the
gradient of the amount of stratification.

Positing that in

any given group, other factors held constant, the greater the
surplus producable by the producers the greater would be the

range of distribution, productivity was measured by consider­
ing the number of people embraced in the largest redistributive

412

network of food and the frequency^ that this overall network
was utilized.

As a check on productivity derived by this

measure, the Polynesian groups were also examined with re­
spect to the amount of non-food producing specialists they
contained and the particular types of technology and environ­
ment present.

In the former case, it was assumed that the

greater the productivity the greater the number of people who

might be divorced from food production.

The particular tech­

nological-environmental situations were rated according to

other assumptions : that other things constant, agriculture
is more productive than hunting and gathering; specialized
techniques of domestication as irrigation agriculture, selec­
tion of varieties and the like, are more productive than the

lack of specialized techniques ; the greater the diversity of

environmental opportunities the more likely that productivity

be greater, etc.
Considering the lack of mathematical precision in
the criteria of stratification and certain gaps in the data,

the gradients of stratification and productivity show a re­
markable correspondence.

On the basis of the amount of

stratification, the cultures were divided among four cate­

gories from more stratified to more egalitarian:

Tahiti, Tonga, Samoa;
Uvea;

Ila. Mangareva, Mangala, Easter Island,

lib. Futuna, Tikopia, Marquesas;

Tokelau, Pukapuka.

I. Hawaii,

III. Ontong Java,

These groupings were found to correspond

to productivity differences, and thus the Polynesian materials

413

indicate that a high probability may be assigned to the
first major generalization of this work:
(1) Other factors constant, the greater the produc­

tivity, the greater the amount of stratification.
It should be stressed that the proviso, other factors

constant, is not to be taken lightly.

The intimate relation­

ship between productivity and stratification is intelligible
in Polynesia due to the fact that a redistributive economic

system prevailed in these islands.

The redistributive system

may be a necessary condition for the generalization.

Further­

more, it is possible that in particular cultures alternative

means may be found to cope with the problems of surplus pro­
duct ion--me ans that may or may not lead to a high degree of

social stratification.

Therefore, cross-cultural comparisons

of productivity and stratification may not lead to as signi­

ficant results (in a statistical sense) as were obtained here.
Needless to say, this does not necessarily invalidate the
general proposition; just as a floating feather does not in­

validate the law of gravity.
The amount of stratification was also considered in

light of the historical differences between western and east­

ern Polynesia.

Although there is abundant evidence of cul­

tural distinctions between western and eastern Polynesia due

to historical exclusiveness of these areas, a greater degree

of stratification was found not to be a part of either the
eastern or the western Polynesian tradition.

This does not

exclude the possibility that in particular islands the amount

414

of stratification in some aspects of culture was not on the

same level of stratification as in other aspects.

For ex­

ample, ceremonial santification of chieftainship in Pukapuka

and Tokelau was more elaborate than the economic and social

role of the chief warranted.

This can be interpreted as a

survival of practices of stratification in a relatively non­
adap tive domain of culture, ideology, as these islands were

settled from high islands where stratification was probably
greater.
The Form of Stratification

The systems of social organization and stratifica­
tion in Polynesia can be divided into three types : ramage

organization and descent line organization on high islands,
and the looser category, atoll organization.

It was posited

that these forms can be understood as adaptive variations.
The ramage organization was found in Hawaii, the

Societies, Tonga, New Zealand, Marquesas, Tikopia, Easter
Island, Mangareva and Mangala.

alogical system.

The ramified system is a gene­

All the members of the society can be

placed on one or a few genealogical trees.

The rank of any

individual is governed by his relative distance from the main
line of descent in the group, the high chief being the direct

descendant of the deified founder of the community.

Primo-

genitural succession to positions of authority carries the
implication of seniority which makes such a ranking system
possible.

Economic and social processes, as distribution and

415

the regulation of Interpersonal affairs are carried out along
the lines of genealogy.
The descent line system was found on Samoa, Futuna

and Uvea.

In contrast to the ramified systems, descent line

societies are composed of a number of discrete lineages,
descent lines.

Descent lines hold one or more titles on the

territorial councils which govern the group.

Primogeniture

is not the succession rule ; in fact there is no definite rule
of succession to the title held by the fami? es.

plied by seniority of birth is absent or not

Rank im­

gnifleant ;

rather, rank is dependent on the traditional, mythological
standing of the family title.

It was posited that these two systems, ramified and
descent line, are alternative solutions to the problem of

distributing surplus production, solutions related to the par­
ticular type of basic adaptation of the culture.

It was

reasoned that other things held constant, a ramified system

would tend to develop where there was familial specialization
of production of surplus strategic resources.

The kinship

connection between households in the ramified system may be
seen as a framework by which an equitable distribution of the

different productions might be obtained, as the kin ties could
be utilized as a basis either of reciprocal exchanges, or of

redistributive, hierarchial distributions.

It was deduced,

therefore, that ramified systems would tend to develop where
the spatial distribution of rich resource zones in the en­

vironment was one too scattered to be exploited by a single

416

household, or where the range of crops was so large as to
preclude effective exploitation of them by a single house­

hold, or both.

On the contrary, descent line systems should

be found where the spatial distribution of rich resource zones
is clustered in a small area, or where the range of crops is
small, or both.

Under the latter conditions, each family

would produce the same strategic goods as every other and the
kinship connection between households which have split off

from each other would tend to dissolve.

Economic redistri­

butions as well as social, political and religious functions

would therefore be carried out within a localized cluster of

kin groups.

As a corollary to the adaptational feature of re­

source distribution, one would expect to find ramage systems

associated with scattered settlement patterns, whereas descent

line systems would probably be associated with nucleated or

village settlements.
The deductions were borne out by the available mater­

ials.

In ten of twelve cases a nearly perfect correlation was

obtained; ramage systems were associated with scattered dis­
tributions of rich exploitative zones, familial specializa­

tion of production and usually, scattered settlement; descent
line systems were associated with clustered exploitative
zones, familial self-efficiency in production and nucleated

settlements.

Easter Island, with ramage organization, had

some familial specialization even though exploitation zones
were by and large restricted to the coastal area.

A previous

history of ramage organization may account for the presence of

417

the system in Easter Island despite the relatively non-

selective environmental conditions.

For Uvea, with a descent

line system, yet seemingly ramified adaptational features, no
explanation is forthcoming at the moment.

However, the total

weight of the evidence supports the generalization that:

(2) Other factors constant, the form of social organiza­
tion and stratification in areas of relatively high
productivity corresponds to the spatial distribution

of resource zones.

The forms of organization on the coral atolls cannot
be understood in the same terms as on high islands.

Familial

specialization of production is prohibited by small surpluses,

and the consequent limitations on exchanges of strategic goods.
Every type of resource is of vital importance to the continued

existence of the society.

It was postulated that effective

exploitation of atolls can best be made, therefore, if there
is separate organization of people for exploiting each resource

available.

Without the possibility of producing large sur­

pluses and hence of specialization, every person would have to
be a member of each type of grouping.

The result would be a

particularly complex system of social groupings with each per­

son, utilizing different types of alignment principles, be­
longing to each type of organization.

It was reasoned that if

this postulate was valid, the longer the atoll was occupied
or the greater the amount of outside influences in the form

of diffusions of social organizational features, or both, the

more complex the organization.

The particular form of

418

stratification organization would depend on the previous
history of the atoll.

The deductions were borne out by the evidence•
Pukapuka, an island in frequent contact with other Poly­

nesian areas, and Ontong Java were occupied for a longer
time and had more complex, cross-cutting organizations than
the other atolls considered, Tongareva, Tokelau and Man-

hiki-Rakahanga.

Pukapuka, for example, had both matrilineal

and patrilineal groupings, age-grades, village and atoll
organization.

The evidence thus supports the conclusion that:

(3) Other factors constant, long occupation of a cul­
ture with relatively low productivity in a stable
environment will result in a multiplicity of cross­

cutting social groupings.

Thus the evidence from Polynesia supports a number
of broad generalizations (1-3)»

In the remainder of this

chapter, an attempt will be made to utilize these generaliza­
tions , as well as some of the materials on which they are

based, in order to offer an interpretation of the evolution
of social organization and social stratification in culture

as a whole,

The Evolution of Social Organization
in utilizing the concept of adaptive variation, a
number of variable relationships between social organization
and the technological adaptation of culture to environment in

aboriginal Polynesia were revealed.

In this section we propose

419

to further develop the conclusions of this study through an
interpretation of the evolution of social organization in
culture as a whole.

It is always possible that what is true

for Polynesia is not true for other areas.

This is es­

pecially significant here inasmuch as Polynesian cultures

are intimately historically related and differences between
them, therefore, may often be understood as adaptive variations.

Consequently the scheme which is advanced is only tentative

and is offered merely as a suggestion and stimulant for fur­
ther research, rather than as a definitive interpretation of

cultural evolution.
The basic premise of our scheme is that the history

of culture as a whole is a record of man’s progressive con­
trol over nature.

Starting in the dim past of one million or

so years ago, archaeological materials reveal continual
achievements--sometimes gradual, sometimes abrupt--in the

harnessing of energy from the external world and the utiliza­
tion of that energy to serve the needs of man.2

It is one

of the tasks of the ethnologist to indicate, as far as pos­
sible, what concomitant developments have taken place in the

economic, social and religious aspects of culture, concomitants

often only barely perceptible in the archaeological record.
Based primarily upon the data and conclusions of this study,
the following scheme of evolution is presented.

(Again it

may be mentioned that no teleologies are implied, and that
Childe, 1948a; 1948b; White, 1943; 1949, Chapter 3.

420

changes in technology and environment throughout history do
not necessarily imply changes in economy, social organiza­
tion and social stratification in every instance.)

Stage 1. Reciprocal Economics; Egalitarian Kin Societies
A concomitant variation of progressive increase in
productivity in culture is progressive increase in stratifi­

cation (1).3

When productivity increases, differentiation

between individuals and between groups of individuals in

economic, social and ideological roles and privileges in­
crease.

As productivity was low in the earliest periods of

human history, it can be inferred that societies were egali­

tarian.

Either there were no statuses carrying differential

privileges, or the major qualifications for status were age,

sex or personal attributes.

Egalitarian principles probably

predominated in the regulation of interpersonal affairs.
Collective kin group retaliation, group ridicule and super­
natural sanctions were the primary means for punishing wrong­

doers.

Any status differences that might exist would carry

little weight in differential applications of sanctions.

The

gradient toward egalitarianism in the Polynesian atolls, ex­
tended a bit farther, might illustrate this organization.

Landtman has outlined a number of other egalitarian societies
including the Kiwai Paupans, Australian aborigines, Bushmen,
Hottentot, Andaman Islanders, Vedda, Puegians and certain

^Numbers in parentheses refer to the above numbered
major generalizations.

421

Asiatic and New World groupsHerskovits also describes a

number of societies of the same type, pointing out that egali­
tarianism is found in cultures with small surpluses."’

More can be said about culture at this stage.

The

Polynesian materials indicate that the connections between

productivity and stratification lie primarily in the redis­

tributive functions of the chiefs.

It may also be pointed out

that in the most egalitarian of the Polynesian societies, as

Ontong Java, there were a great deal of non-intermediated

reciprocal exchanges of goods between kin groups, especially
affinal exchanges.

A relatively small amount of intermedi­

ated, redistributive activities involving a number of house­
holds may be inferred for the earliest stage of culture, along

with the small amount of stratification.

The economies at

this stage can be designated as "reciprocal,' again after
6
Polanyi,
Although no further extensive documentation can
be made here, it is suggested that investigation of the econo­
mies of such groups as are mentioned by Landtman and Hersko­

vits, and other societies of the egalitarian type, will reveal

a predominance of reciprocal methods of exchange between fam­

ily groups.
As to form or forms of social organization in the

earliest phases of culture, little can be said directly from
^Landtman, 193$, Chap. 1,

^Herskovits, 1940, 359 f.
^Polanyi, 1944; 1953.

4^2

the Polynesian data.

The information from the atolls leads

to the inference that those cultures in long habitation in

stable environments probably had complex, cross-cutting
types of social groupings (3).

Another inference that can be

made is that kinship was everywhere the basis of social organ­

ization and interpersonal relations.

Bands, as described by

Steward for cultures of very low productivity might well be
y
expected.
KlrchhofT1s "equalltarlan" clan, as typified by

Iroquois gens, may also have been present, probably under con­
ditions of higher productivity than bands.
Stage II. Redistributive Economies: Stratified Kin Societies

The Polynesian cultures carry the evolution of social
organization from the transition from the egalitarian society

through the full-blown stratified kin society.

The emergence

of stratification in this intermediate period is related to

Increasing productivity (1).

A distinction develops between

chiefs who regulate redistributions of goods to large num­
bers of producers (including specialists), and non-chlefs who

primarily work on production.

As productivity increases, the

authority of chiefs extends into the social domain and into
the religious.

Status differences, now become significant

in the application of sanctions to wrongdoers.

Physical

force may be wielded by members of the chiefly group against

’commoners1 without fear of reprisal.

^Steward, 1936.

The preeminence of

423

chiefs is celebrated in divine descent, and the priesthood

becomes intimately affiliated with the familial cult of para­
mount chiefs.
Some remarks should be made here by way of differenti­

ating this stage of social stratification from the next or
state stage : there is no permanent accumulation of wealth,
nor exclusive control over the means or resources of produc­

tion by any segment of the society; nor is there a monopoly
of coercive authority wielded by any segment; even the economic

role of the chief is implicit in kinship relations, not in­

discriminately enforced by governmental agents.

The Polynesian data indicate that societies are still

primarily organized on a kinship basis at this stage.

The

social organization contains some principle of ranking as
seniority of descent, or mythological, hierarchial standing
of a family title.

The particular forms of organization are

probably related to the spatial distribution of highly pro­

ductive resource zones (3).

The ramified and descent line

systems may have existed.

Meyer Fortes has outlined these

same two types in stratified African societies: the descent

line system in Central Africa among the Bemba, Tonga had Lozi;

and the ramage type (called, "segmentary societies") among
such groups as the Tiv, Gusli, Cyrenacian Beduin, and others,®

Other examples of the ramage type may be found elsewhere in

o
Meyer Fortes, "The Structure of Unilineal Descent
Groups,” American Anthropologist, 55, 17-41.

424

the ethnographic literature, as among the Northwest Coast

Indians and pastoralists of Inner Asia,
Stage III, The State

The latest stage in the evolution of social organi­
zation is the state.

The state is distinguishable from the

stratified kin society in a number of respects.

States are

not organized on a kin basis; economic and political processes
are carried out without regard to kinship along lines of ter­
ritorial administrative units.

True socio-economic classes

exist, the ruling group holding a large part of the wealth
and a monopoly of power.

Law emanating from the ruling group

appears, with sanctions enforced coercively by the state upon

all citizens—theoretically without regard to status or kin­
ship affiliations.

The processes of government are supported

by contributions of wealth and labor from the citizens, con­

tributions which are specified in amount and as regards time
of payment by law.

Many of the political and economic processes in the

states are development from practices found in highly strati­
fied kin societies as Hawaii, for example.

Law enforcement is

in many respects an extension of the differential ability be­
tween chiefs and non-chiefs to exercise force in punishing

those who infringe their rights.

The goods passed up to the

Hawaiian chiefs for redistributions at feasts, to compensate

specialists and communal laborers, become taxes to support
state enterprises.

Indeed, the calls for labor issued to

4^5

inferior relatives by Hawaiian chiefs become corvees in the

state, used for just such enterprises.

Although self regu­

lating markets have become the predominant economic institu­

tions of many latter-day states, it is interesting that the
major form of distribution in some, if not all, of the ar­
chaic states was state- or clerically-controlled redistribu­
tion.

(The clearest examples come from the Inca and the

temple economy of early Mesopotamia.)

In fact, the entire

centralized, monolithic structures of such early ’Oriental’

states as the Peruvian, the Egyptian and the Chinese, re­

semble the socio-political centralization of the ramified
systems of Polynesia.

In the state one clearly sees the

fruition of processes of stratification that began with the

stratified kin society.

426

,

APPENDIX

COMMENTS, BASED ON POLYNESIAN MATERIALS,
ON SOME THEORIES OF THE GENESIS OF
SOCIAL STRATIFICATION

In Part One of this work it has been shown that dif­
ferences in the amount of stratification in Polynesian soci­

eties are adaptive variations related to productivity in the
various islands.

The relation of productivity to stratifi­

cation was made intelligible in terms of the role of the

chiefs as regulators of redistributions of surplus foods.
The greater the productivity and surplus, the greater the role

of the chief in distribution; hence, the greater the tendency
for the chief to exert authority in other realms of culture.

The general relationship between productivity and stratifica­

tion allows some inferences about the processes of adaptation
in Polynesian cultures, although nothing specific about the

distinct problem of the processes of the origin of stratifi­
cation.

However, the relationship does allow some comment on

extant theories of the origin of stratification in human soci­
eties.

For if differences in productivity are related to dif­

ferences in stratification, a theory of the genesis of social
stratification must include, as a necessary condition, an in­

crease of some sort in productivity.

Furthermore, inasmuch

as social stratification is related—not only in Polynesia,

427

but, according to Malinowski

1

and Thurnwald,

2

In many other

areas--to differential roles in the distribution of strategic
goods, a theory of the origin of stratification should make

some reference to the differentiation of statuses in the dis­
tributive system.
It behooves us then to turn to two outstanding
hypotheses of the genesis of stratification, the theories

of K. A. Wittfogel concerning the ’Oriental Society’ and
Gumplowicz’s conquest theory, and to comment on these hypothe­

ses in light of the data and generalizations embodied in our

work on stratification in Polynesia.
The Theory of the Oriental Society

It is reasonable to suppose that, just as differen­
tial power and privilege accrue to the regulator of distribu­

tion vis a vis the producers, so also would the regulator or
director of communal enterprises tend to exert authority be­
yond the productive realm itself.

It may be suggested that

the greater the size of the labor force, the complexity of

the tasks and the frequency of such production, the greater
would be the tendency for the supervisor of communal produc­

tion to extend his authority into the regulation of allied

economic processes, into social affairs and the greater the
sanctification of this authority in ideology.

Kalinowski, 1937, 232-233.
^Thurnwald, 1932, 107, 180-181.

The origin, then

428

of status differentiation may have been in technological-

environmental conditions which required the organization of
a large proportion of the society’s manpower for strategic

tasks, i.e., for tasks, as food production, necessary for the

material support of the human population.

This line of thought has been applied, in a more
specialized sense by Professor K. A. Wittfogel in his ana3
lysis of the "Oriental Society."
Wittfogel is primarily con­
cerned with the relationship of organized irrigation agricul­
ture on a grand scale to the development of absolute forms of

government.

It is Wittfogel’s hypothesis that strong, cen­

tralized authority develops when it is found necessary to
tame water for irrigation agriculture on a large scale, that

is, beyond the use of local wells and reservoirs.

Such a task,

since it involves the organization of huge labor forces, pro­
vides the stimulus for the development of centralized forms
of authority which function, through a bureaucracy, to mobi­
lize the manpower, to control the water and thus to control

the lives of the peasant producers.^

Although Wittfogel’s

thesis is especially concerned with state organizations and

thus goes well beyond the kinship societies of Polynesia, it
^Wittfogel, 1931; K. Wittfogel, "Die Theorie der

Orlentalischen Gesellschaft," Zeitschrift für Sozialforschung,
U, 90-122, 1938.
---------------------------------------- &

^See also Childe, 1948b; Steward, 1949; wherein the
bearing of irrigation on social stratification is seen in a
similar light. The theory has a long history and has gained
widespread currency.

429

1s pregnant with implications for the material we are con­
sidering.

First of all, Wittfogel’ s hypothesis has the gen­

eral implication just outlined, namely, that communal labor
(i.e., those activities in which material goods are produced

by a cooperating labor force composed of members of more than

one consumption unit) provides the stimulus for the develop­
ment of stratification inasmuch as the director of the enter­

prise would tend to exert great authority not only over the

communal production, but over other aspects of the lives of
the producers.

More specifically, Wittfogel points to large-

scale irrigation as a particular type of technology which
would exercise strong demands for centralized leadership, thus

tending to differentiate the socio-political system.

Finally,

this type of irrigation, once existing, is a potent mechanism
by which power differentials would be reinforced through con­
trol of the vital water supply by the ruling group.

Since com­

munal labor varies in scope and frequency from island to island
in Polynesia, and since the extent of irrigation also varies

from island to island, these implications of Wittfogel’s theory

demand serious attention.
But Wittfogel’s hypothesis cannot be tested automati­
cally with the Polynesian materials ; it requires scrutiny in

light of what has already been documented in previous chap­
ters.

It cannot be assumed, for example, that the social

stratification schemes of Polynesian cultures necessarily ori­

ginated in Polynesia.

More serious is the fact that it has

been shown that increasing stratification is a concomitant

4cO

variation of increasing productivity, the differentiation of

roles and privileges in cultural activities being related to
the differentiation of roles occurring in the process of the

distribution of surpluses.

Of crucial import vis a vis the

communal labor thesis is the fact that it is precisely dif­
ferences in the realm of communal expenditures of labor

which may be responsible for differences in productivity or
surplus production.

Irrigation as a special technique of

domestication is far superior (in certain environments) to

dry farming in that it not only brings water to the plants,
but restores the mineral contents of the soil--exactly what
dry farming cannot do.

And, as regards communal labor in

general, it is stating a truism to point out that people work­
ing in organized groups, as in fishing with large nets, pro­

duce more per capita than the efforts of individuals working
as individuals.

A logical separation of the mode of produc­

tion and productivity may be possible, but it is difficult to
analytically distinguish their consequences.

Increasing scope

and complexity of strategic communal enterprises implies in­

creasing productivity, which in turn allows for greater popu­

lation and hence more communal labor.

The process may con­

tinue until the technological, environmental, economic, social
and ideological limits are reached.
Wittfogel does not dwell upon the productivity impli­

cations of advanced irrigation agriculture.

He concentrates

instead on the organizational requirements of water control.

The question arises as to whether social stratification develops

431

in response to the organizational aspects of communal labor
in general and irrigation agriculture in particular, or in

response to increasing productivity which results in central­
ized distribution of surplus production--or both.

It may be

that the question cannot be conclusively answered from the
Polynesian materials.

However, certain points about the re­

lationship between communal labor, productivity and amount
of stratification can be revealed by considering data from

various Polynesian cultures.

From this some light may be shed

on the Wittfogel hypothesis.

First of all, a general correspondence may be found
between the productivity of the society and the scope and fre­

quency of communal labor.

As it has been demonstrated that

productivity is correlated with the amount of stratification,
it follows that communal labor and stratification correspond.
In Hawaii, where productivity was high, a marked development

of the use of organized labor forces occurred in connection
with the construction and maintenance of the remarkable (up

to three miles long) irrigation ditches and terracesThe
ability of the Hawaiian chiefs to mobilize large labor forces
may be seen in Ellis’ description of cutting and hauling san­

dalwood in one Hawaiian district in which between 2,000 and

3,000 men were involved.In Tonga, another group with high
^See Bennett, 1931> passim; Handy, 1940; Perry,1913,94*

^Ellis, 1825, 215» The sandalwood indicates influ­
ence from the post-contact sandalwood trade, but the organi­
zation was built on aboriginal models. For other examples of
large-scale production in Hawaii, see Handy, op. cit., 146-47:
Ellis, 1826, 291-292.

432

productivity a large amount of labor was communally expended
in fishing, construction of chiefs* tombs, of fortresses and

the like.
Frequent, although smaller«scale communal actlvl8
q
ties are indicated for Samoa and Mangareva.
Less frequent

and much smaller«scale communal endeavors occurred in the

Marquesas, Futuna and Tlkopia.^

On the atolls, with the low­

est productivity, complex and large-scale community enter­

prises were minimal.
A second point which may be noted is that no division per­

tains , in Polynesian societies, between the initiators of com­
munal production and the regulators of surplus redistribution-TO
it is usually in both cases the same chief or group of chiefs.
Thus it is not possible to analytically define the consequences
for social stratification of control over communal labor as dis­

tinct from control of redistribution.

Furthermore, these two

forms of control are intimately related and serve to influ­

ence each other.

Regulation of redistribution allows the

7

Cf. Gifford, 1929, passim; W.C.McKern, Archaeology

433

chief to accumulate goods in order to support communal pro­

duction, and regulation of strategic communal production im­

plies the right to distribute the products of the endeavor.

Thirdly, although developed irrigation on Hawaii cor­

relates with the high degree of stratification there, largescale irrigation is not a necessary concomitant of a great

amount of stratification.

For example, in Tongatabu, where

the amount of stratification approached that of Hawaii, there
was no irrigation, as the island was of the raised coral type

and lacked permanent streams.

The situation in Tonga, which is paralleled by Easter
Island, provides a fourth interesting point with regard to

the relationship between stratification and the organization

of communal production.

In both Tonga and Easter Island,

strategic communal production was inhibited by environmental
factors—by the absence of streams in Tonga, precluding irri­

gation, and by the absence of streams, reefs and forests in

Easter Island, precluding irrigation, communal fishing and co­

operative bush clearing.

In both cases, communal production

occurred, but it was largely channelized into non-strategic

realms, the chiefly tombs in Tonga and the famous Easter Island
stone heads.

The fact that masses of manpower were devoted to

these tasks--without analogies provided by organizations of
labor for subsistence pro duc t ion--demons trates that in a

very real sense communal labor is related to and reinforces
stratification.

But it may also imply that stratification,

in particular instances,can precede communal labor.

In Tonga

434

and Easter Island, the organizational framework for com­
munal production may have originated in the distinctions

arising out of differential roles in distribution and later
been used in non-strategic "public enterprise" as a rein­

forcement of the stratification.

Wittfogel’s hypothesis and its implication that

stratification originates in the techno-environmental and
organizational necessities of large-scale communal production

(specifically in taming water) can neither be proven nor disproven by Polynesian data.

Stratification and the scope of

communal labor go hand-in-hand--but so do communal labor and
productivity.

The correspondence between the regulator of

distribution and the regulator of communal production in Poly­

nesia prohibits analysis of the potency of communal labor, as
opposed to productivity, as the originating factor of strati­

fication.

Nor is there clear indication that the presence of

developed irrigation implies of itself extraordinary develop­

ment of stratification; in Tonga a highly elaborate stratifica­

tion system occurred where irrigation was impossible.

Finally,

the fact that large-scale communal labor occurred in Tonga

and Easter Island in what can be called an esoteric realm of

culture rather than a strategic is revealing.

It was not

techno-environmental necessity which was served by communal

labor in these two islands, but socio-political necessity.
This fact opens the possibility that the origin of stratifica­

tion is not to be sought in the organizational features of

large-scale mass enterprise (albeit these features reinforce

435

stratification), but rather in the differentiation of roles

in distributive processes.

In sum, and with regard to cul­

ture as a whole, it is possible that communal labor may only

have become significant as a factor generating social strati­
fication after some stratification was already in existence.
Conquest Warfare

Warfare has a number of implications relevant to an
understanding of social stratification in terms of adaptive
variation.

Problems of offense and defense are often as

vital to the survival of a society as subsistence activities.
One of the most important mechanisms of adaptation in culture

may very well be the elimination of less advantageous varie­
ties through warfare.

As the waging of war implies effective

organizations of manpower and hence the wielding of authority,
the frequency of warfare, its scope and the complexity of its

organizational features are subjects of investigation which
might well prove fruitful to the student o@ social stratifica­
tion.
If one were to analyze the relationship of the organ­

izational requirements of warfare to social stratification ir­
respective of other factors, much the same problem would arise

as confronted us when analyzing the relationship of communal

labor to stratification irrespective of productivity implica­
tions.

For the scale of warfare is undoubtedly related to pro­

ductivity.

The greater the surplus, the greater the available

manpower; hence, the more complex the armies and the bloodier
the struggle.

436

But the problem of the relationship of warfare to

social stratification has another facet, one that has received
more attention in the theoretical literature than the organi­
zational aspects of warfare.

We refer, of course, to the con13
quest of the origin of social classes or of the state.
The

names of Gumplowicz and Oppenheimer are most closely associa­
ted with the conquest theory.^ In the anthropological liter­
ature this theory has been used by Thurnwald and Landtman,

among others.^

Gumplowicz saw the formation of stratified

societies as the outcome of the natural tendencies of hetero­
geneous groups to conquer and exploit each other.

Oppen­

heimer defines the state as a polity of inferior and superior

groups, and sees it to be the result of the conquest of a less
warlike by a more warlike group, the latter setting themselves

up as ruling nobility.

These theories are interesting in light

of the Polynesian materials in two respects.

First, in view

of the two-strata theory of migrations to Polynesia found in
the literature, and secondly, in view of the fact that in some

13

in the preceding and following chapters to document the fact
that an organization of superior and inferior groups can ex­
ist in a society organized on a kinship basis, one that in
our opinion can be differentiated from civil organizations
based on non-personal relationships, i.e., what we would call
states.

societies in our sample, conquest and domination was a fre­

quent aftermath of war, while in others such was not the case.
Various hypotheses have been formulated by those in­

terested in Polynesian history which attempt to depict speci­
fic Polynesian cultures as a fusion of two or more distinct
migrational strains.^

These two (and more) strata theories

have been sharply criticized by Buck and Biddington for
glibly postulating migrations ad hoc to explain cultural dif­

ferences and similarities while ignoring processes of internal

cultural development.

There is no necessity for us to choose

sides in this controversy, as most of these theories do not
directly attempt to explain the origin of stratification as

having arisen through dual migration.

Indeed, the kinship

bonds which usually existed between "rulers" and "ruled" in
Polynesia (see Chapters VIII and IV), would argue against

that thesis.

But it is worthwhile to review the two-strata

theory as it pertains to Tahiti, for here it is specifically

posited that stratification arose out of the conquest of one

distinct people and culture by another distinct people and cul­

Tahitj^and Move a represent all I have been able to
The evidence for conquest consists primarily of a chant:

Which tells of the separation of Tahiti (as a fish)
from Raiatea; the severing of the sinews of the
fish; the absence of a chiefly family, a Hui Arii,
from Tahiti at this time when it had only warrior
lords and so was called Tahiti-Manahune; and the
arrival of the gods on Tahiti which frightened the
people.*
21
20
19
Handy, 1930; Cf, W. E. Mtthlmann, "Staatsbuildung
in Polynésien," Zeit. f. Ethnologie, 65> 38O-389, 1933.
19
Luomala, op. cit.
20
Ibid., 61-62.

21Ibid., 62.

439

It is upon this chant that, literally, an empire is built.

Luomala does not altogether reject the invasion theory.

But she finds no evidence that the manahune were an aboriginal
stock—or we^e in any way different from the arii, except in

status, at the time of European contact.

Nor does there ap­

pear to be any evidence that the Tahitians were unstratified

before the supposed invasion,

On the contrary, the legend on

which the conquest theory is based specifically states that
the Tahitians had "warrior lords," albeit, "only warrior

lords."

There is thus no satisfactory evidence that stratifi­

cation originated through conquest in the Society Islands-or for that matter, anywhere else in Polynesia.
All of this is not to say that conquest is not a
means by which one group of people rises to social and poli­
tical prominence over another.

Leadership of a Hawaiian

island (the social unit) could pass to a group victorious in
22
warfare with the incumbent ramage.
Secular leadership over
23
all Mangala and over Easter Island were spoils of war.
Similar examples may be noted with respect to Samoa, Tonga,

Mangareva and, as indicated, Tahiti.

It is interesting and

significant that conquest warfare was a mechanism by which
stratification might be effected in these highly stratified

(Groups I and Ila) societies.

We find little evidence that

such occurred in the less stratified (Groups lib and III)
22

Emory, 1933; Malo, 1950; Cf. Chapter VIII.

Z^Buck, 1938a; Metraux, 1940; Cf. Chapter VIII.

440

Polynesian societies.

24

Thus, some truth appears in the conquest theory as it

applies to Polynesia.

Stratification is more pronounced where

conquest is a mechanism for achieving prominence in the strati­
fication system.

This relationship may be further connected

with differences in productivity in that the ability to ex­
ploit victory may be intimately related to the size of the
fighting forces.

But whatever the case, in none of the ex­

amples cited is there any evidence that the conquered groups
were not already stratified themselves.

As far back as there

is any record, conquered groups in the islands mentioned had
the same organization as those which had achieved leadership
through war.

Conquest of one group by another does not appear

to have been the origin of stratification in Polynesia.

24

There is mention of the political conquest of
Atafu and Nukunono atolls by the Fakaofu atoll group in
Tokelau (MacGregor, 1937> 19f). This may have been a re­
sult of European influence as the invasion was rather late.
More significant, these invasions really backfired, as the
populations of the defeated atolls soon migrated. (Ibid.,
20-21.)
